The Home Front: House of the Week / `Gold Coast' and Garden
The Home Front:
WEEKEND JOURNAL
The Home Front: House of the Week / `Gold Coast' and Garden
By Troy McMullen
360 words
18 February 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
W10
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
What: Six bedrooms, 6 1/2 baths in 9,000 square feet
Where: Chicago's "Gold Coast," north of downtown, about two blocks from Lake Michigan and four blocks
south of Lincoln Park
Amenities: Three-story red brick Georgian Revival. High arched windows overlooking a private walled-in
garden, second floor home/office complex with kitchen and bath. Two-car garage, eight fireplaces, powder
room, butler's pantry and eat-in kitchen.
Asking Price: $13.5 million
Opening Bid*: $11.5 million
Listing Agents: Thomas Gorman and Heather Bilandic. (312) 981-2395
Due Diligence: This home stands on tree-lined Astor Street, well-known for its 19th-century mansions. It was
built in 1912 by architect Arthur Heun, a member of the Prairie School of design pioneered by Frank Lloyd
Wright. The home was owned for the last 25 years by Jack Gray, chairman emeritus of apparel maker
Hartmarx Corp., and his late wife Anne, an interior designer. (Mrs. Gray designed interiors for several area
landmarks including the Tremont Hotel.) A two-story wing consisting of a coach house and a studio was
added in the late 1970s. The property underwent extensive renovations in the 1980s, plumbing and electrical
systems updated. The living room has a wood-burning fireplace and high arched windows overlooking Astor
Street and a private garden. Tall French doors lead to the foyer and library. The master bedroom suite spans
the entire front of the house facing Astor Street with additional views of the garden. The suite also has a
wood-burning fireplace, two marble bathrooms, a walk-in closet and adjacent dressing room with its own
fireplace and bay area overlooking the garden.
---
*The opening bid is Weekend Journal's estimate of a reasonable starting point for negotiations to buy the
property, based on past sales, prevailing market conditions and interviews with local real-estate experts.
---

wants to appeal his assessment, in House Talk; plus, see more House of the Week photos.

Dow Industrials Fall 80 Points, Lose '05 Gains --- After Testing a 3 1/2-Year High, Gains Vanish on Comments From Bush About Middle East
Thursday's Markets
Dow Industrials Fall 80 Points, Lose '05 Gains --- After Testing a 3 1/2-Year High, Gains Vanish on
Comments From Bush About Middle East
By E.S. Browning
457 words
18 February 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
THE DOW JONES Industrial Average pushed up near a 3 1/2-year high and then ran out of steam, falling so
hard that it gave up the small gain it had been showing for 2005.
The industrials finished down 80.62 points, or 0.74%, at 10754.26. That still is only about 100 points below
the 3 1/2-year high of 10854.54 hit Dec. 28, but leaves the blue chips down 0.27% for the year.
Stocks began the day with a gain, but ran out of steam after President Bush said in a news conference that
Syria, blamed by some officials for a deadly bomb blast in Lebanon, is "out of step" with other Middle East
countries.
"President Bush's comments on Syria caused the market to really weaken after first half hour," said Henry
Herrmann, chief investment officer at mutual-fund group Waddell &Reed in Overland Park, Kan. It didn't
recover.
In addition, the January reading on leading indicators, which can forecast the economy, was soft. And
Federal Reserve Chairman Alan Greenspan suggested again that the Fed will be raising rates.
The market's underlying problem may be a case of the jitters. With indexes near multiyear highs, investors
worry how much more room remains for gains.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock Index fell 0.79%, or 9.59 points, to 1200.75. It now is down almost 1% since
the year began, about 13 points short of the 3 1/2-year high of 1213.55 hit Dec. 30.
Technology stocks again led the pullback, with the Nasdaq Composite Index falling 1.25%, or 26.09 points, to
2061.34.
Crude-oil futures fell 79 cents to $47.54 a barrel on the New York Mercantile Exchange. Gold rose $1.80 to
$427.30 an ounce.
Outside the U.S., stocks advanced in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
rose 0.45%, or 0.79 point, to 174.69.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks retreated. On the Big Board, where 1.57 billion shares traded, 1,092 stocks rose and 2,208 fell.
Bonds declined. The 10-year Treasury note fell 7/32, or $2.1875 for each $1,000 invested, pushing the yield
up to 4.189%. The 30-year bond was down 30/32 to yield 4.580%.
The dollar was mixed. Late in New York, it traded at 105.54 yen, up from 105.44, while the euro rose against
the dollar to $1.3078 from 1.3027.

Bookshelf: Crying for Argentina
Bookshelf:
LEISURE &ARTS
Bookshelf: Crying for Argentina
By Mary Anastasia O'Grady
873 words
16 February 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
D10
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
AND THE MONEY KEPT ROLLING IN (AND OUT)
By Paul Blustein
(PublicAffairs, 278 pages, $27.50)
On an October evening in 2000, in the waning days of the Clinton administration, Lawrence Summers and
Daniel Marx found themselves heading back to Washington on the same airplane flight. Mr. Marx, the finance
minister of Argentina, had been trying to set up a meeting with Mr. Summers anyway. So Mr. Summers, Mr.
Clinton's Treasury secretary, suggested that they sit together.
It is not hard to imagine why Mr. Marx was keen for conversation. In Mr. Summers's eight years at Treasury,
explains Washington Post staff writer Paul Blustein, he had played "a central role in mobilizing giant
international rescues for crisis-stricken countries in the 1990s, actively wielding America's influence as the
[International Monetary Fund's] number-one shareholder to shape the size and terms of Fund loans."
And Argentina needed a loan badly. Its banking system had lost $789 million in deposits that month alone.
Mr. Marx wanted to make the case that his country deserved rescue status. "We're at the point where we're in
the show-me-the-money mode," Mr. Marx told Mr. Summers.
How did South America's breadbasket, whose economy had grown vigorously through most of the 1990s, get
to such a point? It is an extraordinary tale of bad policy and financial gluttony, culminating in a massive debt
moratorium in December 2001. Even today Argentina owes a mind-boggling $108 billion (including interest
due) and has yet to come up with a repayment plan that its creditors will accept. In "And the Money Kept
Rolling In (And Out)," Mr. Blustein tells the tale with precision and panache, offering inside-baseball details
and, along the way, color commentary.
But the moral eludes him. Mr. Blustein lays the lion's share of the blame on Wall Street greed, which, he
figures, led to lending even when signs of insolvency loomed. But such a conclusion minimizes the role of
Argentina's special-interest politics and unrestrained government, not to mention the ambitions of IMF
bureaucrats. It also pays too little attention to the IMF's most powerful member, the U.S., which throughout
the 1990s signaled a determination to keep Argentina afloat. Such a rescue policy did nothing to help the
country mend its profligate ways.
In 1991, battling hyper-inflation, Argentine President Carlos Saul Menem fixed the peso to the U.S. dollar at
one-to-one, instituting a law dubbed "convertibility." Mr. Blustein defines it neatly: "The system required the
central bank to keep enough dollars or gold in reserve to back the total amount of pesos that had been
printed, strictly limiting the ability of the central bank to increase the supply of pesos in the economy." The
idea was to guard the value of the currency, but Argentina had not known such limits in decades, and it
wasn't ready to accept the restraints on the spending that must accompany such a move. Instead of printing
money, it began to borrow. And borrow some more.
There were other problems, too. Special-interest groups blocked attempts to liberalize a still heavily regulated
economy. Even two privatized telecom companies ended up as a dual monopoly, splitting the country
between them and making a key commercial sector uncompetitive. And then there were the powerful unions,
demanding job guarantees and keeping costs too high. Taxes were stiflingly steep, trade policy too restrictive,
and the corruption of the Menem government was, as Mr. Blustein notes, legendary.
Under such conditions, a dynamic economy was nearly impossible. Things got even worse when Brazil
devalued its currency in 1999 and the dollar grew stronger. The effect was to make Argentine goods
expensive for the consumers of Brazil, its biggest trading partner.

--- Page 11 ---
Page 11 of 265 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
So why did financial markets keep lending to Argentina long after the writing was on the wall? The simplest
answer -- one played down by Mr. Blustein despite the evidence he helpfully provides -- is that IMF packages
interfered with the signaling between markets and the Argentine democracy. Such packages were supposed
to confer, as Mr. Blustein notes, "a sort of `Good Housekeeping Seal of Approval' to reassure markets that
the country's policies were on the right track." But once the policies were off-track, the fund was loath to lift
the seal. And since more lending followed the IMF's "one-last-chance" declarations, more borrowing followed,
too.
Mr. Blustein records all this but then ponders how government regulations can better control market avarice
and irrational exuberance. He ponders as well the value of "strict" IMF lending quotas -- i.e., "this time we
really mean it." He even backs a global bankruptcy court run by the IMF, as if the same politicized thinking
that helped the fund to wreck an economy in the first place wouldn't affect courtroom judgments as well.
The lesson is a truism but bears repeating: Markets run to excess when it looks as if someone else will bear
the cost. Yet another bailout? Surely two finance ministers on a plane flight have other things to talk about.
---
Ms. O'Grady edits the Journal's Americas column.

AMERICAS
Brazilian Leader Loses Leverage in Congress
RIO DE JANEIRO -- In a major political setback for Brazilian President Luiz Inacio Lula da Silva, a member of
his ruling Workers Party lost the leadership election for the lower house of Congress yesterday, limiting Mr.
da Silva's influence over the congressional voting schedule as he starts the second half of his four-year term.
The defeat will drive up the political price of passing the government's agenda, such as plans to overhaul the
country's rigid labor code and grant autonomy to the central bank. While Mr. da Silva has won applause on
Wall Street for managing the country's finances, his administration has failed to deliver deeper changes to
make it easier for companies to do business here.
The defeat came after Mr. da Silva and his cabinet lobbied heavily for the party candidate, Luiz Eduardo
Greenhalgh, who lost the race in a runoff vote to 74-year-old Severino Cavalcanti, a member of the
center-right Progressive Party.
Investors took the news in stride. Brazil's currency slipped marginally against the dollar to 2.585 reals from
2.580 reals, while stock prices rose slightly.
The newly elected speaker, although a self-declared ally of the administration, is a member of a party known
to engage in heavy political bargaining. His party will likely push for appointments for government positions as
well as spending in areas that benefit his political base.
"The stress level will go up," said Bolivar Lamounier, head of Augurium, a political consulting firm. "Congress
is telling the administration to not take for granted the approval of legislation. They will have to negotiate
much more."
Mr. Lamounier said the labor overhaul, which already was difficult to approve, will get even more difficult if the
administration isn't more humble in its dealings with congress.
After his win, Mr. Cavalcanti moved to ease concerns about his election, saying in a television interview that
he will work to help Mr. da Silva have "an exceptional administration."
Government officials tried to put the best face on the defeat. "The government has regrouped after each
defeat," said a spokeswoman, adding that Mr. Cavalcanti sided with the government on all major issues.
It was the first time in Brazil's history that the largest party in the house failed to secure the speaker position.
Mr. da Silva's party also failed to elect members to the 11-person committee that controls the chamber's
voting schedule.
-- Geraldo Samor
---
ASIA/PACIFIC
Koreans Shake Off Credit Crunch, Go Shopping
SEOUL, South Korea -- Shrinking domestic demand, the main drag on the South Korean economy for two
years, shows signs of a turnaround, as shoppers shake off effects of a prolonged household-credit crunch.

--- Page 13 ---
Page 13 of 265 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The rebound could help offset slowing export growth and keep the world's 10th-largest economy expanding,
economists say. That is good news for exporters in the U.S., Japan and China, South Korea's biggest
sources of imports.
The Ministry of Finance and Economy said credit-card spending in January jumped 14.8% from a year earlier.
Consumer confidence also has improved. An index compiled by the National Statistical Office, in which
consumers are asked whether they believe they will be better off in six months, rose in January for the first
time in nine months. Average monthly income growth accelerated from 6.8% in the first quarter of 2004 to
7.3% in the third quarter.
Stores are just starting to see an improvement in business after a bad stretch. In the last five months of 2004,
discount-store sales rose nearly 1% from a year earlier, while department-store sales fell about 2%. But Lotte,
the country's largest department-store chain, says it expects that this month (February) will produce its
highest monthly sales growth in two years.
"There is much better statistical data these days," Finance Minister Lee Hun Jai said this month. "This is a
transitional patter that emerges ahead of a major recovery in an economy."
Lim Ji Won, an economist at J.P. Morgan in Seoul, forecasts a 1.5% increase in private consumption this
year, as personal incomes and employment rise. Terence Lim, head of research at Goldman Sachs in Seoul,
expects a rise of 0.6%. But he is confident the turnaround will be sustained, if modest, because consumer
sentiment is improving.
South Korea has struggled to overcome the effects of a spending binge gone bad. Issuers of credit cards, at
the urging of a government eager to boost domestic consumption, a few years ago handed out cards without
proper risk-management controls. At the end of November 2004, one in 13 South Koreans were behind on
card payments. Credit-card delinquency rates, which measure overdue payments as a percentage of total
charges, peaked at 28.3% at the end of 2003, when credit-card debt was 53 trillion won, about $50 billion.
Delinquency rates since fell, as regulators pushed card issuers to tighten screening and rein in credit growth.
Total credit-card debt at the end of the third quarter of 2004, the latest period for which data are available,
was 37.9 trillion won, while the delinquency rate was 23.06%. Regulators and card issuers say they are
confident that the credit-worthiness of borrowers has improved because of higher lending standards.
The government is targeting 5% economic growth this year, though forecasts by private economists are
somewhat lower. Official gross-domestic-product figures for 2004 haven't yet been released, though the
government has estimated that the economy grew about 4.7%.
While hopes for growth in consumer spending have sparked some optimism about the Korean economy,
there are risks ahead. The biggest is the appreciation of the won against the dollar. That, combined with an
uncertain outlook for the U.S. economy, could pinch profits of Korean auto and electronics makers, which
export more than 50% of their production to the U.S.
-- Hae Won Choi
Indonesian Doctor Retracts Newmont Accusation
DENVER -- The Indonesian doctor who accused Newmont Mining Corp. of poisoning villagers near its
Minahasa gold mine on the island of Sulawesi has retracted her allegations.
Jane Pangemanan sent a letter to Indonesian police this month saying her complaints that Newmont's waste
disposal caused diseases among local villagers were "premature." She added in the letter: "There was never
any scientific, comprehensive, detailed and integrated proof" that nearby villagers were being poisoned.
Newmont, of Denver, had filed a defamation suit against Dr. Pangemanan that the company has dropped as
result of her retraction, a company spokesman said. The spokesman added there is no money involved in the
settlement and Dr. Pangemanan said she is retracting her allegations "without any coercion, pressure or
threat from any party."
Newmont, the world's largest gold producer, has been embroiled in months of controversy over its Minahasa
mine. Indonesian authorities have accused Newmont's Indonesian unit, PT Newmont Minahasa Raya, of
dumping mercury and arsenic into nearby Buyat Bay, causing residents to develop tumors and skin diseases.
Newmont has repeatedly denied the charges.

Dow Industrials End Just Below A 3 1/2-Year High --- January Retail Sales Help Stocks Ahead of Greenspan's Testimony; Treasury-Bond Prices...
Tuesday's Markets
Dow Industrials End Just Below A 3 1/2-Year High --- January Retail Sales Help Stocks Ahead of
Greenspan's Testimony; Treasury-Bond Prices Decline
By E.S. Browning
576 words
16 February 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
THE DOW JONES Industrial Average pushed within sight of a 3 1/2-year high as investors waited nervously
for Federal Reserve Chairman Alan Greenspan's Senate testimony today.
Helped by better-than-expected January retail-sales figures from the Commerce Department, the industrials
rose 46.19 points, or 0.43%, to 10837.32. That is a gain of 0.5% since the year began. The Dow industrials
now are only barely below the Dec. 28 finish of 10854.54, which was their highest close since well before the
terrorist attack of Sept. 11, 2001.
Trading was disrupted during the day by an options-trading glitch involving the Nasdaq 100-stock index,
which includes most of the 100 biggest stocks traded on the Nasdaq Stock Market.
By day's end, despite the disruption, all of the major indexes were showing modest gains.
The stock gains reflected hopes that Mr. Greenspan's testimony won't include any significant departures from
what he has been saying for months: that the economy is growing smoothly, that inflation is under control
and that the Fed can continue raising its target lending rate at a "measured" pace.
"We got through earnings season with no real major disasters, and the market was expected to be pretty
tame ahead of the Greenspan testimony," said Stephen Sachs, director of trading at mutual-fund group
Rydex Investments in Rockville, Md. Many traders were avoiding major commitments until they heard what
Mr. Greenspan had to say, Mr. Sachs added.
The expectation that Mr. Greenspan would make positive comments about economic growth helped push
down Treasury-bond prices. The stronger the economy, the more interest rates are likely to rise, and rising
rates make existing bonds, with their lower rates, less attractive. A strong economy also induces investors to
move to higher-yielding corporate bonds from Treasury bonds.
In addition to the retail-sales news, investors were pleased to see Circuit City Stores get a takeover offer.
Both the dollar and crude-oil prices fell, while gold prices continued to rebound.
The broad Standard and Poor's 500-stock index rose 0.33%, or 3.98 points, to 1210.12, now down a slim
0.15% since the year began. It is nearing the 3 1/2-year high of 1213.55 hit on Dec. 30.
The Nasdaq Composite Index rose 0.3%, or 6.30 points, to 2089.21. It remains down almost 4% for 2005 and
isn't near its own 3 1/2-year high hit late last year.
Outside the U.S., stocks advanced in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
rose 0.32%, or 0.56 point, to 174.74.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks advanced. On the New York Stock Exchange, where 1.52 billion shares traded, 1,758 stocks rose and
1,498 fell.
Bonds declined. The 10-year Treasury note fell 7/32, or $2.1875 for each $1,000 invested, pushing the yield
up to 4.106%. The 30-year bond was down 21/32 to yield 4.489%.
The dollar weakened. Late in New York, the currency traded at 104.43 yen, down from 105.17 yen, while the
euro rose against the dollar to $1.3018 from $1.2959.

Greenspan's Quiver
Greenspan's Quiver
By Brian S. Wesbury
1,367 words
15 February 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
A18
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Twice a year, typically in February and July, the chairman of the Federal Reserve gives nearly identical
testimony to both the House and the Senate. The Monetary Policy Report to the Congress (formerly known
as the Humphrey-Hawkins report) reflects the thinking of the Fed as a whole.
With his retirement looming on Jan. 31, 2006, Alan Greenspan has just four such testimonies left whereby he
can help shape monetary policy in a post-Greenspan-era. One area likely to be addressed tomorrow is
"inflation targeting." According to the well-connected Wall Street Journal reporter, Greg Ip, the Fed
discussed this at its last meeting.
Inflation targeting by central banks in the U.K., New Zealand, Canada, Israel, Sweden and elsewhere has
been successful. But establishing a specific target for inflation (say between 0% and 2%) would hamper Fed
freedom, while increasing accountability. This is a trade-off that the Fed is unlikely to accept, despite the clear
benefits such targeting would bring to the U.S. economy.
---
After 17 years, Mr. Greenspan has accumulated incredible amounts of political capital and is reported to be in
total control of monetary policy. It seems clear that the Fed operates on a Greenspan Standard: The
chairman's assessment of the economy rules the day.
While most market participants and politicians give the Fed stellar reviews, those reviews are somewhat
suspect. The combination of Mr. Greenspan's political capital, politicians who are reluctant to challenge the
Fed, a press corps willing to trade glowing reviews for access, and private economists who all dream of
becoming Fed members, guarantees the Fed an endless supply of get-out-of-jail-free cards.
In 1989, the Fed hiked the federal-funds rate to 9.75% and the real (inflation-adjusted) funds rate hit 5%.
The recession that started in July 1990 is widely blamed on Saddam Hussein's invasion of Kuwait and rising
oil prices. No one seems to remember that Iraq did not invade Kuwait until August; one month after the
Fed-induced recession began.
In response to the recession, the Fed cut the funds rate to 3% in 1993, which pushed the real rate below zero
-- an unsustainable position. The result was a sharp increase in rates in 1994 and one of the worst years in
bond market history. Many suggest that all turned out fine because the stock market resumed its climb in
1995, the economy did not fall into recession, and inflation remained low.
But massive losses in bond funds (including Orange County) and the collapse of Mexico suggest otherwise.
Market participants were caught by surprise when the Fed tightened so aggressively. This should go down as
a Fed mistake.
The Fed denies targeting the stock market in 1999-2000, when it pushed the funds rate to 6.5% (a real rate of
4%). It claims that it was targeting the "effects" of a rising stock market -- overheating, inflation, etc. But this
is a distinction without a difference. It is impossible to target the effects of a rising stock market without
targeting the stock market itself. Of course, popular wisdom blames the resulting deflation, recession, and
Nasdaq collapse on a "bubble" -- and not on the Fed.
The Fed claims that it employs "risk management" techniques that assess probabilities and outcomes of
different forecasts and policy decisions. But how can this type of monetary policy work if the Fed always
blames any negative events on things outside of its control. It is easy to claim success when you can do no
wrong.
With Mr. Greenspan at the helm, the Fed is attempting to become more transparent by releasing more
detailed statements and accelerating the publication of minutes. But because the Fed has used these tools to

--- Page 18 ---
Page 18 of 265 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
manipulate expectations in financial markets, a lack of transparency has developed. The market is still
involved in a guessing game, not knowing exactly where the Fed will go next.
In early 2003, following some comments by Fed members about buying Treasury bonds to counteract
deflation, the 10-year Treasury bond yield fell to 3.1%. It then jumped to 4.6% -- a move of 150 basis points --
in just six weeks as this talk subsided. Many believed the Fed would not hike rates before the election in
2004, but this was wrong too. Last week, a better-than-expected report on initial unemployment claims
caused long-term bond yields to move up by 10 basis points in one day. It is not the data that matters per se,
but what the data mean for Fed policy.
This uncertainty is now impacting the global economy. Earlier this year, in Davos, Fan Gang, director of the
National Economic Research Institute at the China Reform Foundation said, in less than perfect but quite
clear English: "The U.S. dollar is no longer -- in our opinion is no longer -- [seen] as a stable currency, and is
devaluating all the time, and that's putting troubles all the time." Of course, he is referring to the 30% run-up
in the value of the dollar versus the euro during the late '90s, and the 40% drop during the past four years.
Fed policy has created a great deal of volatility and the falling value of the dollar has distorted world-wide
prices.
Oil valued in dollars is up 20% versus its peak in 2000, but when valued in euros, the price of oil has fallen by
20%. In other words, it is not a shortage of oil that is driving prices up; it is the falling value of the dollar. If
there were a true shortage of oil, its price would be up in all currencies.
Inflation is rising despite a huge surge in productivity. Nonfarm productivity climbed at a 4% annual rate in
the past two years, double its 30-year average. Yet the consumer price index, excluding food and energy,
increased from a 1.1% rate in 2003 to a 2.3% rate in 2004. The "core" PCE deflator, the Fed's preferred
measure of inflation accelerated from 1.0% to 1.5%. Rising inflation in an age of rapid productivity growth is
a sign of wildly accommodative Fed policy.
What is possibly the most interesting development of all is that the bond market is ignoring the increase in
inflation. Real, after-tax, 10-year Treasury yields have been below zero for the longest period since the
stagflation of 25 year ago. This anomaly is the result of a deliberate Fed policy that confuses transparency
with market management. By telling the market that it will hike rates at a "measured pace," the Fed has
encouraged many investors to use leverage liberally -- borrowing short-term money to invest long term.
The impact is a lower set of long-term yields than is justified by the fundamentals. These low long-term bond
yields signal to some at the Fed that there is no need to worry about inflation. The markets take the Fed's
sanguine attitude as a sign that rate hikes will be minimal, which drives long-term rates down further. This
feedback loop could lead to some very bad monetary policy decisions.
Inflation targeting would be truly transparent and help avoid these mistakes. Rather than attempting to
manage markets, the Fed would focus solely on price pressures. This policy would be more stable and
certain, and less risky.
If the Fed were focused on a complete set of inflation measures, including the dollar, gold, commodity
prices and broad inflation aggregates, it never would have tightened so much in 1999 and 2000. While the
stock market may have fallen, the damage would have been less severe and the U.S. would have avoided
recession and deflation. In addition, the Fed would never have had to cut rates to 1% in the years that
followed.
With only 11 months left before his retirement, a proper legacy for Alan Greenspan would be to institutionalize
the low-inflation environment of the past decade. Inflation targets could do just that -- and at the same time
bring true transparency and less volatility to monetary policy.
---
Mr. Wesbury is chief economist at Griffin, Kubik, Stephens &Thompson Inc.

Power Play: In Qatar, Oil Firms Make Huge Bet On Alternative Fuel --- Supporters Say New Diesel Is Cleaner, More Efficient; Untested on...
Power Play: In Qatar, Oil Firms Make Huge Bet On Alternative Fuel --- Supporters Say New Diesel Is
Cleaner, More Efficient; Untested on Large Scale --- `It Was Just Like Water'
By Russell Gold
2,312 words
15 February 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
RAS LAFFAN, Qatar -- Here in the Persian Gulf, hundreds of workers are piecing together a coiled labyrinth
of pipes that they hope will turn natural gas into an ultraclean automotive fuel -- and challenge the oil industry.
These plants will produce a clear liquid that operators say will have the high efficiency of diesel fuel, but
virtually none of the sooty pollutants. Millions of diesel cars and trucks around the world could run on this fuel.
The gas-to-liquids, or GTL, plants under way here are part of a big bet by a small nation to reshape global
energy markets. Some of the world's biggest energy companies, including Exxon Mobil Corp., Royal
Dutch/Shell Group and ChevronTexaco Corp., have committed $20 billion to build GTL facilities in an
industrial park here that's twice the size of Manhattan. They are using an unusual technology whose lineage
traces back to Nazi Germany and apartheid South Africa. The projects are one of the largest and riskiest
gambles by the industry in years.
"Qatar is going to become in the gas business what Saudi Arabia is in the oil business," says Wayne Harms,
Exxon Mobil's top official in Qatar. Largely blocked from doing business in Saudi Arabia, Exxon is investing in
Qatar's natural gas. It expects to spend $15 billion to $17 billion in coming years building facilities in the giant
industrial park, including $7 billion for a GTL plant, the largest single investment in the company's history.
The companies say these GTL facilities -- unlike some earlier, smaller efforts -- make economic sense
because of technological advances and the lower costs from building on such a large scale. They say the
result will be an alternative fuel that should be priced competitively with crude oil.
The challenges are also huge. Only small amounts of GTL fuel are sold in the world today. The process to
make it gobbles up a lot of energy, and no one has ever built a GTL plant of the size of those here. If the
costs of producing GTL on this gargantuan scale run higher than expected, or if the price of competing crude
oil tumbles, the investments could be at risk.
Qatar has championed GTL in the hope it will provide a new market for its huge natural-gas reserves. If GTL
catches on, natural gas could loosen crude oil's grip on one of the biggest energy markets of all -- powering
the world's vehicles, which accounts for 26% of all energy use. Home to some 900 trillion cubic feet of natural
gas -- more than 14% of world gas reserves and enough to meet current global demand for natural gas for 10
years -- Qatar would be a leading beneficiary in such a sea change.
"We want to be a big part of bringing this fuel to the world," says Qatar's energy minister Abdullah bin Hamad
al Attiyah, as he sits in his wood-paneled office, decorated with gold-plated miniatures of offshore-drilling
rigs. He calls GTL "the fuel of the 21st century."
Whether or not Qatar's natural-gas bet pays off could be important to the West. Unlike Saudi Arabia, which
has long resented Western influences in its closed society, Qatar is courting the U.S. and other industrialized
countries. The current emir, Sheik Hamad bin Khalifa al Thani, who ousted his father in a bloodless coup in
1995, has radically reshaped Qatar in ways that ruffle Saudi Arabia's feathers.
Unlike Saudi Arabia, Qatar has a largely free media. When Saudi Arabia refused to allow U.S. warplanes in
the kingdom to fly missions against Iraq in the late 1990s, Qatar opened its airspace. The U.S. military ran its
war to oust Saddam Hussein from Iraq from Qatar.
The promise of GTL fuel is alluring. Diesel engines tend to get about one-third better fuel economy than
gasoline engines. The problem is that conventional diesel -- which is made from crude oil -- is much higher in
pollutants that cause smog than is gasoline. By contrast, GTL diesel, because it comes from natural gas, one
of the cleanest fossil fuels, has just a fraction of the pollutants found in crude oil. And current diesel engines
can run on it without modification.

--- Page 20 ---
Page 20 of 265 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Despite such benefits, few cars use GTL diesel today. Only a tiny amount is manufactured right now, mostly
at small plants in Malaysia and South Africa. Few drivers have ever heard of GTL or are even aware of the
possibility of a cleaner diesel fuel.
Even the energy companies say they aren't yet sure exactly how they'll market it. They might try to sell it
directly to consumers, or initially to refiners, who may blend it with diesel fuel they already manufacture. "We'll
put our product where it makes us the most money," says Exxon's Mr. Harms.
Western energy companies are taking the gamble because GTL plants in Qatar offer a rare opportunity: a
chance to make a big investment in a place where there are a lot of undeveloped resources. Despite running
up record profits, the oil giants are locked out of much of the Middle East, home to some 65% of the world's
oil reserves. Most of the known oil reserves are in a handful of Persian Gulf countries -- Saudi Arabia, Iraq,
Iran and Kuwait -- that are either unstable or closed to Western development.
Qatar, however, is far more open. In the capital's bustling City Centre mall, a four-story behemoth with an
indoor ice-skating rink, women browse in chic shops for Western clothes, although many wear black chadors
in public. Women can vote, work and drive in Qatar, none of which is permitted in Saudi Arabia. Several
years ago, the energy ministry even abandoned the Middle East's traditional Thursday-Friday weekend in
favor of a Friday-Saturday break that kept it more in step with the rest of the globe. The government later
followed suit.
Natural gas is more broadly dispersed around the world than oil. Much of it is in less-insular nations such as
Russia, Qatar, Norway and Australia. So hopping into the gas-to-liquid business allows the Western energy
companies back in the game, in a big way.
Even before GTL, natural gas was chipping away at oil's position as the world's primary fuel. Natural gas
provides 23% of global energy today, up from 17% in 1970, thanks largely to a recent spree of building
natural-gas-fired electricity-power plants. If natural-gas use continues to grow, it could pass coal as the
world's second-most-used fuel by the end of the decade and catch oil as the top fuel by 2025, according to
forecasters for Royal Dutch/Shell Group. Oil now accounts for 39% of global energy use.
The first GTL diesel fuel from Qatar is expected to hit the market later this year. By 2010, Qatar and its
partners plan to produce up to 750,000 barrels a day -- about 6% of current global consumption.
Diesel usage is expected to grow quickly in industrialized economies, although it isn't widespread in the U.S.
While some trucks use the fuel, less than one percent of new cars in the U.S. run on diesel. By contrast,
about half the new passenger cars in Europe run on conventional diesel.
Shell discovered the North Field, the world's biggest natural-gas field, off the coast of Qatar in 1971. The
problem was that the gas was too far away from potential markets for a pipeline. In essence, the field was like
an enormous bank, but Qatar had no way of getting the money out.
In the mid-1990s, Qatar began building expensive facilities to chill natural gas into a liquid and ship it to
Western markets on giant thermos-like ships. The natural gas is chilled, shipped, and then reheated into gas
form, mostly for industrial use or for electrical-power generation. GTL, on the other hand, transforms the
natural gas into automotive fuel.
The chemical process at the heart of modern GTL plants was developed in 1923 Germany. That country was
rich in coal, but had little petroleum, so two scientists decided to figure out how to turn coal into a liquid fuel.
They flooded coal with steam to turn it into a gas, and then ran it through pipes lined with cobalt to create a
liquid fuel. The Nazi government subsidized the nascent industry as it geared up its war machine. Germany
got as much as 15% of its motor fuel from coal-to-liquids plants, according to Texas A&M University history
professor Anthony Stranges.
After the end of the war, the U.S. government experimented with the German technology because it feared
domestic crude-oil supplies were running out. But gigantic discoveries of oil in the Middle East soon led to an
abundance of global crude, and the experiments were dropped.
Interest in coal-to-liquids research dried up world-wide -- except in South Africa. Like Germany, South Africa
has a lot of coal, but little crude oil. The state oil company acquired the rights from Germany to key parts of
the technology and developed its own coal-to-liquids plants.
When the United Nations led an oil embargo against South Africa's government, the country was forced to
keep refining its technology. It eventually determined that it was more cost-effective to use natural gas, not
coal, as the main ingredient.
But South Africa doesn't have a lot of natural gas. In the mid-1990s, its former state oil company, privatized
and renamed Sasol Ltd., went looking for a large supply of natural gas. It set its sights on Qatar. At first, the

--- Page 21 ---
Page 21 of 265 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
reaction was lukewarm. "It was like selling encyclopedias. You knocked on doors and tried to get people
interested," Cavan Hill, then Sasol's manager of business development, says of the first cold calls to Qatari
officials. "There was a lot of skepticism about whether this was an academic curiosity or whether it could be a
commercial process."
The Qataris brought in an outside energy consultant to analyze the economics of a GTL plant. The results
were good enough that Mr. Hill was able to secure meetings with Qatar's top officials. Finance minister
Youssef Kamal, also the vice chairman of the state-run Qatar Petroleum, recalls being intrigued when he saw
his first vial of natural gas-derived diesel. Crude oil is generally black because it's full of impurities such as
sulfur. "They showed me this liquid and it was just like water," he says.
(MORE)
In July 2001, Sasol and Qatar officials signed an agreement to jointly build the first commercial-scale GTL
plant. Then Qatar started leaning on Western energy giants. Mr. Attiyah, the energy minister, sent them a
message: If they wanted additional access to Qatar's natural gas, they would have to build GTL plants. He
"had to work pretty hard to cajole the companies, to get everyone where he wants them to be, to do GTL
projects," says Exxon's Mr. Harms.
In 2003, Qatar and Shell signed an agreement to build a $6 billion GTL plant. Exxon finalized a deal last year.
There are ongoing talks with ConocoPhillips and a joint-venture between Sasol and ChevronTexaco to
expand GTL plants.
Like all alternative fuels, the economics of GTL hinge on world oil prices. Bernard J. Picchi, an analyst for
Foresight Research Solutions LLC, says it will cost about $14 to produce every barrel of GTL diesel. With oil
currently at about $47 a barrel, that makes GTL look sweet. If world oil prices plummet to $25 a barrel, a GTL
plant still could yield a 19% return on investment. Below $20 oil, however, the returns aren't attractive, Mr.
Picchi says.
Once the first plant is operational later this year, the natural gas will be fed into a three-quarter-mile structure
that is longer and heavier -- at about 19,000 tons -- than the main span of the Brooklyn Bridge. The gas is fed
through a series of four furnaces where it is mixed with oxygen and water and burned to create a
carbon-monoxide gas. The gas is then fed into a 20-story-tall cylinder with 4-inch-thick steel walls. Exactly
what goes on inside is a proprietary secret, but essentially the gas is exposed to cobalt -- a metallic-gray ore
often found on meteors. When the gas comes into contact with the cobalt, the interaction rearranges the
molecular structure and turns the gas into a waxy petroleum. This wax is then put under high pressure to
break it into diesel, and a smaller amount of other products, including motor oil.
Shell built a similar, but much smaller, plant in Malaysia in 1993 to see if it could manufacture GTL. The GTL
fuel from that plant has been used in small-scale tests in buses in London and Shanghai and in a fleet of
water-delivery trucks in California. It's also for sale in limited quantities to the public in Germany, Greece and
Thailand.
On a recent day, Benjasook Chumhepan filled up her Toyota pickup with "Pura Diesel" at a Shell station in
Bangkok. The GTL diesel costs about 7% more than regular diesel -- an effort by Shell to see if drivers are
willing to pay a little more for a cleaner fuel. Ms. Chumhepan says she buys it regularly because it cuts down
on the truck's emissions. "The engine runs smoothly and doesn't emit black smoke," she says.
---
Kullawee Pongpattanajit in Bangkok contributed to this article.

Home Prices: Our Towns
WEEKEND JOURNAL
Home Prices: Our Towns
By June Fletcher
2,708 words
11 February 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
W1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
[As home prices soar, we survey more than 1,000 neighborhoods, finding big gains from Miami to Wayzata,
Minn. June Fletcher on unexpected hot spots -- and whether the boom can last.]
ROBERT ALBERTSON put his five-bedroom lakeside house in tiny Wayzata, Minn., up for sale last year at
$1.4 million -- and, almost immediately, pulled it off the market. Calls from eager buyers convinced Mr.
Albertson, who helped invent Mr. Coffee, that it was worth waiting to see if prices would rise.
They did: A few months' delay added another $100,000 to the value of the Tudor-style home, according to
two recent appraisals, and he plans to put it back on the market at $1.5 million. Says Mr. Albertson, who
bought the house in 1963 for $38,000: "Prices here have just gone crazy."
What home-price bubble? So far, predictions of the U.S. housing market's demise have been greatly
exaggerated. Despite some dark clouds -- war, a looming deficit, the weak dollar -- this market has continued
to rise, with existing-home prices up 8.3% through the end of 2004, according to the National Association of
Realtors, compared with 7.5% the year before. As low interest rates fueled sales, dozens of cities saw
double-digit gains: In Glendale, Calif., where prices had already doubled in the past four years, the median
home price rose 29.8% last year, to $440,000. In the old whaling town Marion, Mass., prices rose 13.5%, to
an average of $550,000.
No one can say for sure if this can continue. But at least some homeowners can brag that their neighborhood
did even better than most. In a town-by-town survey conducted for Weekend Journal by housing specialists
Fiserv CSW, we analyzed home-price changes in more than 1,200 ZIP codes for the year ending September
2004. To get a sense of the longer-term phenomenon, we also looked back at the change in these areas
compared with five years ago.
Some of the results were striking. From Florida's Gulf Coast to the Jersey Shore, some of the fastest-growing
neighborhoods are ones that used to be second choices. Some people are relocating less for jobs than for
views: Waterfront access was the feature many of our fastest-appreciating towns shared, along with new
commuter lines or magnet schools. Spillover was a factor in some booming areas, as many of our "hot"
neighborhoods turned out to be right next door to longtime enclaves of wealth (see: Montclair, N.J.; south
Orange County). Meanwhile, only 10 neighborhoods we surveyed showed price declines; four of those were
in Salt Lake City suburbs, which saw heavy building ahead of the 2002 Winter Olympics.
The housing market's continued growth has surprised some economists who thought, last year at this time,
that home prices were headed for a fall. Many of these people still believe prices will drop this year, as
expected mortgage-rate increases kick in. "It's like the stock market in the late '90s," says economist Dean
Baker, co-director of the Center for Economic and Policy Research in Washington, D.C. He points out that
overall, housing prices have grown by 40% since 1995, adjusted for inflation, the steepest rise in five
decades. "It's too overheated," he says.
However, other economists say they don't see home prices falling anytime soon -- though they do expect
gains to be more modest. Lawrence Yun, senior forecast economist for the National Association of Realtors,
expects growth to slow to 5% over the next year based in part on anticipated interest-rate rises. However, he
doesn't see prices falling, he says, because job creation is expected to rise, boomers are still in their peak
buying years and the inventory of homes available for purchase is currently at a historic low.
Even economists who believe the market is still primed for growth say some areas are overheated. They
point to Las Vegas and Southern California, for example, where speculators are "flipping" homes for profit
shortly after buying them. In Las Vegas, 16.1% of all housing loans last year went to investors rather than
those buying a primary residence, nearly double the national average, according to San Francisco research
firm Loan Performance Inc.

--- Page 24 ---
Page 24 of 265 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
For our survey, Fiserv CSW looked at price changes for the five years ended in September for 1,237 ZIP
codes across the country -- areas where median home sales were higher than in surrounding areas, and
where 100 or more home transactions had typically been recorded. Overall, home prices in these areas were
up 14.7% in the year ended September 2004.
In general, neighborhoods in the West showed the strongest growth, but grew only half as fast in the
Northwest, still reeling from the tech-industry slowdown. In the South, coastal towns performed the best. Price
gains in the Midwest and Southwest, with the exception of bright spots like Scottsdale, Ariz., and Oak Park,
Ill., were sluggish. And the priciest town in America? In our survey, it's Santa Monica, Calif., with a median
home price of $1.928 million -- up nearly 30% in the last year alone.
In Rockville, Md., homeowner Bryan Hubbard says local home prices have grown "much faster than I
anticipated." Mr. Hubbard, a spokesman for the U.S. Defense Department, says he's now asking $990,000
for his seven-bedroom Victorian home in the Washington, D.C., suburb, double what he paid for it four years
ago, and is marketing the home without the help of a real-estate agent. If his family moved into town today,
Mr. Hubbard adds, "I don't think we'd be able to afford the house we have."
Here is our region-by-region look at the market -- including ZIP codes with median home prices above
$400,000 and particularly strong price growth over the past five years:
Southeast
St. Pete Beach, Fla. 33706
ONE-YEAR APPRECIATION: 22.7%
FIVE-YEAR APPRECIATION: 105.6%
SIDEWALK VIEW: Sleepy beach town wakes up
"Life is short -- max out your credit cards," reads the sign in a jeweler in this seaside town, situated on a
two-mile barrier island in the Gulf of Mexico. Once a sleepy retirement mecca and vacation retreat for the
likes of Clarence Darrow and Al Capone, the 17,000-resident town is benefiting from the growing local tech
industry.
Median home prices here have more than doubled in five years, to $410,000, as foreign investors take
advantage of a weak dollar and bidding wars are common. New condominium projects are pulling median
prices up even more, with starting prices of around $700,000.
Kathleen Johnson, formerly a real-estate investor in San Francisco, scouted areas along the Gulf of Florida
before settling in St. Pete Beach five years ago. She bought two properties in 2002 -- a waterfront condo for
$197,000 and an inland home for $239,000 -- and sold them last year for a combined $164,000 gain. Many
neighbors, she says, are also speculating in real estate. "It's easy to find the buyers," she says, but "harder
and harder to find the homes."
Northwest
Lake Oswego, Ore. 97034
ONE-YEAR APPRECIATION: 11.1%
FIVE-YEAR APPRECIATION: 41.8%
SIDEWALK VIEW: Hobbit village by a lake
This bedroom community eight miles south of downtown Portland is one of the best-performing areas in the
Northwest, which has seen slowdowns in the software, aerospace and lumber industries. Once a steel town,
Lake Oswego now has a series of sloped-roof, English-style cottages surrounded by a dense pine forest.
Construction crews are everywhere, adding new turrets to Tudor-style mansions or demolishing cottages. A
downtown renovation is transforming old industrial areas into parks and offices. "There's some big money out
here, and they don't want to look" at auto shops and machinery, says local architect Ray Totten.
Last year, 54 homes sold for more than $1 million here -- but the only people with access to the lake are
homeowners with frontage property. Most of the buyers for the area's homes (median price last year:
$525,000) are year-round residents who instead say they're attracted to the area's top-ranked schools.
Physician Arthur Rott wants to sell the 3,100-square-foot home where he's lived for 15 years, but he's not
sure if he should bother to update the kitchen and baths. Looking out his bay window at a home being torn

--- Page 25 ---
Page 25 of 265 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
down across the street, he says, "My house is a perfect `Leave It To Beaver' kind of place -- but that's not
enough around here anymore."
Midwest
Wayzata, Minn. 55391
ONE-YEAR APPRECIATION: 9.6%
FIVE-YEAR APPRECIATION: 67.5%
SIDEWALK VIEW: Construction hits Mayberry
There's not a McDonald's or Burger King anywhere along the 10-block stretch of road bordering the lake in
Wayzata, Minn., and "two cars at a stop sign count as a traffic jam," says real-estate broker Keith Anderson.
Just 12 miles west of Minneapolis, Wayzata is the gateway to Lake Minnetonka, one of the most popular
recreation spots in the state. Residents spend their summers sailing, riding the red-and-gold trolley to the
town center or cruising on a restored 1906 steamboat, the Minnehaha.
This quaintness comes at a price. The 4,100-resident town had one of the highest rates of appreciation in the
state over the last five years. At $522,000, the median home price is almost 2 1/2 times that of the
surrounding area, and brick and Tudor-style mansions here have sold for up to $12 million. Some critics
worry about young professionals buying up remaining buildable lots to put up big homes that lack small-town
character. Says architect Sarah Susanka: "They're grander, but blander."
Mid-Atlantic
Rockville, Md. 20850
ONE-YEAR APPRECIATION: 20.5%
FIVE-YEAR APPRECIATION: 105.4%
SIDEWALK VIEW: Historic town, with gridlock
Just 20 miles northwest of the White House, Rockville is one of the newer stops on the Washington, D.C.,
Metro line and one of the capital's major bedroom communities. The 200-year-old town includes Victorian-era
homes and a historic cemetery -- F. Scott Fitzgerald and his wife, Zelda, are buried there -- but the town's
overall feel is more modern. The main thoroughfare is lined with strip malls, and known for its near-daily traffic
jams.
(MORE)
Many of Rockville's 55,000 residents work for the federal government, but technology is a growing presence.
In the last year, the area added 615,000 square feet of office space, more than twice the amount of
neighboring Gaithersburg. Planners are also demanding some more pedestrian-friendly projects: In the
town's center, a $353 million redevelopment is under way to create a leafy plaza -- the plan is to hold a
farmer's market there -- surrounded by red-brick buildings for a library and shops. Another developer has
hired architect Andres Duany, founder of the New Urbanist movement, to design a center that will mix
residential lofts, offices and stores.
On average, homes in Rockville (median price in our survey: $485,000) are selling for 99.7% of their asking
price, says real-estate agent Wendy Banner -- well above the more typical national level of 90% to 95%.
Northeast
Montclair, N.J. 07042
ONE-YEAR APPRECIATION: 18.6%
FIVE-YEAR APPRECIATION: 104.3%
SIDEWALK VIEW: Small-town English shire, with commuters
Long considered the poor cousin of tonier New Jersey suburbs such as nearby Short Hills, Montclair is now
one of the fastest-appreciating places in the state -- with a median home price of $410,000 in this ZIP code.
Brokers say one big draw is its magnet schools: Residents can choose to send their children to any of the
town's schools, which specialize in subjects like international studies and science. Last year's addition of a
direct-line train to midtown Manhattan also boosted housing demand.

--- Page 26 ---
Page 26 of 265 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Despite New York's proximity and some famous residents -- Hall of Fame Yankee catcher Yogi Berra,
cosmetics-company founder Bobbi Brown -- Montclair's tone is small-town retro, with wide sidewalks,
streetlights and stores like the Soda Pop Shop. Come spring, shop owners compete to create the prettiest
flower basket.
Since Montclair's boom has been relatively recent, many of its dated houses need considerable work. Sybil
Eng, an epidemiologist who commutes to New York, paid $811,000 for a six-bedroom, 1927 colonial last
summer, even though it needed renovations -- starting with the cracked bathroom tiles, white linoleum kitchen
floors and oven too small for a Thanksgiving turkey.
West
Capistrano Beach, Calif. 92624
ONE-YEAR APPRECIATION: 39.2%
FIVE-YEAR APPRECIATION: 161.2%
SIDEWALK VIEW: Luxury living creeps into low-key town
As the old song suggests, every spring the swallows return to Capistrano. Homeowners have flocked here for
a different reason: While the 7,445-population town has only a handful of businesses and few local jobs, the
beachfront area has become increasingly desirable as prices have grown in better-known neighboring towns.
One of the most expensive homes in Orange County is next door in Monarch Beach, a $30 million mansion
owned by Stephen Udvar-Hazy, founder of International Lease Finance Corp. A few miles to the north in
picturesque Laguna Niguel, six properties are currently for sale for more than $10 million. But an oceanfront
home in Capistrano Beach recently sold for $6 million. "Around here, that's relatively affordable," says
real-estate agent Joe Smith.
In downtown "Capo Beach," as the locals call it, the dominant business is the thatched Coconuts restaurant,
and many of the homes are custom-made, with touches like navy blue tile trim, leaded windows or widow's
walks. Even with the price spikes, most homes sell swiftly, agents say. But there's some evidence that the
speculative froth may be subsiding. Contractor Larry Sackett, who says he "flipped" about 43 houses here
during Southern California's boom of 1988-1993, says he isn't buying anything now. "I think the market is past
its peak," he says. "I don't want to get burned."

Dollar Is Showing Life, Helping U.S. Bonds
Tuesday's Markets
Dollar Is Showing Life, Helping U.S. Bonds
By E.S. Browning
544 words
9 February 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
STOCKS REBOUNDED from Monday's mild declines, but investors were surprised after regular trading hours
by disappointing quarterly results from networking-equipment maker Cisco Systems.
The dollar rose sharply against the yen. But after hitting a three-month high against the euro on Monday, it
inched down against the European currency. The dollar had been at a six-year low against the euro on Dec.
30, and since has rebounded almost 7%. It has rebounded 3.7% against the yen, from a five-year low hit in
mid-January.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average yesterday edged up 8.87 points, or 0.08%, to 10724.63, down just 0.5%
for 2005.
Volatile computer-chip stocks led the market, jumping more than 2% amid hopes that the chip companies are
working through excess inventories. The Nasdaq Composite Index, laden with technology stocks, rose
0.22%, or 4.65 points, to 2086.68, still down 4% this year.
"Generally the sentiment has been positive, because we are seeing 67% of companies meeting or beating
earnings estimates," said Som Dasgupta, head of stock trading at PNC Financial Services in Pittsburgh.
But Cisco's fourth-quarter revenue missed analyst expectations, and profit fell short of the unofficial "whisper"
numbers circulating among investors. That stock fell 20 cents to $18.04 in after-hours trading.
The dollar's rebound has been a mixed blessing for stocks. Big multinational companies, whose stocks have
been among the recent gainers, benefit from a weak dollar by making their exports cheaper and their foreign
revenues more valuable in dollar terms. A rising dollar reverses that process.
But at the same time, the relentless dollar decline had been sparking broader worries about market stability
and about longer-term foreign interest in owning U.S. securities. So the recent stabilization has been a relief.
The stronger dollar also helped Treasury bond prices, which rallied yesterday. Rising bond prices have
pushed down yields, helping to keep market interest rates low. That has supported stocks by quieting fears
of rising interest rates and rising inflation. Bond yields help determine interest rates on many fixed-rate
mortgages, and low mortgage rates encourage housing sales and consumer spending.
Oil reversed its recent slide, rising 12 cents to $45.40 on the New York Mercantile Exchange. Gold declined a
bit.
The broad Standard &Poor's 500-stock index rose 0.05%, or 0.58 point, to 1202.30, down 0.8% on the year.
Outside the U.S., stocks declined in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
fell 0.51%, or 0.88 point, to 170.29.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks advanced. On the Big Board, where 1.41 billion shares traded, 1,823 stocks rose and 1,463 fell.
Bonds gained. The 10-year Treasury note rose 7/32, or $2.1875 for each $1,000 invested, pushing the yield
down to 4.031%. The 30-year bond was up 24/32 to yield 4.385%.
The dollar was mixed. Late in New York, it traded at 105.79 yen, up from 104.85, while the euro rose against
the dollar to $1.2767 from $1.2765.

World Watch

1,174 words

The Wall Street Journal

EUROPE
Britain Proposes Tighter Rules on Immigration
LONDON -- The British government proposed tighter immigration controls and said only skilled workers who
speak English would be allowed to settle in Britain permanently.
Home Secretary Charles Clarke said yesterday the government also would fingerprint all foreigners applying
for visas to stop them from remaining in Britain once their permits expire.
The measures, which wouldn't affect citizens of the European Union, are part of a Europe-wide drive to tackle
illegal immigration -- an issue particularly sensitive for Prime Minister Tony Blair's government as it gears up
for national elections expected in May.
"This country needs migration. Tourists, students and migrant workers make a vital contribution to the U.K.
economy. But we need to ensure that we let in migrants with the skills and talents to benefit Britain, while
stopping those trying to abuse our hospitality and place a burden on our society," Mr. Clarke said.
Announcing a five-year plan for immigration and asylum to the House of Commons, Mr. Clarke said the
government would introduce a points system -- already in place in Australia and Canada -- favoring those
with sought-after skills, such as doctors and engineers.
The government will limit the number of dependents who can join a migrant worker in Britain and scrap the
automatic right to permanent residency for people who have lived in Britain for four years.
Under the proposals, only skilled workers who can support themselves financially can apply to stay
permanently. They must have lived in Britain for five years and be able to speak and write in the English
language.
The European Union justice and interior ministers hope to agree on common immigration and asylum rules by
2010.
-- Associated Press
---
ASIA/PACIFIC
Markets Respond Positively to Thai Election
BANGKOK, Thailand -- Credit-ratings agencies and capital markets welcomed the strengthened mandate
Prime Minister Thaksin Shinawatra received from Sunday's parliamentary elections, even as some analysts
expressed concern over potential abuse of power by an unprecedented single-party government here.
The Stock Exchange of Thailand index rose to its highest level since the beginning of the year early
yesterday, and the baht hit a nearly-five year high, as investors gambled that the government will use the
momentum from its massive victory to push forward long-delayed restructuring.
With more than 60% of the vote counted, Mr. Thaksin's Thai Rak Party looked set to win 374 of 500 seats in
the parliament, while the Democrat opposition party was a distant second with 94 seats, according to
unofficial results. Official results might not be available until later in the week. But if initial estimates are
confirmed, Mr. Thaksin, a former telecommunications tycoon, will have enough votes to form Thailand's first

--- Page 31 ---
Page 31 of 265 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
single-party government, block any censure motions against him by the opposition and get constitutional
amendments passed.
"There is a strong, stable, democratically elected government that won't be hamstrung by the need to build a
coalition or keep the opposition in check -- a position few governments in Asia are in," said Brian Coulton,
Fitch Ratings' Hong Kong-based head of Asian sovereign ratings.
Mr. Thaksin said his policies over the next four years will focus on the "supply side" rather than on demand,
which had been a priority in his first term. He indicated he will push ahead with privatizations in the electricity
and telecommunications sectors as well as with plans to upgrade the country's infrastructure.
Some analysts warned that Mr. Thaksin could use his mandate to amend the constitution and further
entrench his powers, or to exercise control over the country's central bank. But, said Fitch's Mr. Coulton, "If
there is a trade-off between short-term stability and long-term accountability, it won't be positive."
-- Dow Jones Newswires
China's Overseas Direct Investment Rises 27%
BEIJING -- Direct investment overseas by China rose 27% to $3.62 billion in 2004 from a year earlier, the
Ministry of Commerce said, highlighting the country's growing influence abroad.
The increase, though small compared with the $60 billion of foreign direct investment China received last
year, represents a significant shift in government policy.
After years of trying to keep money at home, the government now is trying to encourage companies to seek
opportunities abroad. The new policy in part aims to deflect criticism abroad of China's swelling
foreign-currency reserves and to secure China's access to natural resources.
China's contracted investment overseas, a leading indicator of money in the pipeline but not yet spent, rose
78% to $3.71 billion.
About 69% of the overseas direct investment by China in 2004, or $2.51 billion, was spent acquiring equity in
overseas companies. Reinvestment of profit from continuing concerns accounted for the remaining $1.11
billion.
China has been encouraging many of its big companies to look abroad for investment opportunities to help
offset the continued growth of foreign direct investment. Companies have particularly targeted the mining
sector to help reduce China's dependence on overseas companies to supply growing demand by its steel and
other refineries.
Some 53% of the total actual overseas direct investment was spent in the mining sector, spokesman Chong
Quan said in the Ministry of Commerce statement. A further 26.5% of the total was spent in the business and
services sector and 13.5% in the manufacturing sector, he said.
-- Dow Jones Newswires
---
THE AMERICAS
Venezuela to Review Foreign Investment Contracts
CARACAS, Venezuela -- Venezuela will review all foreign investments in its nonoil basic industries in a
nationalist drive to maximize technological and development benefits for the country, the sector's minister
said.
Basic Industries Minister Victor Alvarez said President Hugo Chavez ordered that investments should be
more closely aligned with his self-styled "revolutionary" social-development agenda in mineral-rich
Venezuela.
Mr. Alvarez, appointed last month, said the government would seek an increased transfer of technology,
technical training and assistance, and national content from projects with international partners.
"We are defending our national sovereignty over the use of our national resources, which must serve the
endogenous development of the nation," he said.
He made clear the planned review includes aluminum, gold and iron-ore mining investment contracts, as well
as multimillion-dollar projects under discussion with foreign partners to expand production in these areas.

--- Page 32 ---
Page 32 of 265 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The Chavez government has always assured investors in Venezuela's mining-and-metals sector that it
respects existing contracts. But Mr. Alvarez said Venezuela from now on would be seeking commitments to
transfer new technology, generate more jobs and create local downstream manufacturing industries.
Similar government reviews of foreign investments in Venezuela's oil sector have caused jitters in energy and
financial markets in recent weeks.
The minister said he isn't worried that Venezuela's campaign to extract more national benefit from foreign
investments might frighten off investors. "No, we're practically having to fight off interested investors from
Russia, China, Iran, India, Japan, the European Union, Brazil, Argentina, the United States, Canada, Italy and
Spain," he said.
-- Reuters

G-7 Endorses Forgiveness of Debt For Poor Nations, Differs on How
The Economy
G-7 Endorses Forgiveness of Debt For Poor Nations, Differs on How
By Michael M. Phillips
564 words
7 February 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
LONDON -- The world's richest nations signaled a willingness to forgive billions of dollars in
developing-country debt, but left unresolved vexing questions about how to pay for their good intentions.
Top economic officials from the Group of Seven major industrialized nations for the first time jointly promised
as much as 100% cancellation of debts that 27 very poor countries owe the World Bank, International
Monetary Fund and other international lenders.
"It's certainly progress in the sense that G-7 countries are lining up to point in the same direction. Have we
come to agreement on what is the best way to do it? Obviously not," a senior G-7 official said.
The failure to lay out a clear path toward debt relief reflects a continuing split in the G-7. Britain, in particular,
is pressing for G-7 states to cover the cost of paying off developing nations' debts to the World Bank, which
are valued at about $20 billion. Britain is already paying 10% of interest payments for Tanzania and
Mozambique. Britain also wants the IMF to tap its $43 billion gold reserve to finance the write-off of its loans
outstanding to the poorest nations, which are valued at around $9 billion. The U.S. wants the World Bank to
cover the cost of its own debt relief and is dubious about the need to dig into IMF gold reserves.
Neither side showed signs of moving far from the position it brought into the meeting of finance ministers and
central-bank governors here over the weekend. Instead, the officials said they would entertain debt-relief
funding proposals at their next gathering, in Washington in April. At the same time, the IMF is expected to
present a list of options for paying for cancellation of its loans, including ways in which it could draw on its
gold stock of 103.4 million ounces without roiling gold markets.
Despite the discord, British Chancellor of the Exchequer Gordon Brown portrayed the meeting as a landmark
in the road toward a debt-relief policy that would free up money for developing countries to spend on health,
education and other basic needs, instead of loan payments. "It is the richest countries hearing the voices of
the poor," said Mr. Brown, who has staked his political ambitions on the effort to steer fresh aid to Africa and
elsewhere.
Antipoverty activists welcomed the G-7's general endorsement of debt cancellation, but underscored the need
for the groups to arrive at final agreements at the April meeting and at the leaders' summit that President
Bush, British Prime Minister Tony Blair and others are planning to attend in Scotland in July.
Separately, the G-7 agreed to suspend until the end of the year collecting loan payments from countries
affected by the Indian Ocean tsunami, extending an earlier, short-term debt moratorium.
The G-7 ministers remained divided over how best to increase overall development aid for poor countries. Mr.
Brown has called for a "Marshall Plan" for Africa to double foreign-aid flows. Supported by Germany and
France, Britain pushed for its own plan in which countries would commit future aid spending to back the
issuance of development bonds now. The bond revenue would be spent on aid immediately.

Dollar Gains on Counterparts Ahead of Possibly Key Session
Currency Trading
Dollar Gains on Counterparts Ahead of Possibly Key Session
By Laurence Norman Dow Jones Newswires
403 words
4 February 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C5
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The dollar gained ground across the board, snapping out of days of thin and listless trading ahead of what
promises to be a key session for the foreign-exchange markets.
The dollar touched a two-week high versus the euro and a three-week high against the yen during New York
trading, as weak fourth-quarter productivity raised inflation concerns and a fall in gold prices boosted the
U.S. currency. The dollar was further supported by comments from European Central Bank officials who said
inflation could fall below 2% in coming months. As expected, the ECB kept rates steady at 2%.
The burst of life in currency markets came ahead of what could be a very busy day. The Labor Department
will report nonfarm payrolls data early today in New York trading. The consensus is for an increase of
200,000, though many economists say the risk lies very much on the upside.
The same day, finance ministers and central bankers from the Group of Seven industrialized nations will start
a two-day meeting in London. The Governor of the People's Bank of China Zhou Xiaochuan and Federal
Reserve Chairman Alan Greenspan will also speak at a conference in London before the G-7 meeting opens.
In late New York trading, the euro was at $1.2975, from $1.3035 late Wednesday. The common currency
earlier fell to $1.2941, its lowest level since Jan 21. The dollar also lifted to 104.45 yen, from 103.71 yen and
1.2021 Swiss francs from 1.1920 francs. The pound slipped to $1.8825 from $1.8861, and the euro was at
135.46 yen from 135.20 yen.
Brazil's real settled at a new 32-month high as expectations that the dollar will remain weak proved more
influential than offers by the central bank to buy U.S. currency in the spot market and accept dollar-linked
bonds in exchange for bonds tied to the base interest rate, or Selic. The real ended at 2.603 reals to the
dollar in Brazil's markets, its strongest close since June 2002 and the latest in a string of fresh 2 1/2-year
highs. On Wednesday the currency had finished at 2.615 reals to the dollar.
---
Andrea Walsh in Sao Paolo contributed to this article.

Markets Are Relieved As Fed Rate Move Contains No Surprise
Wednesday's Markets
Markets Are Relieved As Fed Rate Move Contains No Surprise
By E.S. Browning
624 words
3 February 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
STOCKS MARCHED ahead to a third consecutive session of gains as investors breathed a sigh of relief
following the Federal Reserve interest-rate decision.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average rose another 44.85 points, or 0.43%, to 10596.79. It still is down 186.22
points, or 1.7%, since the year began, but it has recovered 169.59 points in the past three trading days.
Investors did get some bad news after regular trading hours, as Amazon.com announced disappointing
quarterly profit. Its stock fell 15% in after-hours trading.
As expected, the Fed raised its target rate for overnight bank lending by another quarter point, to 2.5%, its
sixth such move since June. Investors already are predicting a similar rate increase when policy makers meet
next month.
What the markets feared was the Fed's statement that accompanies its rate decision. Minutes of the previous
Fed meeting, in December, indicated that some Fed decision makers were worrying about incipient inflation.
Investors feared this could be reflected in the Fed statement, suggesting that the central bank was going to
fight inflation by raising interest rates more rapidly. That would throw a wet blanket on the economy, hurting
stocks and bonds.
To the market's relief, the Fed's statement yesterday was almost identical to others it has released recently. It
said that "underlying inflation" is "expected to be relatively low," and indicated that Fed interest-rate
increases can continue to be "measured."
"It was a carbon copy of past statements, and there was a very muted reaction," said David Briggs, head of
stock trading at Pittsburgh mutual-fund group Federated Investors. "The good news is that the economy is
growing rapidly and inflation doesn't seem to be a big problem."
But, reflecting widespread hesitancy among professional investors now, he said he wants to see more than a
three-day rally before he is persuaded that the January slump is over.
Oil prices fell again, giving stocks additional support. Gold and the dollar both rose.
After the Fed announcement, a variety of banking companies, including J.P. Morgan Chase, Bank of America
and Wachovia, quickly raised their prime lending rates, which are the base rates for many corporate and
consumer loans, to 5.5% from 5.25%. (The Wall Street Journal's official published rate also changed to 5.5%,
effective with today's Money Rates table.)
But Treasury-bond yields were little changed after the Fed announcement, because the news was widely
anticipated. That means that rates for loans pegged to Treasury yields, including many fixed-rate mortgages,
would be little affected.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index rose 0.32%, or 3.78 points, to 1193.19, down 1.6% this year. The
Nasdaq Composite Index rose 0.31%, or 6.36 points, to 2075.06, down 4.6% for 2005.
Investors now will be biting their nails over the January employment report, due tomorrow, which is widely
followed as an indicator of the economy's strength.
Outside the U.S., stocks advanced in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
rose 0.51%, or 0.87 point, to 171.17.
In major U.S. market action:

--- Page 45 ---
Page 45 of 265 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Bonds were mixed and stocks advanced. The 10-year Treasury note fell 2/32, or 62.5 cents for each $1,000
invested, pushing the yield up to 4.145%. The 30-year bond was up 9/32 to yield 4.581%.
The dollar strengthened. Late in New York, it traded at 103.71 yen, up from 103.67, while the euro fell against
the dollar to $1.3035 from $1.3046.
