Poorest Nations May Get Debt Relief
International
Poorest Nations May Get Debt Relief
By Michael M. Phillips
588 words
2 February 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
A13
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
WASHINGTON -- The U.S., Britain and other wealthy nations are inching toward an agreement to write off
billions of dollars of debts owed by the world's poorest countries.
With antipoverty groups ratcheting up pressure, top economic officials from the Group of Seven major
industrialized nations are expected to try to hammer out the details at a meeting in London this weekend.
"There will be progress" toward an accord on debt relief, predicted one European official.
British Chancellor of the Exchequer Gordon Brown, who holds the rotating chairmanship of the G-7 finance
ministers' group, is pressing for a quick resolution that would relieve the worst-off nations of outstanding debts
owed to the World Bank and International Monetary Fund. Both institutions provide loans to developing
nations, many of which struggle to repay them. Antipoverty activists say the debt cancellation, which would go
far beyond previous efforts, is essential if poor countries are to free up resources for education, health, water
and other basic needs.
"We're hopeful this can be a place where there is concrete action instead of just the rhetoric you usually see
coming out of meetings of the G-7 finance ministers," said Neil Watkins, national coordinator of Jubilee USA
Network, a debt-relief advocacy group.
The G-7 governments remain split over how to finance the write-off, and a final accord may have to wait until
President Bush and the other G-7 leaders meet in Scotland in July. The G-7 consists of the U.S., Britain,
France, Germany, Japan, Italy and Canada.
There appears to be consensus among the G-7 that the IMF could fund a write-off of its loans to the poorest
nations -- currently valued at about $9 billion -- by tapping its huge gold reserves. The IMF holds 103.4
million ounces of gold, valued at about $43 billion at market prices. To avoid upending gold markets, the IMF
would likely arrange private sales to some of its 184 member nations or perform complex accounting
maneuvers to direct the reserve to debt relief. Or it could spend the $4 billion left over from a smaller
debt-relief initiative funded through its gold resources in 1999-2000.
The trickier issue is how the World Bank could write off loans to the poorest countries. The World Bank
borrows on global markets at very low interest rates and lends to developing nations at slightly higher rates.
If it wrote off big loans, the bank's credit rating might suffer and the bank might find itself having to pay more
to borrow and having to charge more to lend.
Britain is proposing that rich nations use their own money to repay World Bank loans on behalf of the
borrowers. That would keep money flowing back into the World Bank and allow it to maintain new-lending
levels.
The Bush administration, facing record budget deficits, wants the World Bank to forgive existing debts and
reduce the amount it lends in the future. The U.S., represented by Treasury Secretary John Snow, also wants
the bank to increase the amount of aid it provides in the form of grants, instead of loans, so countries don't
slip back into unmanageable arrears. The U.S. position troubles debt-relief activists, who fear that it would
drain World Bank resources.
"There are other proposals out there," said John Taylor, Treasury's top international official. "This is the most
doable one, and the most dramatic."

Business and Finance
Business and Finance
What's News
Business and Finance
502 words
27 January 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J

Wall Street Advice: Get Heavy on Metal --- Firms Push Copper, Nickel and Silver as Demand Rises, but the Easy Money Has Been Made
Wall Street Advice: Get Heavy on Metal --- Firms Push Copper, Nickel and Silver as Demand Rises, but
the Easy Money Has Been Made
By Jeff D. Opdyke and Jane J. Kim
1,619 words
26 January 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
D1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
FORGET PLASTICS. The word on Wall Street these days is metals.
Amid the voracious global demand for precious and industrial metals, the investment industry is creating a
host of new vehicles designed to profit from them. Some strategists are also recommending that individual
investors move a portion of their assets into metals.
Last week, Merrill Lynch &Co. resurrected its Metals &Mining Weekly research report after three years of
silence. Earlier this month, Byron Wien, Morgan Stanley's senior investment strategist, said that silver, now at
$6.71 an ounce, has the potential to trade above $10 this year; gold, he said, may rise above $500. (It's
currently trading at $422.) A new gold exchange-traded fund, or ETF, launched last year to track movements
in that precious metal, and the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange, where metals trading
occurs, is soon to release its own ETFs that will act as metal index funds.
The flurry of activity comes in response to rising global demand for precious metals, such as gold and
platinum, and base metals, such as copper and nickel. Economies depend on them for everything from
stainless steel (made with nickel) to electrical switches (made with silver) to new homes (loaded with copper
wiring). Depressed metals prices in prior years, though, meant that mining companies didn't invest in
increasing supplies. As such, nickel demand, for instance, has outstripped supply for the last four years, and
is expected to continue doing so for another year or two.
Deutsche Bank AG last year began encouraging some investors to include in their asset allocation decisions
a 3% stake in commodities, including metals. Morgan Stanley's Individual Investor Group also recommends
investors increase their short-term position in alternative investments, which includes, among others, metals
and managed futures funds, in which a manager actively trades commodity and financial futures, including
metals contracts.
In many cases, Wall Street's interest in metals is coming after the easy money already has been made.
Metals prices have moved higher, and many metals-related stocks have soared recently. As copper prices
essentially doubled during the past two years, shares of Phelps Dodge Corp., the Phoenix-based copper
giant, have tripled.
Nickel in December averaged $6.30 a pound, up from $1.89 a pound in 1998. That's the highest price the
metal has seen in 15 years. Silver is up about 40% for the past two years, and gold is up more than 50%
since Sept. 11, 2001.
But some are betting that the bull market in metal prices isn't over. For one thing, China's growth continues to
absorb vast amounts of the world's metal production, particularly steel, copper and aluminum.
There are several ways for individuals to invest in metals, including stocks, mutual funds and ETFs, direct
and indirect ownership of the metal, or futures contracts.
The most obvious route is buying stocks of companies that mine for and produce various metals. This is often
a leveraged play, since a small price movement in, say, copper is magnified on a company's earnings line.
While many metals and mining stocks have roared ahead, some are just now primed to move, such as
aluminum stocks, which have spent the past year generally flat to down. Aluminum tends to move later in a
metals-price recovery, and as such, Daniel Roling, the senior metals and mining analyst at Merrill Lynch,
predicts that 2005 "will be the year for aluminum."
He is particularly bullish about Alcoa Inc., Alcan Inc. and Century Aluminum Co., expecting aluminum prices
will move toward 90 cents a pound from 78 cents a pound in 2004 and fatten these companies' bottom lines.

--- Page 52 ---
Page 52 of 265 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Zinc, which hit a seven-year high last week, will also help companies such as Lundin Mining Corp., which
trades on the Toronto Stock Exchange, given China's demand for the metal and the lack of zinc mining
production on the horizon. Investors looking for pure silver plays should consider Silver Wheaton Corp., a unit
of Wheaton River Minerals Ltd. that also trades on the Toronto Stock Exchange, and Pan American Silver
Corp., says Frank Holmes, chief investment officer of U.S. Global Investors Inc., a San Antonio firm that runs
natural-resources mutual funds. He also likes FNX Mining Co. for exposure to nickel and Anooraq Resources
Corp. as a way to play the growing demand for platinum.
With stocks, though, investors take on corporate risks that can send share prices slumping even as metals
prices surge. Moreover, some stocks have already been played out for now. Bear Stearns Cos. in December
cut its ratings on shares of copper and nickel producers like Phelps Dodge, Freeport-McMoRan Copper
&Gold Inc., Inco Ltd. and Falconbridge Ltd., a unit of Toronto mining company Noranda Inc. The firm cited
valuations and the likelihood that 2005 will be the peak year for the underlying commodities.
Various precious-metals mutual funds exist, though many focus largely on gold, which tends to act more like
a currency, while other metals are much more industrial. One exception: the Vanguard Precious Metals and
Mining Fund. It reopened its doors last year and broadened its strategy to include metals such as platinum,
nickel and copper. The fund gained 8% last year, according to Chicago investment research firm Morningstar.
The World Gold Council in November launched the streetTRACKS Gold Shares, an ETF in which investors
own shares backed by actual gold. Each share, listed on the New York Stock Exchange under the symbol
GLD, represents one-tenth of an ounce of bullion. Barclays Global Investors is working on its own version of a
gold ETF, and the Nymex says it will very soon unveil its own metals-tracking ETFs. (ETFs resemble
index-tracking mutual funds, but trade on exchanges like stocks.)
Owning the metal directly is another option. Investors can buy gold, silver, platinum and palladium ingots
from precious-metals dealers and online trading firms, such as www.kitco.com , www.apmex.com and
www.bulliondirect.com . Sizes generally range from less than an ounce to 1,000-ounce bricks. Prices are
based on whatever the so-called spot price is at the moment, plus a markup of between 2% and 15% or
more, depending on the size of the trade.
Kitco.com also offers pool accounts, in which investors own a nonspecific portion of a large pool of precious
metals held by Kitco. Pools are available in the four key major precious metals as well as rhodium. The
benefit: Prices per ounce are much closer to spot metal prices. With silver recently at $6.69 an ounce, Kitco
offered an ounce of pooled silver for $6.79, while other dealers were selling one-ounce bars for as much as
$7.92.
Finally, there are futures contracts, which obligate a buyer or seller to purchase or deliver a set amount of
some commodity at a pre-established price on a specific date in the future. Metals contracts trade on the
Nymex, the London Metals Exchange and Chicago Board of Trade.
Investors use these as speculative tools to bet on a rise or fall in a metal's price. Futures allows investors to
own large sizes of a particular metal for a relatively small investment. With gold, for instance, investors can
control a 100-ounce contract -- roughly worth an underlying $42,230 -- for a minimal $2,025.
The leverage means the potential for huge profits if gold prices move higher, since every $1 gain in the price
of gold translates into a $100 change in the contract's value. Of course, it also means that if gold tumbles,
you can lose your entire investment and possibly more.
---
Metals Choices
Some ways to gain exposure to metals:
-- Stocks: Owning companies that mine for and produce various metals.
-- Mutual funds and exchange-traded funds: Most focus on
gold, which tends
to be influenced by currency and geopolitical issues.
-- Direct ownership: Precious- metals dealers and online trading firms sell
silver, gold, platinum and palladium.
-- Futures contracts: Potentially the riskiest -- and most lucrative -- way
to play metals.

Page 53 of 265 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.

What's Ahead for Metals
Many metals and mining stocks have already had a strong run. Here's the
outlook for some key metals.
METAL: Aluminum
OUTLOOK: Still room for appreciation, as prices tend to rise later in a
metals recovery. Will benefit from tight supply and strong demand from China.
POTENTIAL WINNERS: Alcoa Inc., Alcan Inc. and Century Aluminum Co.
METAL: Copper
OUTLOOK: Prices are expected to remain firm in the first half of 2005,
although additional production could boost supply next year.
POTENTIAL WINNERS: Phelps Dodge Corp., Freeport-McMoRan Copper &
Gold Inc.
METAL: Gold
OUTLOOK: Given its role as an inflation hedge and a store of value, the
outlook for a return of inflation may benefit the precious metal, as will a
weak dollar.
POTENTIAL WINNERS: Precious-metals mutual funds, such as American Century
Global Gold, or an exchange-traded fund such as streetTracks Gold Shares.
METAL: Nickel
OUTLOOK: Although prices are likely to peak this year, demand should still
continue to outstrip supply over the next year or two.
POTENTIAL WINNERS: Inco Ltd., Falconbridge Ltd., a unit of Noranda Inc. and
FNX Mining Co.
METAL: Silver/Platinum/Palladium
OUTLOOK: New uses for silver will help bolster demand, while China needs
platinum and palladium to develop clean-air technologies.
POTENTIAL WINNERS: SilverWheaton Corp., a unit of Wheaton River Minerals
Ltd., Pan American Silver Corp. and Anooraq Resources Corp.

Gold Falls $5, Losing Its Luster As Dollar Shines Against Rivals
Commodities Report
Gold Falls $5, Losing Its Luster As Dollar Shines Against Rivals
By Gavin Maguire Dow Jones Newswires
551 words
26 January 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C8
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Gold futures dropped $5 per ounce on heavy speculative liquidation of long positions, sparked by the steep
climb in the U.S. dollar against its major rivals in the foreign-exchange market.
The front-month February contract settled $5 lower at $422.10 an ounce on the Comex division of the New
York Mercantile Exchange.
A good portion of gold's decline came overnight after the U.S. currency pushed higher against the euro, to
mute demand for dollar-alternatives such as gold.
February futures duly opened on the back foot and nearly $2 lower, and continued to face selling pressure
through the opening two hours of the New York session.
A midmorning pickup in the greenback's rate of ascent introduced more sellers to gold and dunked prices
from around $424.50 to $421.50 by noon, before a light spurt of buying of previously established short
positions edged levels back above $422 by the close.
Market watchers are divided over what to expect next from the yellow metal.
Leonard Kaplan, president of money-management firm Prospector Asset Management, argued that further
losses in gold should be expected in the days and weeks ahead as the U.S. dollar continues to nose higher
on the prospect of higher U.S. interest rates.
"U.S. interest rates are definitely rising, and that will support the dollar and therefore hurt gold. The funds
are liquidating on that, and the rest of the market is also selling on the realization of that fact," he argued.
Mr. Kaplan said a slide in Comex February prices to the $405-to-$410 area could be seen in the next three to
four weeks.
Larry Young, senior trader at Infinity Brokerage in Chicago, had a much more bullish view and expected a
strong rebound in gold prices over the coming year as geopolitical uncertainty steered investors toward safe
havens like gold in increasing numbers.
"We're just so closely attuned to the euro-dollar [exchange rate] right now that we're moving in lockstep with
the currency markets before anything else," he said.
"But in time I think there's going to be a lot of chaos and mayhem in this world, like the violence in Iraq as it
tries to have elections, which will be good for gold and other flight-to-quality products," he said.
Mr. Young said that while further price slides could be seen over the near term, they should be viewed as
buying opportunities by players seeking to amass greater exposure to the market ahead of his expected
forthcoming price rise.
In other commodity markets:
CRUDE OIL: Futures prices on the New York Mercantile Exchange rose amid reports of refinery troubles in
the U.S. and the ongoing threat of an oil workers' strike in Nigeria. Trader uncertainty ahead of key U.S.
petroleum weekly inventory reports on Wednesday also supported futures prices. Crude oil for March delivery
rose $0.83 a barrel to $49.64
UNLEADED GAS: Futures prices on the New York Mercantile Exchange rose on news reports of a fire
shutting down a ConocoPhillips gasoline refinery in Louisiana. Gasoline for February delivery closed up 4.55
cents a gallon, at $1.3445.

Lesson in Values: As Dollar Weakens, Hidden Strengths May Stave Off Crisis --- 'Twin Deficits' Would Sink Other Currencies, but U.S. Is...
Lesson in Values: As Dollar Weakens, Hidden Strengths May Stave Off Crisis --- 'Twin Deficits' Would
Sink Other Currencies, but U.S. Is Clearly a Special Case --- Brazil Takes Tough Medicine
By Greg Ip
2,202 words
18 January 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Corrections &Amplifications
THE U.S. TREASURY and Federal Reserve on March 2, 1995, intervened in currency markets to support the
dollar, selling deutsche marks and yen valued at $600 million to buy dollars. A page-one article yesterday
incorrectly said the Treasury and Fed bought $600 million of marks and yen. In addition, the U.S. federal
budget deficit in fiscal 2004 was 3.6% of gross domestic product. The article incorrectly said the deficit was
4.5% of GDP.
(WSJ Jan. 19, 2005)
(END)
Up to a point, a falling currency is a blessing. After that, it's a curse.
The dollar has fallen 16% against a basket of its trading partners' currencies over the past three years. In
theory, that should, with time, make U.S.-made goods more competitive with those made abroad, boosting
U.S. growth and employment.
But a growing chorus warns that the U.S.'s gaping budget and trade deficits will lead to a crisis in which the
dollar falls much more sharply, driving up interest rates and squeezing the economy.
There are plenty of troubling precedents. Over the past decade, a dozen smaller economies from Mexico to
Thailand have gone from growth to deep recession when their currencies collapsed. Even rich countries like
Canada have been forced to adopt austere budget policies to cope with currency-induced turmoil. "We are
increasingly vulnerable to the kind of sudden stop, where the capital inflows dry up all at once, that's been the
bane of emerging markets over the years," says Barry Eichengreen, an economic historian at the University
of California at Berkeley.
Could it happen here? It certainly hasn't yet. In a crisis, foreign investors dump stocks and bonds, fearing
depreciation will cause further losses. Yet U.S. Treasury bond prices, and thus long-term interest rates that
move in the opposite direction, have changed little in the last year -- and stocks are higher. A review of past
crises world-wide suggests the U.S. has enough going for it now to avoid a similar fate. Yet the magnitude of
the imbalances hanging over the dollar is also without precedent, suggesting a crisis remains possible.
The contrasts with the last dollar crisis, in the late 1970s, are both encouraging and unsettling.
Between September 1977 and October 1978, the dollar fell 16% against major currencies, slammed by rising
oil prices and sluggish economic growth that pushed the U.S. from a trade surplus to a trade deficit. The
weaker dollar helped boost the price of imports, and inflation jumped to over 8% from 6%.
Worried the falling dollar was undermining its anti-inflation efforts, the Carter administration announced a
multipart support package on Nov. 1, 1978: The Treasury would use gold sales and foreign borrowing and
draw on its reserves with the International Monetary Fund to defend the dollar. At the same time, the Federal
Reserve raised its discount rate a full percentage point.
The dollar stabilized but remained weak, and inflation rose to 10% by late 1979. In the summer of 1979, Mr.
Carter took what turned out to be a more substantial step. He installed Paul Volcker, then president of the
New York Federal Reserve Bank, as Fed chairman. Mr. Volcker concluded that dramatic action was needed
to break the back of inflation.
On Oct. 6, the Fed adopted an unorthodox new monetary policy for strangling inflation by clamping down
on growth in the money supply, knowing that would send interest rates soaring. Mr. Volcker's new strategy

--- Page 61 ---
Page 61 of 265 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
did bring down inflation and eventually bolstered the dollar, but the price was high: the deepest recession
since the Great Depression.
A lot has changed since 1978. At the time, the falling dollar was both a cause and consequence of high
inflation, which corroded its purchasing power at home and abroad. Today, inflation is much lower and
investors believe the Fed will keep it that way. While the falling dollar and high oil prices have boosted
inflation a bit lately, investors believe that will be temporary. Ten-year Treasury-bond yields remain near
40-year lows of 4%, compared with 9% in 1979. The Fed thus believes it can gradually raise its short-term
interest-rate target, still very low at 2.25%, without slowing the economy in the process.
But some things are worse. In 1978, the U.S. current account deficit -- the balance on goods and services
trade plus the balance on investment income between the U.S. and its trading partners -- was less than 1% of
gross domestic product. It is now approaching 6%. That deficit represents the extent to which U.S.
households, corporations and governments spend and invest more than they earn. Compared with 27 years
ago, U.S. households save far less of their after-tax income, and thus must borrow more to finance their
housing and consumption. The federal government also borrows more; its budget deficit was 2.7% of GDP in
1978 but was 4.5% last year.
Just as an individual who spends more than he earns must borrow or sell some of his assets to pay his bills,
the U.S. finances its current-account deficit by either borrowing or attracting foreign investment in its
businesses and stock market. Lately, it has borrowed heavily by selling Treasury bonds and other IOUs, often
to foreign central banks. As a result, the U.S. has gone from having net foreign assets equal to 9% of GDP in
1978 to net liabilities equaling about 25% now. Mr. Eichengreen says there is no historical precedent for such
a large economy being so heavily in debt to the rest of the world. Timothy Geithner, current head of the New
York Fed, recently highlighted the risks: "We are significantly more dependent today on the confidence of the
rest of the world in U.S. economic policy."
The rash of crises that struck emerging markets in the last decade demonstrates the consequences of a
sudden and severe loss of investor confidence. Brazil had moderate inflation in 2002 but large
current-account and budget deficits. The rise of a leftist presidential candidate, Luiz Inacio Lula da Silva,
undermined investors' faith that Brazil would keep inflation down and repay its debts. The government
struggled to sell bonds. "All of a sudden, people who thought they had a safe investment started to run," says
Arminio Fraga, then the central-bank governor and now head of his own investment firm, Gavea
Investimentos. Between April and October Brazil's currency, the real, plunged 40%.
Mr. Fraga and his colleagues, after meeting with Mr. da Silva's advisers, assured officials overseas that Mr.
da Silva would pursue "sensible" policies. Still, as the currency plunged, investors were steadily ratcheting up
expectations of inflation. Given Brazil's history of hyperinflation in the late 1980s and early 1990s, Mr. Fraga
knew those expectations had to be squelched. But at what cost?
Mr. Fraga concluded that "a recession was pretty much in the cards. Given the loss of confidence Brazil was
experiencing, we felt: better a managed slowdown with no threat of hyperinflation than a total loss of control
which would have led to a depression." So the bank jacked up its target for overnight interest rates to 25%
from 18% in just three months.
Brazil went through a recession, though it was relatively mild; growth was flat in 2003. By comparison,
Mexico's economy shrank almost 7% after its currency collapsed, Thailand's by 10% and Argentina's by more
than 20%. Economists Nouriel Roubini at New York University and Brad Setser have identified several
common factors in 14 emerging-market crises from Mexico in 1994 to Brazil in 2002. Among them: large
current-account or budget deficits, financing of those deficits with foreign "hot money" that can leave as
quickly as it comes in and political uncertainty.
Mr. Roubini says the U.S. shares several of these vulnerabilities: large current-account and budget deficits --
nicknamed "twin deficits" -- with little prospect of resolution, and a growing portion of those deficits financed
with short-term borrowing from foreigners. "The average emerging economy would have already gone belly
up with these twin deficits," Mr. Roubini says. The U.S. has been spared, he says, because it can still borrow
in dollars instead of foreign currency, meaning others bear the pain if the dollar depreciates. By contrast,
developing countries often borrowed in dollars to take advantage of lower interest rates but faced a crushing
repayment burden when they could no longer keep their currency pegged to the dollar. Brazil's Mr. Fraga
notes fear of government default was a key contributor to its crisis, but that has "zero probability" in the U.S.
"We have some residual credibility," says Mr. Roubini. Nonetheless, U.S. dependence on short-term
borrowing from foreigners "is becoming increasingly dangerous."
Even rich countries can be rocked by a run on their currencies. It happened to Britain in the 1970s. Several
times in the early 1990s, Canada's dollar came under pressure because of big current-account and budget
deficits. The central bank raised interest rates in response, but that worsened the budget deficit by adding

--- Page 62 ---
Page 62 of 265 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
to interest on the public debt. Don Drummond, a top finance-ministry official at the time, remembers being
bombarded by reporters' questions the day Standard &Poor's reduced the credit rating on Canada's
foreign-currency debt. They thought "we were bankrupt internationally. It was a real blow to the Canadian
pride."
Mr. Drummond, now chief economist at TD Bank Financial Group in Toronto, says the Canadian government
used the crisis atmosphere to muster support for sweeping tax measures and spending cuts that eventually
produced budget and current-account surpluses. The U.S. today "spookily" parallels Canada of 10 years ago,
he says, with both current-account and budget deficits about the same size relative to GDP.
Canada never faced a single "crisis" moment at which investors balked at buying Canadian bonds, and even
the S&P downgrade had negligible financial impact. The lesson for the U.S. is not to expect a "catalytic"
event, Mr. Drummond says. "It's more of a drift where you pay more and more of a cost. Nothing changes
dramatically in one day."
The U.S. has an additional advantage over any other country when it comes to crisis prevention: Its economy
is too important for the world to passively accept a dollar collapse.
That's one reason many countries prop up the dollar. China runs a large trade surplus with the U.S.,
something that would normally force its currency, the yuan, to rise against the dollar. To prevent that, China
buys billions of dollars in Treasury securities. That protects its exports and helps keep U.S. interest rates
low.
(MORE)
The increased depth, reach and sophistication of markets is one reason Federal Reserve Chairman Alan
Greenspan is optimistic the U.S. can avoid a crisis. "An ever more flexible international financial system"
means global imbalances are more likely to be "defused with little disruption," he argued in November 2003.
Indeed, as imbalances have grown in the past decade, currency markets, by some measures, have become
more orderly. It's been a decade since the dollar's drop seemed dangerous enough to spark a concerted
response from the U.S. and its allies. On the morning of March 2, 1995, Ted Truman, then top international
staffer at the Fed, was getting reports of massive dollar sales, some triggered by derivatives strategies,
driving the U.S. currency down sharply against the deutsche mark and yen. Bond yields were rising. Mr.
Truman went to see Mr. Greenspan and recommended the Fed and Treasury intervene in the markets to buy
dollars. "I don't think it's going to do any good," Mr. Truman recalls telling Mr. Greenspan. "But by not being
there we are saying we totally don't care what the conditions of the markets are."
Mr. Greenspan agreed, and that afternoon the Fed and the Treasury waded in, buying $600 million of marks
and yen. The next day it repeated the action, joined by 13 central banks. The dollar stabilized. Bond yields
dropped.
The U.S. intervened to support the dollar a few more times that year, but hasn't done so since; markets have
generally been smooth, and the Clinton and Bush administrations came to see intervention as being of limited
use.
Mr. Truman, now a scholar at the Institute for International Economics in Washington, predicts that in the next
five years, the U.S. will have to intervene again "either because it's a period of disorder or because we can't
withstand the political criticism from our partner countries." He adds: "The very richness and increased
flexibility of markets that Alan Greenspan has emphasized probably translates into fewer episodes of
disorder, but when they come, they're going to be bigger."
(See related letters: "Letters to the Editor: Weak Dollar: Is U.S. Pulling a Tom Sawyer? " -- WSJ Jan. 31,
2005)

Blue Chips Advance, Second Time in '05, While Dollar Drops
Monday's Markets
Blue Chips Advance, Second Time in '05, While Dollar Drops
By E.S. Browning
428 words
11 January 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
STOCKS AVOIDED another down day -- by the skin of their teeth.
Up almost 60 points during the day, the Dow Jones Industrial Average sagged in the afternoon, as it has for
more than a week. A late rebound left the blue-chip average with its second "up" day this year, an advance of
17.07 points, or 0.16%, to 10621.03. It is down 1.5% since the year began, as a variety of stocks have given
back some of their gains from the last 10 weeks of 2004.
The Nasdaq Composite Index registered its first gain of the year, up 0.40%, or 8.43 points, at 2097.04, a
decline of 3.6% for 2005.
Investors got bad news after regular trading as aluminum maker Alcoa reported lower-than-forecast quarterly
profit. The stock fell nearly 1% in after-hours trading. Investors will closely watch Intel's profit news, due after
regular trading today.
Many people remain nervous about the minutes of the Federal Reserve's December interest-rate meeting,
which were released last week and indicated that the Fed is concerned about inflation risks and prepared to
raise rates steadily this year, said stock trader Brian Williamson at Boston Co. Asset Management, a
subsidiary of Mellon Financial that manages about $42 billion in stocks.
"You get a feeling of a lack of conviction," Mr. Williamson said. With stocks at three-year highs late last year,
he said, many wonder how much higher they will go, and when would-be buyers see signs of selling, he said,
they often step aside.
Oil prices fell 10 cents to $45.33 a barrel on the New York Mercantile Exchange, after soaring through $47
during the day. Gold and bonds were little changed and the dollar declined.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index rose 0.34%, or 4.06 points, to 1190.25, down 1.8% this year.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks rose. On the Big Board, where 1.49 billion shares traded, 2,066 stocks rose and 1,239 fell.
Bonds were mixed. The 10-year Treasury note fell 1/32, or 31.25 cents for each $1,000 invested. The yield
rose to 4.277%. The 30-year bond was up 7/32 to yield 4.825%.
The dollar weakened. Late in New York, it traded at 104.21 yen, down from 104.84 yen, while the euro rose
against the dollar to $1.3085 from $1.3049.

What Wall Street's Best Are Telling Their Clients --- Investment Firms Push Large Stocks and Short Bonds; A Comeback for Telecom?
What Wall Street's Best Are Telling Their Clients --- Investment Firms Push Large Stocks and Short
Bonds; A Comeback for Telecom?
By Jane J. Kim
2,194 words
11 January 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
D1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
WALL STREET'S ANNUAL RACE to predict what will happen in the markets over the next 12 months is on.
In recent weeks, major banks have been presenting their biggest clients with forecasts as well as
asset-allocation and investment strategies geared to 2005. Goldman Sachs Group Inc. sees the Standard
&Poor's 500-stock index at 1325 by year-end (it closed yesterday at 1190), while Bank of America Securities,
a unit of Bank of America Corp. has a target of 1200. Prudential Equity Group, a unit of Prudential Financial
Inc., is bullish on energy stocks; PNC Advisors, the investment-management arm of PNC Financial Services
Group Inc., is unenthusiastic about them.
But, from a review of what some of the biggest outfits are recommending, a number of common themes and
strategies emerge. Most agree that the economy will grow at a steady if somewhat slower pace -- with stock
returns, like last year's, in the mid-to-upper single digits. Nearly all advocate putting more money overseas
and, given the likelihood of higher interest rates, shifting to bonds with shorter maturities. The general
leaning is toward large-cap stocks -- rather than small-cap or medium-cap ones -- because they tend to hold
up better in a slower economy.
Technology stocks remain somewhat out of favor, with "cautious" outlooks on them from Bank of America,
Merrill Lynch &Co. Inc., and Prudential Equity Group. By contrast, there is fairly widespread optimism about
health-care stocks, and even the long-battered telecom sector is attracting new fans.
In recent years, the reports -- which are usually updated once a month -- have tended to fall on the
conservative side. Typically, the forecasts are laid out by a firm's investment strategy team and then
discussed and then voted on by a broader investment policy committee. Some say that process weeds out
more-extreme views.
Indeed, stocks did better than most Wall Street analysts expected last year. The consensus estimate last
year was that earnings would grow 12.8%, according to Thomson Financial. Instead, earnings are poised to
grow 19.4% year over year.
By and large, this year's reports are split into two camps. More-optimistic strategists see notable growth
opportunities, noting that companies are sitting on piles of cash; that bodes well for increased business
spending and for mergers and acquisition activity. The doubters worry about slowing profits, widening U.S.
budget and trade deficits, rising interest rates and the specter of inflation.
Here is what Wall Street's best and brightest say is in store for what is already shaping up to be a bumpy
year. (The chart below offers a look at what different firms are recommending -- and how they fared in their
2004 forecasts.)
Stocks
By and large, Wall Street is recommending that investors put more money into stocks. But they will have to
work a little harder at finding the right companies.
That is why investors may want to consider sectors in which consumer demand isn't likely to wane even if the
economy is sluggish, such as health care and consumer staples like food, beverage and tobacco. After a
tough year, the health-care sector is relatively cheap right now, and long-term demographic trends and rising
health care costs make the sector an attractive place to invest in, says Benjamin Pace, U.S. chief investment
officer for Deutsche Bank's Private Wealth Management Group, a unit of Deutsche Bank AG. He likes biotech
companies such as Gilead Sciences Inc., which he says has a strong product line. Meanwhile, automotive,
retailing and restaurant companies may suffer if consumers have less discretionary income to spend this
year.

--- Page 70 ---
Page 70 of 265 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Companies' strong balance sheets should boost spending on machinery, plants and equipment. That would
benefit industrial companies such as Union Pacific Corp., whose business of transporting goods, such as
shipping coal to power plants or grain to western ports, is picking up, Mr. Pace says.
Lehman Brothers, for example, added General Electric Co. to its Core and Growth U.S. Equity Strategy
model portfolios last month. Tom Wirth, a portfolio manager at Chemung Canal Trust Co., says he has
"significantly increased" the firm's position in GE and expects industrials and basic materials companies to
lead the markets this year. Energy stocks, such as Schlumberger Ltd., should also do well given the outlook
for increased drilling activity, says David Darst, chief investment strategist at Morgan Stanley's Individual
Investor Group.
Large-capitalization stocks, generally thought of as higher quality, are often a favorite in periods where profits
are decelerating. Much of Wall Street thinks these stocks as a whole are attractively valued relative to
small-cap value stocks, which have had a strong run over the past several years. Current valuations make
companies with a market cap between $10 billion to $50 billion more attractive than small-capitalization
stocks or even megacap stocks worth more than $100 billion, Mr. Darst says. That means investors should
choose a company like a Target Corp. over a Wal-Mart Stores Inc., for example, or an Allergan Inc. rather
than one of the big drug companies, he suggests.
Within international markets, investors should increase their allocation to emerging markets such as Asia and,
to a lesser extent, Latin America, from Europe and Japan, whose economies are expected to grow more
slowly, says Arun Motianey, director of investment research at Citigroup Private Bank, a unit of Citigroup Inc.
Bonds
Rising interest rates and higher inflation are generally bad news for bonds. The value of existing bonds
drops, since investors can get higher interest payments on new bonds. As a result, many experts are advising
investors to trim their bond exposure, or at least move to bonds with shorter durations, which are less volatile.
Citigroup Private Bank downgraded its short-term view on U.S. Treasurys to "bearish" last month. Many have
also grown more cautious on high-yield, or junk, bonds, whose yields are no longer as attractive, relative to
their risk.
The best opportunities in fixed income, strategists say, are abroad. PNC Advisors' chief investment strategist
Jeff Kleintop, who expects close to zero returns on bonds, last month recommended international bonds,
given his outlook for a decline in the dollar and the fact that interest rates are unlikely to rise as much in
Europe and Japan as they will in the U.S.
Mortgage-backed bond funds, which invest in pools of home-mortgage securities, are also a good opportunity
given that rates are rising, says Mary Miller, director of fixed income at T. Rowe Price Group Inc. That is
because their higher yields will serve to cushion any price drops and there is less chance that homeowners
will pay off their mortgages early. One such fund is Accessor Mortgage Securities, which is highly rated by
investment researcher Morningstar Inc. Municipal-bond funds are also attractive for tax-conscious investors
since the funds are relatively cheap compared with taxable alternatives.
Many firms also recommend Treasury Inflation Protected Securities. These vehicles are a good hedge
against rising prices because their principal payments, unlike those of ordinary Treasurys, are adjusted every
six months for inflation.
Alternative Investments
A number of strategists are increasing their recommended dose of alternative investments -- such as
commodities and hedge funds. A "voracious" demand for commodities, especially from markets such as
China and India that are building up the infrastructure in their countries, is likely to help keep the price of
commodities relatively high for at least next three to five years, Mr. Pace of Deutsche Bank says. The bank,
for example, is recommending that clients invest in commodities such as oil, gas, gold, silver, aluminum,
steel and other so-called soft commodities, such as soybeans, wheat and corn. Investors can also buy mutual
funds that invest in commodities or an exchange-traded fund such as iShares Goldman Sachs Natural
Resources Index, he notes.
Last week, Morgan Stanley raised its recommended allocation to private equity and venture-capital funds,
given a stronger climate for initial public offerings and mergers and acquisitions. The firm, however, has a
cautious view on real estate investment trusts, which had a strong run last year, given valuations and the
outlook for rising interest rates.
---

expense-account abuse.

shareholders.

GM CEO Rick Wagoner said rising health-care costs and higher interest rates will pose challenges for the

South Korea's Hyundai Motor said it is aiming to boost U.S. auto sales by 15% this year.

from 2001 to 2003.

Dow Industrials Have `Up' Day, First of 2005 --- Blue Chips Gain 25.05 Points, But the Nasdaq Composite Index Still Hasn't Had Reason to...
Thursday's Markets
Dow Industrials Have `Up' Day, First of 2005 --- Blue Chips Gain 25.05 Points, But the Nasdaq
Composite Index Still Hasn't Had Reason to Cheer
By E.S. Browning
528 words
7 January 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
AFTER STARTING the year with a hangover -- three consecutive days of declines -- most stock indexes
yesterday finally squeaked out their first "up" day of 2005.
But another late-day pullback limited the gains, leaving many technology stocks down for a fourth consecutive
day.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average edged up 25.05 points, or 0.24%, to 10622.88, after a 185.18-point drop in
the year's first three days. The blue-chip average broke a six-day losing streak, but remains down 1.5% for
2005. The Dow yesterday had been up almost 70 points at lunchtime.
The Nasdaq Composite Index, full of tech stocks, eased 0.06%, or 1.24 points, to 2090.00. It has fallen on all
four days of the new year, something that hadn't happened since 1991. Stocks are proving sluggish after their
sharp rally late last year.
Some investors believe that a part of last year's surge was "borrowed" from this year -- based on the
anticipation of this year's economic strength -- and that, with most fourth-quarter earnings still not announced,
stocks lately have been at least temporarily refunding some of what they borrowed.
Investors yesterday were nervous about December job-creation numbers due this morning, said Stuart
Schweitzer, global investment strategist at JPMorgan Fleming Asset Management in New York. "There is
some fear of employment weakness," Mr. Schweitzer said. Job creation has been slow during the economic
recovery, creating worries about consumer spending.
But Federal Reserve Bank of Kansas City President Thomas Hoenig made upbeat comments on the
employment outlook and said he is "optimistic on the U.S. economy." He was noncommittal on interest-rate
prospects, but his comments softened worries that Fed interest-rate increases might harm the economy.
December chain-store sales were a bit better than expected.
Still, last week's new unemployment claims were higher than expected, and crude-oil futures jumped back
above $45 a barrel: up $2.17 to $45.56 on the New York Mercantile Exchange, the biggest increase since
Dec. 15. Traders said that crude's repeated resurgence every time it gets close to $40 a barrel is an
indication of continuing uncertainty about supplies, about production capacity and about stability in the Middle
East.
The dollar rallied for a fifth session against the euro, and some traders who had been speculating on further
dollar declines covered their bets, which helped push the U.S. currency still higher. Although many expect
further dollar declines later, there was a widespread view that any sign of U.S. economic strength in the short
run could push the dollar higher, at least temporarily, amid expectations of continued U.S rate increases.
Gold continued to pull back.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index rose 0.35%, or 4.15 points, to 1187.89, down 2% for 2005.
The 10-year Treasury note rose 4/32, or $1.25 for each $1,000 invested. The yield fell to 4.271%.

Year-End Review of Markets &Finance 2004; Natural Disasters, Elections &Rising Rates
Year-End Review of Markets &Finance 2004; Natural Disasters, Elections &Rising Rates
By Cary Szafranski, Roland Keane and Gregory J. Corcoran
16,310 words
7 January 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
R12
English
(Copyright (c) 2005, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
It was a year of catastrophes and war, and a year in which many events were viewed mostly through the
prism of the U.S. presidential race. Hurricanes barraged Florida. Typhoons plus an earthquake battered
Japan. As the year drew to a close an earthquake and resulting tsunami devastated the nations along the
Indian Ocean, producing a horrific death toll.
The U.S., Afghanistan and Ukraine all held widely followed presidential elections. Yasser Arafat's death
potentially changed the Middle East peace equation.
In Iraq, the U.S.-led coalition transferred power to Iraqis, though the violence didn't abate. Through it all, Iraq
moved toward elections scheduled for the end of this month.
The resolution of the U.S. election caused investors to head to the markets. Stocks, which had been
rangebound all year, took off once the race looked settled, with many widely followed indexes posting solid
gains. Meantime, the unfolding saga of the crippling of a Russian oil giant exacerbated the spike in oil prices.
The Federal Reserve raised interest rates five times, the most active year for rate moves since 1994. The
Boston Red Sox won their first World Series since 1918.
Here is a summary of the news events as chronicled on the front page of The Wall Street Journal. In most
cases, the dates reflect when the items were published.
JANUARY
5 An Afghan conference called a loya jirga reports achieving its goal of drafting a constitution, passing a
post-Taliban test. The constitution gives interim President Hamid Karzai the strong presidency he demanded,
and elections to that office are set for June.
NASA exults as the Spirit lands on Mars in an apparent dry lake bed propitious for exploration.
Georgia elects U.S.-educated lawyer Mikhail Saakashvili to succeed ousted President Eduard Shevardnadze.
6 President Bush extends an order first issued by Ronald Reagan declaring Libya a threat and sets
sanctions, saying he wants disarmament proof.
Pete Rose admits to gambling on baseball, but says he never wagered against his Cincinnati Reds.
Halliburton is cleared by the head of the Army Corps of Engineers of any wrongdoing related to a Kuwait fuel
contract. Pentagon auditors allege the company's KBR unit overcharged the U.S. government by more than
$100 million for delivering gasoline to Iraq. The Pentagon later begins a criminal probe.
The Agriculture Department says it will begin destroying 450 cattle held under quarantine in its investigation
of mad-cow disease.
7 Indian President Atal Bihari Vajpayee and Pakistan President Pervez Musharraf agree to start a "historic"
quest for peace, deciding in talks in Islamabad to formally tackle all issues dividing the nuclear neighbors,
including the Gordian knot of Kashmir.
Iran and Egypt agree to restore diplomatic ties sundered in 1979.
8 President Bush lays out an election-year plan to overhaul immigration policy. The plan would let millions of
guest workers live in the U.S. for at least three years and offer them incentives, such as participation in Social
Security, to register under it.

--- Page 77 ---
Page 77 of 265 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
9 The Pentagon tells Germany it will begin withdrawing troops next year in a major post-Cold War
realignment.
A Wisconsin Catholic bishop bars any state or federal lawmaker who supports abortion rights from
Communion in the Diocese of La Crosse.
Hewlett-Packard agrees to resell Apple's iPod music player and promote Apple's online music store in a
surprise partnership. The pact marks the first time Apple has sold hardware for use under another brand.
12 The Palestine Liberation Organization asserts it has the right to unilaterally declare a state even without a
peace deal.
A U.S. delegation visits North Korea's main nuclear facility at Yongbyon and is shown what the hosts said
was weapons-grade plutonium.
Royal Dutch/Shell Group says it overstated by 20% its proven oil and natural-gas reserves, a key measure of
its future prospects.
The Pentagon launches an examination of senior military officials hired away by big defense contractors. The
inquiry stems from a scandal at Boeing.
13 The Supreme Court backs Bush secrecy on Sept. 11 detainees, refusing to hear a case arguing it was
unconstitutional to hold hundreds of foreigners rounded up after the 2001 terrorist attacks without giving any
information about them.
Nasdaq says six big companies now listed on the NYSE, including Hewlett-Packard and Charles Schwab, will
also list shares on the Nasdaq Stock Market.
A French court orders Morgan Stanley to pay LVMH $38 million in damages for publishing research biased
against the firm.
14 Former Enron finance chief Andrew Fastow and his wife agree to plead guilty to criminal charges for their
roles in the energy concern's collapse. Mr. Fastow later agrees to serve 10 years in return for his cooperation.
15 President Bush proposes an ambitious manned-space venture. The president will seek $12 billion for
initial stages of his plan for exploration of the solar system. Bush also pledges to renew shuttle flights and
complete the international space station by 2010.
Libya ratifies the nuclear test-ban treaty, the United Nations says.
J.P. Morgan Chase agrees to acquire Bank One for $58 billion in stock, forming the second-biggest U.S. bank
with assets of $1.1 trillion. Bank One Chairman and CEO James Dimon is tapped to assume the top post of
the combined company when J.P. Morgan CEO William Harrison steps down in 2006.
Coca-Cola says the SEC launched a formal inquiry into allegations of accounting and marketing fraud.
16 Federal Reserve data show that the wealth of U.S. families has risen near levels last seen when the stock
market peaked in 2000. The recovery has been boosted by a rebound in stocks and rising home values,
which in turn have helped fuel consumer spending despite weak job growth.
Microsoft violated terms of its antitrust settlement, the government says. The company agrees to alter its next
Windows version.
19 A suicide bomber strikes at the heart of U.S. power in Baghdad, as a truck bomb kills at least 20, mostly
Iraqis lined up for work at occupation headquarters. American civilian contractors and soldiers are among
about 60 wounded.
President Bush installs Charles Pickering on a federal appeals court while Congress is out of session,
allowing him to serve until at least next year. Democrats had bottled up his nomination for more than two
years.
20 Iowa revives John Kerry's hopes as the winnowing of Democrats began. The Massachusetts senator gets
a boost from late-deciding voters and ads stressing Vietnam heroism. John Edwards comes in second.
Howard Dean's third place strips him of front-runner status. Richard Gephardt finished a campaign-ending
fourth.
The estate of Joan Kroc, the late widow of McDonald's founder Ray Kroc, plans to donate $1.5 billion to the
Salvation Army, one of the largest individual charitable gifts ever.

--- Page 78 ---
Page 78 of 265 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
21 President Bush makes an election-year appeal for continuity to guard the U.S. The president's State of the
Union address lays out a first-term narrative of a steady post-Sept. 11 sweeping back of terrorist forces
requiring, among other things, a Patriot Act renewal.
Dissident Episcopalians meet and vote in Texas for their own "church within a church" rather than accept
New Hampshire's openly gay bishop.
Martha Stewart pleads not guilty to charges of obstructing a probe into her sale of ImClone Systems stock as
her criminal trial began.
22 Eastman Kodak plans to slash its work force by as much as 21%, or as many as 15,000 jobs, by the end
of 2006 and take charges of $1.3 billion to $1.7 billion, moves the company says are necessary for its shift to
digital imaging.
23 Halliburton says two employees took as much as $6 million in kickbacks in return for awarding a Kuwaiti
company with lucrative work supplying U.S. troops. The disclosure raises further questions about
Halliburton's dealings in Iraq in the wake of allegations about overcharging for gasoline.
26 Israel and Hezbollah begin a long-discussed prisoner swap. Israel will free 435 and get back a kidnapped
businessman, as well as learning more of the fate of missing servicemen such as pilot Ron Arad.
Died: Bob Keeshan, 76, TV's beloved children's entertainer "Captain Kangaroo," in Windsor, Vt.
27 The Sept. 11 Commission says that though the government asserted hijackers' visas seemed in order,
attention to detail might have foiled them.
A pair of winter storms blanket much of the eastern half of the U.S. and police blame them for at least 34
highway deaths.
Stocks soar to levels not seen since before the Sept. 11, 2001, terrorist attacks, fueled by strong corporate
earnings and low interest rates. The Dow industrials surge 1.27% to 10702.51 and the Nasdaq climbs 1.41%
to 2153.83.
Parmalat hid nearly $16 billion of debt and reported earnings more than five times their actual size, an initial
report finds.
28 New Hampshire gives Massachusetts Sen. John Kerry an emphatic victory in the fight for the Democratic
presidential nomination, former Vermont governor Howard Dean a lifeline.
The U.S. flies nearly 30 tons of nuclear equipment out of Libya for analysis and soon may reward Tripoli's
disarmament moves.
Amazon reports full-year net income of $35.3 million, the first annual profit for the Internet retailer.
29 David Kay calls intelligence on Iraq "almost all wrong," but not cooked. The just-resigned top U.S. arms
inspector tells the Senate there should be an inquiry, but disputes Democrats' assertions that the main reason
for mistakes was pressure to tailor facts to support a war.
Britain's Tony Blair escapes disapprobation he feared in a report on the suicide of an Iraq arms expert, but
the BBC comes in for scorching criticism of its assertion that the government "sexed up" intelligence. Its
chairman resigns.
FEBRUARY
3 President Bush bows to mounting criticism of prewar Iraq weapons intelligence and will this week call for a
bipartisan inquiry, with a report after the elections.

The New England Patriots win the Super Bowl, beating the Carolina Panthers 32-29. A day later, the National
Football League, Federal Communications Commission and CBS profess shock over Janet Jackson's
breast-exposing halftime show. A federal investigation is promised.

--- Page 79 ---
Page 79 of 265 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
3 President Bush sends up a $2.4 trillion election-year budget heavy on security. Defense spending would
rise 7% and Homeland Security 10%, but other domestic spending would rise just 0.5%.
Britain's Tony Blair will follow Bush's lead and permit an investigation of prewar Iraq intelligence.
Israel's Ariel Sharon sets off a clamor in his Likud Party as he lays out plans to withdraw nearly all settlers
from the Gaza Strip.
Capitol police evacuate part of a Senate building when a letter containing powder arrives in Senate Majority
Leader Bill Frist's office. It is being tested for ricin. A day later, a finding that the powder was ricin leads to the
closure of all Senate office buildings.
4 John Kerry builds up his lead as John Edwards stays alive, Joseph Lieberman quits. Mr. Kerry cruises to
victories in primaries in Delaware, Missouri, Arizona, North Dakota and New Mexico. Mr. Edwards wins South
Carolina.
The main shareholders of Russian oil giant Yukos agree to sell Sibneft back to its owners, another move to
unwind the $13 billion merger.
5 The developer of Pakistan's atom bomb admits on television to selling nuclear secrets to Iran, Libya and
North Korea without his government's knowledge. President Pervez Musharraf later pardons Abdul Qadeer
Khan.
Full gay marriage is the standard Massachusetts legislators must adopt to satisfy the state constitution, not
Vermont-style civil unions, the state high court decides in clarifying an earlier ruling.
Ayatollah Khamenei decrees a compromise in Iran's election deadlock, ordering a review and reinstatement
of some debarred Feb. 20 candidates.
Oracle sweetens its bid for PeopleSoft to $9.4 billion, which PeopleSoft's board five days later rejects citing a
lengthy antitrust review.
6 Central Intelligence Agency chief George Tenet gives a rare public speech to defend prewar reports, saying
Saddam Hussein's desire to possess illicit arms was clear even if it hasn't been shown yet that such an
arsenal existed and that his agency never called the threat "imminent."
9 Cigna will cut 3,000 jobs, or nearly 9% of its work force, and slash its once-hefty dividend by 92% as the
health insurer struggles with a big drop in membership.
10 Secretary of State Colin Powell insists Pakistan dismantle an atomic-weapons trading network "by the
roots" as Islamabad denied a military plane had delivered nuclear equipment to North Korea.
11 French lawmakers vote to ban religious emblems such as Muslim headscarves from state schools.
The Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries vows to cut its production about 9% beginning April 1,
hoping to prevent an expected decline in oil prices. The crude-oil benchmark jumps $1.04 to $33.87 a barrel.
12 Comcast Corp. launches an unsolicited $48.7 billion offer for Walt Disney Co., a bid that seems designed
to exploit one of the weakest moments in Chairman Michael Eisner's career. Disney's board later rejects the
Comcast proposal and offers a vote of confidence for Eisner.
Ret. U.S. Gen. Wesley Clark drops out of the presidential race after John Kerry wins primaries in Tennessee
and Virginia Tuesday.
South Korean scientists report the first cloning of a human embryo and the extraction of its stem cells.
China asks banks to curb 2004 lending growth to cool the fast-growing economy.
13 U.N. nuclear inspectors say documents Iran failed to divulge show the same centrifuge design as one sold
to Libya by the Pakistani network.
The Sept. 11 Commission plans to seek public testimony by President Bush, Bill Clinton, Dick Cheney and Al
Gore. Initial reaction by the White House was noncommittal.
San Francisco officials perform 87 gay weddings in defiance of California law as Massachusetts continues
debate on legalizing such unions.
ImClone Systems won approval for its experimental cancer drug but its shares underwent a preapproval
plunge before trading was halted.

--- Page 80 ---
Page 80 of 265 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
17 The SEC is investigating if Titan Corp. made illegal payments to foreign consultants, possibly delaying the
company's sale to Lockheed Martin.
18 John Kerry wins Wisconsin primary in an unexpectedly close challenge, as independents break for John
Edwards. A day later, Howard Dean drops out.
Iran lawmakers commit an almost unthinkable act of dissent over clerical interference in elections, telling
Ayatollah Khamenei his regime has turned the Islamic revolution into an instrument of repression.
Cingular wins the bidding war for AT&T Wireless, beating Vodafone with an offer of $41 billion. The deal will
create the largest U.S. wireless company with more than 46 million customers.
Five NYSE specialists tentatively agree to pay about $240 million to settle civil charges that they stepped
ahead of customer orders.
Tyson Foods is found to have illegally depressed cattle prices by a jury in Alabama, which called for $1.28
billion in damages. The presiding judge in March rules Tyson won't have to pay the full $1.28 billion.
El Paso Corp. reduces its proven natural-gas reserves by 35%, forcing a $1 billion write-down in the value of
its natural-gas assets.
Italian police arrests a son and daughter of Parmalat founder Calisto Tanzi, widening a probe.
20 The U.N. backs the U.S. in opposing Iraqi elections anytime soon.
U.N. inspectors find centrifuges for enriching uranium to arms grade at an Iran air base. They are more
advanced than Tehran had admitted.
Jeffrey Skilling is indicted on an array of charges stemming from an alleged plan to manipulate Enron's
financial statements. The indictment of the fallen energy company's former president and CEO leaves former
Chairman Kenneth Lay as the highest-ranking Enron figure who hasn't been charged with criminal
wrongdoing.
Coke says Douglas Daft will retire and that it will conduct a search for a new chief.
The U.S. attorney's office in Manhattan launches a criminal investigation related to tax shelters that were sold
by KPMG.
Shell says the SEC launched a formal probe of the oil company's surprise restatement of its energy reserves
last month.
23 Iran admits buying atomic gear from the "subcontinent." U.N. inspectors are in Libya trying to grasp its
haphazard nuclear push. Separately, Iran's clerics crow over the reshaping of Parliament accomplished by
their editing of ballots, but turnout in last week's elections was anemic.
Arnold Schwarzenegger tells his attorney general to halt gay marriages after a court refuses to.
25 President Bush backs a constitutional amendment banning gay marriages.
First charges are filed against detainees in Guantanamo Bay. Two men from Yemen and Sudan, captured in
2001 and described as close aides to bin Laden, are accused of conspiracy to commit war crimes.
North Korea repeats demands to be paid if it freezes its nuclear programs. The U.S. reiterates its insistence
that North Korea dismantle all nuclear programs, as six-nation talks open in China.
Fed Chairman Alan Greenspan says Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac pose serious risks to the financial system
and urges Congress to curb their growth.
The World Trade Organization rules the European Union is allowed to impose sanctions on U.S. companies
in retaliation for antidumping laws.
27 Iraq's Shiite leader says he will compromise on transition plans and give his blessing to U.S. plans to set
up a caretaker regime by July as long as U.N.-guaranteed elections are in place by year end.
Smith &Wesson's chairman quits after a newspaper reports he was jailed for a string of armed robberies in
the 1950s and '60s.
A church-sponsored study finds that 4% of the 109,694 members of the U.S. Roman Catholic clergy in the
past five decades were accused of molestation.

The Justice Department files suit to block Oracle's $9.4 billion hostile bid for PeopleSoft, saying a takeover
would choke competition in the market for business-applications software. Oracle says it will fight the suit.
MARCH
1 Haitian President Jean-Bertrand Aristide flees to Africa amid revolt as President Bush sends in the Marines.
North Korean nuclear talks end without much more than a pledge to continue six-nation negotiations and the
formation of a working group to seek progress in the interim.
A Philippine ferry fire leaves 180 missing just after it left Manila for southern islands. Al Qaeda ally Abu
Sayyaf claims it was its handiwork.
2 An interim Iraqi constitution is approved, calling for a federalist state with two official languages, a prime
minister who runs the country's daily affairs and a president who can launch a war -- but only with the
approval of parliament.
3 John Kerry wraps up the Democratic presidential nomination with a near-sweep of the 10 "Super Tuesday"
contests, with John Edwards dropping out after being competitive only in Georgia, and Vermont staying loyal
to Howard Dean.
Nearly 200 people die in Iraq's worst series suicide bombings and attacks on Shiite pilgrims gathered in
Karbala and Baghdad for Ashura, the holiest day of their calendar, which marks long-ago events that led to
the split with the Sunni branch of Islam.
New York files charges against the mayor of New Paltz for marrying gay couples.
Bernard Ebbers, the former chairman and CEO of WorldCom, is indicted for allegedly helping to orchestrate
the largest accounting fraud in U.S. history. After a two-year probe, prosecutors got their break as Scott
Sullivan, WorldCom's former finance chief, pleaded guilty to three charges and agreed to cooperate in the
case.
The Baby Bells score a victory as an appeals court strikes down FCC rules for how regional phone
companies open their networks to rivals.
4 Royal Dutch/Shell's boards force out Chairman Sir Philip Watts and a top deputy amid probes of the
company's overbooking of oil and natural-gas reserves.

The Big Board notifies about two dozen individual specialists they are under investigation for potential trading
abuses.
5 International Monetary Fund Managing Director Horst Kohler quits to seek the presidency of Germany. After
a succession fight, the international lender in May names former Spanish Finance Minister Rodrigo Rato as
Kohler's successor.
The FCC proposes fines against radio companies for carrying "shock jocks," in the latest phase in a sweeping
federal crackdown on controversial TV and radio content. Among the targets are radio host Howard Stern and
his employer, Infinity.
8 Martha Stewart is convicted of obstruction charges related to her sale of ImClone stock and vows to appeal.
Spain's Telefonica agrees to buy BellSouth's Latin American wireless operations for slightly more than $4.1
billion.

10 The Nasdaq falls below 2000 for the first time this year, easing 13.62 to 1995.16.

Page 82 of 265 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
11 In a possible blow to President Bush, the Senate approves 51-48 a measure requiring 60 votes for tax

Iran admits its military took part in nuclear activities and says it may quit cooperating with the U.N. as it plans

Lee Boyd Malvo is sentenced to life in prison for the sniper shootings in Washington a day after the attacks'

12 Spain suffers the worst terrorist attack in its history. Ten coordinated bomb blasts rip apart trains in or near
Madrid, killing 191 and wounding more than 1,400. The DJIA falls 168.51 to 10128.38, a low for the year thus
far and its biggest fall since May.
California's high court halts San Francisco same-sex weddings. Massachusetts lawmakers preliminarily
approve a state amendment to ban gay marriage but allow civil unions.
15 Spain's ruling party loses national elections in the wake of the Madrid bombings, as voters hand a surprise
victory to the Socialist opposition, which promises to remove the country's troops from Iraq. Evidence mounts
that al Qaeda was responsible for the attacks.
Russian President Vladimir Putin wins a second term with 71% of the vote.
16 The Agriculture Department will try to screen hundreds of thousands of cattle for mad-cow disease amid
pressure from beef importers and Congress.
Bank of America and Fleet Boston agree to $675 million of fines and fee cuts to settle civil-fraud charges in
the mutual-fund scandal. The penalties bring to more than $1.65 billion the total to be paid by fund-industry
participants to settle allegations of improper trading.
19 Saddam Hussein's regime illegally skimmed as much as $10.1 billion from the U.N. oil-for-food program,
much more than previously estimated, according to a new U.S. congressional analysis.
Shell cuts its estimate of reserves for the second time in three months and pushes back its year-end filings
with the SEC.
Parmalat prosecutors seek indictments against the Italian arms of Bank of America, Deloitte Touche and
Grant Thornton.
22 Taiwan's president wins another term by a slim margin, putting Beijing on the defensive. Chen Shui-bian
survives an unexplained shooting.
Israelis kill Hamas leader Sheik Ahmed Yassin, Gaza residents say, amid a continuing offensive as Ariel
Sharon lobbies cabinet ministers for support of his Gaza-withdrawal plan.
23 The Food and Drug Administration steps up warnings of possible suicide risks in taking antidepressants,
telling doctors and families to watch for danger signs and asking manufacturers to beef up cautionary advice
on labels.
Putnam's board concludes three top executives knew about improper trading by fund managers in 2000 and
should have reported it to fund trustees.
24 The record industry sues individuals at 21 universities nationwide, claiming that they illegally shared music
files.
The Sept. 11 panel details chances not taken against al Qaeda. In testimony by top Bush and Clinton security
officials and reports by the commission's staff, the picture painted is one of a too-heavy reliance on diplomacy
instead of military action in the years before New York and Washington were struck.
Israel threatens the entire Hamas leadership with death as Abdel Aziz Rantisi, who survived a missile attack
by Israel last year, takes command of the group in Gaza following Sheik Yassin's slaying. The next day,
Hamas vows to kill Ariel Sharon in retaliation for Israel's slaying of Sheik Yassin, but backs off threats to
attack the U.S.
25 At a Sept. 11 hearing, longtime White House national security aide Richard Clarke expands on
accusations he has made in recent days that the Bush team didn't take al Qaeda seriously enough early on
and seemed more intent on building toward an Iraq war.
EU regulators slap Microsoft with tough sanctions and a record $612.7 million fine for allegedly abusing its
market power for technology that links computers and plays music and video. The software titan is ordered to

disclose code from its Windows operating system and to offer PC makers a version of Windows that doesn't
include Media Player.
26 President Bush says at a New Hampshire campaign stop he would have used any means to stop the

portrait of pre-Sept. 11 laxity on al Qaeda. Bush's message is hurt somewhat as Homeland Security says a

Pakistan says it has killed 50 terrorist suspects and caught 163 in its al Qaeda hunt, and may have broken up
a spring offensive.
The Senate votes 61-38 to join the House in making the harming of a fetus during an attack on a woman a
discrete crime.
Ex-Dynegy executive Jamie Olis is sentenced to more than 24 years in prison in an accounting-fraud case

29 Afghanistan's Hamid Karzai postpones the first post-Taliban elections to September from June, saying
neither ballot logistics nor security is yet adequate.
30 The U.N. shakes up its security staff as it weighs returning to Iraq. Secretary-General Kofi Annan ousts the
official in charge at the time of the Aug. 19 bombing of U.N. Baghdad headquarters and disciplines others for
lapses. The U.N. wants a summer return.
Massachusetts lawmakers vote approval of a proposed state constitutional amendment that will allow gay
civil unions, but not marriages.
31 President Bush relents and will allow Condoleezza Rice to testify to the Sept. 11 panel. President Bush
and Dick Cheney agree to speak privately to the full commission.
The U.S. arms inspector says he is shifting from looking for stockpiles to documenting Iraqi intentions to
make illicit weapons.
APRIL
1 The U.S. is shocked by savage displays of Sunni Iraqis' hatred. In Fallujah, an attack that killed four
American civilian security contractors is followed by their charred corpses being pulled by a mob from their
burning vehicles and dragged through the streets. Two are hung from a Euphrates bridge.
Shell's planners warned top managers as early as 2000 that the oil giant risked disappointing markets with
overly optimistic output-growth targets, according to a body of documents internal investigators turned over to
U.S. regulators.
Riggs Bank faces scrutiny for failing to file "suspicious activity" reports on withdrawals by Saudi Arabia's
envoy to Washington. A month later, Riggs is fined $25 million for money-laundering violations related to its
oversight of bank accounts held by foreign diplomats.
2 The Dow industrials will add American International Group, Pfizer and Verizon Communications, the first
changes to the average in more than four years. They replace AT&T, Eastman Kodak and International
Paper.
5 U.S. employers added 308,000 jobs in March, hitting a four-year high, the government reports.
The case of Tyco ex-Chief Executive Dennis Kozlowski and former Chief Financial Officer Mark Swartz ends
in mistrial; juror Ruth Jordan later denies to fellow jurors that she signaled solidarity with the defense team by
using a hand gesture.
8 The former Iraqi regime garnered $10.1 billion in illegal revenue from the U.N. oil-for-food program,
including $5.7 billion in oil smuggled out of Iraq, because of inadequate oversight, congressional auditors say.
Computer Associates' ex-finance chief, Ira Zar, and two other former finance officials agree to plead guilty to
at least two criminal charges, including securities fraud and obstruction, in the federal probe of the software
maker's accounting. Mr. Zar becomes the highest-ranking officer yet charged in the probe of the company.
9 A wave of kidnappings by insurgents targets aid workers and contractors who keep vital deliveries moving
to U.S. forces. Seven workers for a Halliburton unit and two soldiers riding shotgun with their convoy go
missing; 40 others from 12 nations have been abducted in the past week.

--- Page 84 ---
Page 84 of 265 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
16 The U.S. strains to calm Iraq as al Qaeda works to divide allies. Nations in the coalition spurn a purported
tape by bin Laden, which the Central Intelligence Agency judges authentic and recent, offering cessation of
terrorist attacks if they pull forces from Muslim countries. The Pentagon tells 20,000 U.S. soldiers in Iraq their
tours are being extended.
19 The U.S. is set back in its quest to hand off authority in Iraq. Spain's new premier, Jose Luis Rodriguez
Zapatero, says he wants to pull his nation's 1,430 soldiers out as soon as possible; Rice suggests others may
follow.
