Year-End Review of Markets &Finance 2004
ß--- -4-
Hamas vows revenge after Israel succeeds in its second attempt to kill Abdel Aziz Rantisi. The absence of
any large-scale attack after Sheik Ahmed Yassin's killing in March leads some to doubt if the Palestinian
group still has the power to carry out such threats.
20 The Hague affirms Bosnian Serbs committed genocide at Srebrenica in 1995, but Radislav Krstic, a
general who took part, is convicted of lesser crimes.
McDonald's Chief Executive Jim Cantalupo dies suddenly of a heart attack; Charlie Bell is named to succeed
him. In his 16 months as chief executive, Mr. Cantalupo executed the most dramatic turnaround in
McDonald's history.
US Airways Group CEO David Siegel quits after failing to turn around the carrier; former Lehman CEO Bruce
Lakefield succeeds him.
21 Former Air Force official Darleen Druyun pleads guilty to discussing a job with Boeing weeks before she
removed herself from talks over a $23 billion defense contract sought by the Chicago aerospace concern.
22 Suicide bombers sow terror in Iraqi and Saudi Arabian cities. At least 68, including many schoolchildren,
are killed in police-station attacks in Basra blamed on Sunni extremists with possible al Qaeda ties.
Computer Associates' Sanjay Kumar steps down as chairman and chief executive amid fears he might be
charged in the company's accounting probe. Lewis Ranieri is named chairman. The company soon restates
more than $2 billion in revenue and names Kenneth Cron interim CEO.
The FCC rejects AT&T's request to avoid long-distance fees on calls using the Internet.
23 Inspectors find airport security still fails to detect all weapons, and it doesn't matter if private or
government workers man gates.
26 China rules out direct Hong Kong elections for 2007 and 2008, arrogating to itself power over the city's
political development.
Aventis agrees to be acquired by rival French drug maker Sanofi for about $65 billion in cash and stock, amid
pressure by the French government for Swiss suitor Novartis to back off. The deal forms the world's No.3
drug maker.
Aon, Marsh &McLennan and Willis are subpoenaed by New York Attorney General Spitzer's office for
information on a form of payment from insurers to brokers.
27 Boeing faces a federal criminal probe that has expanded into whether it used Lockheed Martin documents
to compete for NASA contracts.
29 Nortel Networks fires chief Executive Frank Dunn and other senior officials, as the firm's accounting
problems spread to its top ranks. The telecommunications-equipment maker also warns of a sharp downward
revision of 2003 results, pushing its shares down 28%. William Owens is named to succeed Mr. Dunn.
30 Google files for an initial public offering, proposing to sell an estimated $2.7 billion in stock through a
Dutch-auction process designed to put more shares in the hands of individual investors.
MAY

--- Page 85 ---
Page 85 of 265 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
3 The U.S. is investigating just how widespread was abuse of Iraqi prisoners after shocking photos surface.
Westerners scramble for flights out of Saudi Arabia after gunmen kill six employees of ABB, including two
Americans.
4 Frank Quattrone, the ex-head of technology investment banking at Credit Suisse First Boston, is found
guilty after a retrial on charges he interfered with a U.S. investigation at the investment bank in 2000.
5 Coca-Cola turns to a former senior executive, E. Neville Isdell, a 60-year-old Irish citizen, to take the posts
of chairman and chief executive, ending a long search after failing to lure outsiders to take the job. The
company's No.2 executive, Steven Heyer, resigns a month later after losing the top job to Isdell.
6 Bush tries to assuage surging Arab anger at Iraqi prisoner abuse. Appearing on Mideast TV, the president
calls evidence of maltreatment "abhorrent" and vowed to punish the guilty, but stops short of the apology
many demand.
7 The "morning after" pill won't be allowed to be sold over the counter, the FDA says in overruling its own
advisory panel.
Fed Chairman Greenspan says the U.S. budget deficit threatens the nation's economic stability.
The wife of Enron ex-finance chief Fastow is sentenced to a year in prison after pleading guilty to a tax
charge arising from the Enron scandal.
10 The Labor Department reports more strong employment gains, including 288,000 new nonfarm jobs in
April, raising expectations the Fed will boost rates in June.
Krispy Kreme issues its first profit warning since going public in 2000, pushing the donut maker's shares
down 29%.
11 A United Nations-backed tribunal issues an arrest warrant for ex-Gen. Wiranto, presidential candidate of
Indonesia's Golkar party, for East Timor abuses.
Citigroup agrees to pay $2.65 billion to settle a lawsuit brought by investors of the former WorldCom, who lost
billions of dollars when the telecommunications operator filed for Chapter 11. The suit alleges Citigroup and
other investment banks that underwrote WorldCom bonds didn't conduct adequate due diligence.
12 An ally of al Qaeda releases a Web video of Nick Berg, of West Chester, Pa., being horribly executed in
what killers called revenge for the prisoner abuse. The group's Web site says Abu Musab al-Zarqawi drew the
knife.
Bush bans all exports to Syria except for food and medicine, saying Damascus supports terrorism and is
undermining the war effort in Iraq.
Oil prices settle above $40 a barrel for the first time in 13 years on doubts that a call for OPEC to loosen
output limits would help meet world-wide demand.
Retailer Gap documents work-force violations at many of the 3,000 factories world-wide that make its clothes
and paints a bleak picture of plants vying for contracts.
13 Ralph Nader is endorsed by Ross Perot's old Reform Party, giving him a potential ballot line in seven
states.
A Pfizer unit agrees to plead guilty to criminal wrongdoing and the parent company will pay about $430 million
in fines in a settlement over the marketing of its Neurontin drug to doctors for unapproved uses. The deal
represents one of the largest-ever Medicaid-fraud settlements.
14 Millions of impoverished Indian voters throw out the Vajpayee government and hand power to the
opposition Congress Party of Sonia Gandhi. The following week, Gandhi is selected to be parliamentary
leader of India's Congress Party, but she refuses the prime minister post following market turmoil.
18 A car bomb kills the president of the Iraqi Governing Council. Abdel-Zahraa Othman is among nine Iraqis
killed.
20 Economist Manmohan Singh agrees to be India's next prime minister after Congress Party loyalists fail to
persuade Gandhi to reconsider.
The EU's executive arm agrees to break its six-year blanket ban on biotech food.

--- Page 86 ---
Page 86 of 265 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Intel becomes the latest company to lose in a shareholder vote recommending that stock options be treated
as expenses.
21 Ahmad Chalabi's home is raided by American and Iraqi forces. The Governing Council member's Baghdad
offices also are raided, apparently as part of an investigation of seven of his employees.
Richard Strong and his Strong Funds agree to a $175 million settlement of allegations that he took part in and
allowed rapid trading.
Microsoft announces a deal to license software to cable operator Comcast, a big step in its effort to break into
the TV business.
24 The military's prisoner-abuse scandal appears to extend beyond a few soldiers. The Pentagon
acknowledges it is reviewing the deaths of 33 detainees captured in Iraq and Afghanistan, including nine
homicides.
The U.N. atomic agency gathers evidence suggesting North Korea supplied nearly two tons of uranium to
Libya in 2001, diplomats said.
Russia clinches a deal with the EU on terms for its entry to the WTO.
25 Philippine President Gloria Macapagal Arroyo wins a narrow victory. Her coalition also gains the majority
of seats at stake in the legislature.
Eliot Spitzer sues former NYSE Chairman Dick Grasso and Compensation Committee ex-Chairman Kenneth
Langone, seeking to recover more than $100 million of Grasso's $200 million compensation package. The
civil suit alleges the two men misled directors about the size of the package and that Grasso intimidated them
into approving the payouts.
Bernard Ebbers is charged with six new counts, accusing the former WorldCom CEO of making false filings
to the SEC.
26 Comcast says it plans to offer phone service to about 40 million households by the end of 2006,
intensifying the battle between telephone and cable operators.
27 Top officials warn of a possible terror attack this summer. Attorney General John Ashcroft, his deputy
James B. Comey Jr. and Federal Bureau of Investigation Director Robert Mueller cite credible intelligence
from multiple sources as well as recent public statements attributed to al Qaeda. There is no specific
information on the timing, method or place of an attack.
Terry Nichols is convicted of 161 state murder charges for his role in the 1995 Oklahoma City bombing. The
following month, he escapes execution when a jury deadlocks in the penalty phase.
28 The U.S. economy expanded at a faster-than-estimated 4.4% rate in the first quarter, the government
reports.
Former Rite Aid CEO Martin Grass is sentenced to eight years in prison for his role in an accounting fraud at
the drugstore chain.
JUNE
1 Qwest Communications and MCI are the first big telecommunications companies to sign a pact on
network-leasing rates, a divisive industry issue.
2 Iraq gets an interim regime, the U.S. a glimpse of a path to the exit. A deal making Ghazi Ajil al-Yawer
president, as U.S. choice Adnan Pachachi bows out, is the last piece of the puzzle for a government to serve
until early 2005 elections.
The U.N. atomic agency says Iran admitted it imported centrifuge parts it previously said it made itself.
Tehran denies dealing with North Korea.

Mel Karmazin quits as Viacom president. Chief Executive Sumner Redstone names MTV Chairman Tom
Freston and CBS Chairman Leslie Moonves as co-presidents, saying the two are in line to succeed him
within three years.
3 The army expands its use of forced service extensions for troops. Many headed for Iraq or Afghanistan are
told they can't leave at the end of their hitch.
4 George Tenet submits his resignation after a rocky time as CIA chief.
7 Ronald Reagan dies at 93 after a long battle with Alzheimer's disease.
The Labor Department says an additional 248,000 nonfarm jobs were created in the U.S. in May and revises
sharply higher prior months' gains. Almost one million jobs have been created since February, the biggest
three-month increase since May 2000.
MGM Mirage offers to buy rival Mandalay for $4.85 billion, plus the assumption of $2.8 billion in debt, in what
would be the biggest-ever casino-industry deal.
8 Microsoft and SAP say they broke off recent, secret merger talks. A combination of the U.S. and German
software companies would have wedded Microsoft's dominance in operating systems and desktop
applications with SAP's lead in business applications.
10 Bush comes up empty in his appeal for allied help in pacifying Iraq. France's Chirac says at the G-8
summit he sees no role for NATO. Spain's premier says his troops won't return.
Defense Secretary Donald Rumsfeld approved Guantanamo Bay interrogation methods such as "stress
positions" and "fear of dogs" amid a terror alert in December 2002. A review was forced a month later by
military officers' complaints.
Israel plans to start compensating Gaza settlers with an eye toward full evacuation by October 2005, a
government timetable says.
The U.S. government says it won't appeal a court ruling that tossed out regulations requiring the regional
Bells to lease parts of their local-phone networks to rivals at discounts. AT&T soon announces it will stop
marketing local and long-distance service to new customers in seven states and lowers its revenue forecast
by as much as $2.5 billion.
14 The SEC opens a probe into payments that a joint venture involving Halliburton's KBR unit made in the
building of a Nigerian natural-gas complex.
15 Baghdad attackers hit foreign workers, killing three from General Electric. Two guards also are part of the
toll of 13, including an American, two Britons and a Frenchman, who die in the car bombing.
Boeing wins one of the Pentagon's biggest contracts of the year, beating out Lockheed to supply the Navy
with the next generation of submarine-hunting jets. The program is valued at an estimated $3.9 billion in the
development phase and could be valued at more than $15 billion.
The Supreme Court rules foreign customers can't sue international companies in U.S. courts alleging antitrust
violations.
16 A purported al Qaeda cell in Saudi Arabia releases a Web video of captured American Paul Johnson and
threatens to kill him if Riyadh doesn't free allies.
The Sept. 11 fund will pay about $6.9 billion in compensation to more than 5,500 people, representing 97% of
the eligible families.
17 The Sept. 11 panel says Khalid Sheikh Mohammed's original conception was for as many as 10 jetliners
to be used in coast-to-coast attacks to cripple the U.S. economy, but bin Laden feared that was too
complicated. The panel finds Osama bin Laden's wealth has been vastly overstated, and charities provided al
Qaeda funds.
21 Reacting swiftly after the beheading of American Paul Johnson, Riyadh says its police killed al Qaeda's
local chief, who had claimed responsibility, and three comrades.
EU leaders hammer out a deal on a constitution after a long struggle.
22 A price-fixing probe in the chemical industry widens into a series of global inquiries involving Dow
Chemical, DuPont, Bayer and others, helped in part by amnesty for whistle-blowers. U.S. and European

--- Page 88 ---
Page 88 of 265 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
investigators are looking into alleged conspiracies to fix prices in a half-dozen chemicals used in plastics,
rubber and synthetics.
Goldman and Morgan Stanley tentatively agree to pay a total of $80 million to settle accusations over their
handling of IPOs.
23 A judge rules a sex-bias suit against Wal-Mart Stores can move forward as a class action, presenting the
retailer with the prospect of fighting a lengthy legal battle or paying a multibillion-dollar settlement. As many
as 1.6 million current and former female U.S. employees are eligible for the class.
24 The SEC approves a rule requiring fund firms to have independent chairmen, a move that will displace
most funds' heads.
25 John Mack resigns as Credit Suisse's co-chief executive. The departure comes after Mr. Mack says he
clashed with some at the firm over his desire to pursue a merger with another financial-services company. His
departure leaves Oswald Grubel as the sole CEO.
A U.S. appeals court bars the FCC's relaxed media-ownership rules from taking effect, a setback for media
companies that was hailed by consumer groups.
28 "Fahrenheit 9/11," Michael Moore's anti-Bush film, posts $21.8 million in ticket sales to set a new bar for
documentaries.
29 The U.S. abruptly hands authority over to Iraqis before schedule. The transfer takes place in near-secrecy
in the heavily guarded occupation headquarters in Baghdad.
The U.S. restores diplomatic ties to Libya after a 24-year break.
UAL's request for a government loan guarantee is rejected for a third time, forcing the United Airlines parent
to scramble for $2 billion in financing in order to emerge from Chapter 11.
30 Bank One agrees to a $90 million settlement with the SEC and New York Attorney General Spitzer over
charges the firm's mutual-fund group entered deals to allow short-term trading.
A Russian court rules Yukos is liable for $3.41 billion in back taxes, ratcheting up pressure on the oil
company as it seeks a settlement.
JULY
1 The Fed raises its short-term interest rate to 1.25% from 1%, suggesting it will continue to raise rates a
quarter-point at a time but warning it could move to half-point increases if inflation accelerates.
2 Saddam Hussein jousts with an Iraqi judge in front of a rapt TV audience. The toppled dictator concedes
nothing in the session laying the charges against him, including slaughters of Kurds, Shiites, Iranians and
Kuwaitis.
The Pentagon says it may release low-risk detainees from Guantanamo Bay rather than letting them file
Supreme Court-mandated legal challenges. A group of lawyers demands Defense Secretary Donald
Rumsfeld give them access to the base in Cuba.
6 The U.S. economy created 112,000 jobs in June, less than half of the 250,000 economists had forecast.
7 John Kerry picks John Edwards as his running mate, showing he hopes to tap economic unease. The
presidential candidate succeeds in keeping the media off the scent. The New York Post is red-faced after
reporting Dick Gephardt would be the vice presidential nominee.
8 Kenneth Lay is indicted for his role in Enron's collapse. The energy giant's former CEO, charged with being
part of a wide-ranging scheme to defraud investors, denies any wrongdoing.
9 Tom Ridge drowns out presidential campaigning with an election terror warning. Offering no specifics and
declining to raise the alert level, the Homeland Security chief says worries have been magnified by March 11
Madrid bombings. He denies any political motives.
Prosecutors win criminal convictions against Adelphia founder John Rigas and his son Timothy, former CFO,
but fail to convince a jury the looting at the cable company involved the firm's former assistant treasurer.
MCI sues former CEO Ebbers in a bid to collect more than $300 million that he owes on $408 million of loans
from the company.

--- Page 89 ---
Page 89 of 265 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
12 Presidential politics are roiled by a scathing report on Iraq. The Senate findings on prewar CIA failure to
correctly assess Saddam Hussein's arsenal produce bipartisan condemnation. The report finds no cases of
direct pressure to produce desired results. While Democrats say a pro-war administration stance was
pressure enough, a report on whether President Bush exaggerated information he was given isn't due until
after the elections.
Los Alamos discloses a new case of missing computer files. The nuclear-arms lab says whoever took them
will face severe consequences.
13 Morgan Stanley reaches agreement with the Equal Employment Opportunity Commission to pay $54
million to settle claims by dozens of women who accused the brokerage house of denying them promotions
and pay raises.
Riggs Bank ignored evidence of corruption involving U.S. oil companies and an African autocrat, and helped
former Chilean dictator Augusto Pinochet hide millions of dollars, a congressional report alleges.
15 Bipartisan opposition sinks the gay marriage-banning amendment President Bush sought, dimming
Republican hopes for a politically advantageous cultural wedge.
16 Southwest Air's Chief Executive James Parker unexpectedly steps down after just three years, calling the
job "draining." The move follows contentious labor-contract talks with the flight attendants union. Chief
Financial Officer Gary Kelly is named as successor.
19 The Sept. 11 report makes waves days ahead of being released. Anticipated evidence that Iran let at least
eight of the "muscle" hijackers transit its territory and conceal visits to Afghan training camps is expected to
renew questions about why President Bush was so focused on Iraq in the tragedy's aftermath.
Boeing agrees to change its employment and compensation practices and to pay as much as $72.5 million to
settle a sex-bias suit.
20 The FDA is reviewing Boston Scientific's limited recall of its Taxus cardiovascular stent to see whether
enough is being done to protect consumers. Several more major medical centers, including the Cleveland
Clinic, say they will curtail their use of the stent.
Delta Air Lines reports a $1.96 billion second-quarter loss, with weak fares undercutting a surge in
passengers.

21 Microsoft says it will make a one-time dividend payment this year of $32 billion, buy back as much as $30

Schwab ousts Chief Executive David Pottruck and names founder Charles Schwab to succeed him.
22 Fresh abductions boost anxieties of foreign workers in Iraq. Insurgents press what so far has been a

DreamWorks files for a $650 million initial public offering of its animation unit, maker of the hit "Shrek" series.
23 A Wall Street Journal/NBC News poll shows Democratic contender John Kerry in a virtual dead heat with
President Bush. The poll also shows that voters remain wary of the Massachusetts senator on national
security.
Molson and Adolph Coors unveil their planned merger, while ex-director Ian Molson seeks to thwart the deal
with a counteroffer valued at as much as $4 billion. The bid sets the stage for a possible takeover battle and
escalates a feud between Mr. Molson and his cousin, Chairman Eric Molson, who backs the Coors deal.
Parmalat spent the $17.4 billion allegedly looted from the company on acquisitions, debt fees and affiliated
firms, a study says.
26 Democrats gather for their national convention in tightly policed Boston. The convention has a post-Sept.

Spain's Santander offers to acquire Britain's Abbey National in a deal valued at $15.56 billion, in what would
be Europe's biggest cross-border banking transaction. Both companies' boards approve the bid.
Mylan Laboratories agrees to acquire King Pharmaceuticals for $4 billion in stock, in a bid by the generic-drug

28 The government brings civil and criminal charges against four former executives of the U.S. Foodservice

Four brokerage houses will pay a total of $20 million to settle allegations they overcharged investors buying
corporate bonds and underpaid when they sold.
29 Attacks batter Iraq ahead of a conference to pick a Parliament. In a bloody day's worst incident, a car
bomb kills at least 68 Iraqis in Baqouba.
30 Shell reaches preliminary settlements with U.S. and British authorities to pay fines of $150 million for
overstating energy reserves.
AT&T's debt is downgraded to junk level by Moody's, which cites the impact of competition.
AUGUST
2 Homeland Security Chief Tom Ridge says new intelligence indicates al Qaeda has set sights on the New
York Stock Exchange, Citigroup, Prudential headquarters in Newark, N.J., and the World Bank and
International Monetary Fund in Washington.
Sumitomo Mitsui announces interest in Japan's fourth-biggest lender, UFJ, which had been in talks with No.2
bank Mitsubishi Tokyo. The unusual takeover battle underscores a more competitive environment for Japan's
banking industry.
Schering-Plough agrees to pay $345.5 million to settle charges it defrauded Medicaid.
Michigan's high court says governments can't transfer property from one private owner to another solely for
economic development.
5 Bristol-Myers agrees to pay $150 million to settle SEC charges of accounting fraud involving $1.5 billion of
inflated revenue.
6 General Motors voluntarily recalls nearly 250,000 Saturn Vue sport-utility vehicles after a suspension failure
during a new federal rollover test.
9 An Iraqi judge orders Ahmad Chalabi and his nephew to be arrested. The onetime Iraqi exile leader is
accused of counterfeiting. The nephew he put in charge of the tribunal trying Mr. Hussein is accused of
murdering a senior finance official.
South Africa's National Party agrees to join the ANC, nailing the coffin of the white supremacist apartheid
system that it founded and ran.
Surging oil prices -- now more than $44 a barrel -- appear to be hampering U.S. economic expansion.
Employers added 32,000 jobs in July, a low for the year and a sharp deceleration from the spring.
10 The Russian government takes control of Yukos's key production asset three days after a Moscow court
ruled such a seizure was illegal.
Car-safety regulators say more than a third of the most popular 2004 SUVs are prone to roll over.
Trump Hotels &Casinos plans to file for Chapter 11 to complete a restructuring with Credit Suisse First
Boston, which will assume control of the casino operator.
11 The Federal Reserve raises short-term rates a quarter-point to 1.5%, the second increase of the year, and
signals more remain in store. The Dow industrials climb 130.01 points to 9944.67. Oil prices briefly top $45
but settle at $44.52.
President Bush picks Porter Goss as CIA chief, setting an election-year battle.
Libya agrees to pay $35 million to non-U.S. victims of the 1986 Berlin disco bombing that prompted U.S. air
attacks on Tripoli and Benghazi.
Halliburton's KBR unit hasn't adequately accounted for more than $1.8 billion of work in Iraq, Pentagon
auditors say.

Page 91 of 265 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Marsh &McLennan and Willis are accused by a California policyholders advocacy group of collecting special
commissions from insurers and failing to disclose them properly.
12 Britain grants its first license for human embryonic cloning research.
Iamgold agrees to combine with the operations of Gold Fields that are outside southern Africa in a $2.1 billion
share swap.
13 Gov. James McGreevey shocks New Jersey by announcing he is gay, has engaged in an extramarital
affair with a man and will step down.
California's top court voids the 4,000 gay weddings that San Francisco performed this year because its mayor
lacked such authority.
Hewlett-Packard lowers financial targets for the quarter after its server-and-storage unit posted an operating
loss of $208 million resulting from a botched software installation and aggressive discounting. Three top
executives are fired.
Stocks tumble to their lows for the year on rising oil prices and weak technology earnings. The industrials
slide 123.73 to 9814.59; the Nasdaq falls 29.93 to 1752.49.
Mitsubishi Tokyo signs an agreement to acquire UFJ, putting it in a strong position to win Japan's first bank
takeover battle.
16 Hurricane Charley spends itself off New England after leaving 25 dead and damage projected to be $12
billion to $20 billion in Florida.
Miramax lays off 13% of its staff as co-Chairmen Harvey and Bob Weinstein weigh their future at Disney, the
film company's parent.
17 Venezuela says President Hugo Chavez soundly defeated a vote to recall him.
Insurers are likely to weather the cost of insured losses from Hurricane Charley, estimated at $5 billion to $10
billion.
AT&T, SBC and seven other phone companies propose a plan to overhaul how firms pay each other to
complete calls.
18 Yukos sells its stake in a Siberian natural-gas venture for $357 million, helping the oil company's efforts to
pay off its tax bill.
19 The refugee count in Darfur, Sudan reaches 1.2 million, and the U.N. food agency calls the situation in the
country's west critical.
Google sharply cuts the size of its stock sale and sets a price far lower than it had anticipated. After reviewing
auction bids, the Internet search company sets the IPO price at $85 a share -- 37% lower than the top of the
$108-to-$135 expected range it had declared. Google also says investors and insiders would more than halve
the number of shares they had planned to sell. A day later, the shares rise 18% on the first day of trading to
$100.34.
The SEC staff warns Freddie Mac that the company is likely to face civil charges involving alleged
securities-law violations.
Parmalat sues Deloitte and Grant Thornton for $10 billion in a U.S. court, alleging improper audits by their
Italian arms.
The SEC bans mutual-fund firms from directing trading business to brokers as a reward for selling fund
shares to investors.
20 UAL says it likely will need to terminate its pension plans in order to become a viable business, citing
surging fuel prices.
Nortel fires seven finance officials as it strives to clean up accounting problems. The company also plans to
cut 10% of its staff.
23 John Kerry fights fresh attacks on his war record, filing a Federal Election Commission complaint alleging
that the Vietnam group behind them is linked to the Bush campaign.

--- Page 92 ---
Page 92 of 265 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Gold futures rally to a four-month high of $415.50 an ounce, spurred by the climb in oil prices and concern
about the economy.
General Growth's plan to buy Rouse for $7.2 billion would create the largest U.S. mall company in number of
malls owned.
The Federal Communications Commission freezes for six months the rates regional Bell companies can
charge rivals to lease parts of their long-distance networks.
25 Top Pentagon officials are faulted in a report on prisoner abuse. An outside panel finds the worst
instances of abuse in Iraqi prisons were committed by a handful of low-ranking soldiers, but says senior
civilian and military officials at the Pentagon also are at fault for not providing sufficient resources or
oversight.
One Russian airliner crashes and another goes missing with a combined 89 people aboard after taking off
from a Moscow airport. President Vladimir Putin orders the nation's top intelligence agency to investigate
amid fears of terrorism ahead of Chechen elections.
The war-crimes tribunal opens in Guantanamo Bay, where the attorney defending bin Laden's driver question
the trial's legitimacy.
Sumitomo Mitsui offers to buy UFJ for $29 billion in a challenge to rival bank Mitsubishi Tokyo. The offer,
which amounts to about 23% more than UFJ's market value, threatens to escalate Japan's first bank-takeover
battle into a bidding war.

Year-End Review of Markets &Finance 2004
ß--- -7-
Shell is being fined about $150 million by U.S. and British authorities for overstating its reserves of oil and
natural gas.
26 Kaiser Permanente is reconsidering its use of Merck's blockbuster arthritis medicine Vioxx after a major
study of the HMO's patients found the drug may increase the risk of serious heart problems.
27 Grand Ayatollah Ali Sistani forges a peace deal, accepted by Muqtada al-Sadr and Iraqi leaders. The
five-point agreement calls for Najaf and Kufa to be declared weapons-free, all foreign forces to withdraw from
Najaf, police to be in charge of security, the government to compensate victims of fighting and a census to be
taken before elections.
Moscow sets a starting price of $1.93 billion for its 7.6% stake in oil giant Lukoil.
30 Republicans gather for their national convention amid antiwar protests. A New York official estimates
120,000 took part in a tightly policed demonstration; organizers contend the turnout was more than triple that.
Alan Greenspan warns that the U.S. may have promised retirees more in Social Security and Medicare
benefits than it can deliver and urges policy makers to "recalibrate" benefits without raising taxes.
31 Merck is hit by a disappointing study of its cholesterol drug Zocor, a setback to the drug maker amid
intense competition from rivals. The study found large doses of the drug for patients at a high risk of a heart
attack didn't yield better results than a placebo or less-aggressive Zocor treatment and raised the risk of a
dangerous side effect.
SEPTEMBER
1 Iraq insurgents post a Web video of 12 Nepalese construction workers being executed.
Suicide bombers blow up two Israeli buses in Beersheeba, killing 16, injuring more than 80 and ending a
six-month lull in assaults of such magnitude. Hamas claims responsibility.
A Hollinger probe finds former CEO Conrad Black and ex-President David Radler siphoned off more than
$400 million through "aggressive looting" of the publishing company, in what amounted to a "corporate
kleptocracy." The two executives quit in 2003 when the company alleged they and other officials had received
$32 million of unauthorized payments.

--- Page 93 ---
Page 93 of 265 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
A federal judge orders the release of $60 million in compensation owed to Freddie Mac's former chief
executive, a blow to mortgage-company's regulator, the Office of Federal Housing Enterprise Oversight.
The World Trade Organization clears the way for U.S. trading partners to impose punitive tariffs on U.S.
exports.
2 Iran tells the United Nations it will convert 37 tons of yellowcake to uranium hexafluoride as part of a
production "test." Experts estimate that further enrichment could yield five atomic bombs.
Malaysia's high court throws out the sodomy conviction against former Premier Anwar Ibrahim and orders him
freed from prison after six years.
GM and Ford announce fourth-quarter output cuts, following a weak August in which SUV sales plunged amid
high oil prices.
Time Warner raises the stakes in the auction for MGM, making an all-cash bid of as much as $4.6 billion.
3 Intel cuts its forecast for third-quarter revenue and profit margins, surprising analysts and sending shares
down nearly 8% in afterhours trading. The company said sales of microprocessors were hurt by weak
demand world-wide and moves by computer makers to reduce their component inventories.
7 Tiny coffins wring howls of grief near Chechnya after a school siege in Russia's North Ossetia region ends
in explosions and gunfire, leaving hundreds dead.
Hurricane Frances weakens over Georgia after leaving five dead and insured damage estimated at $6 billion
in Florida. Cape Canaveral gets a battering.
The Army plans to move within months to break up Halliburton's as much as $13 billion Iraq support-services
contract, which has been plagued by accounting turmoil and accusations of overcharging, and put out the
work for competitive bid.
8 The U.S. Iraq war tally surpasses 1,000. The death toll includes 1,000 troops and three civilians and doesn't
distinguish between combat and nonhostile causes. In another grim milestone, more than 100 foreign
hostages have been taken.
US Airways suffers a setback in its bid to avoid Chapter 11, as a faction of its pilots union's leadership council
blocks a plan to put a cost-cutting proposal out for a vote. The airline has been seeking $800 million in annual
concessions from its unionized workers.
Russia's Tax Ministry sends Yukos and its banks a collection order for $2.7 billion in back taxes and interest
owed for 2001.
9 Bushmaster settles a suit by Washington-area sniper victims, reportedly the first time a gun maker paid
damages in a negligent-distribution case.
A Pentagon review panel finds for the first time a Guantanamo Bay detainee was wrongly classified "enemy
combatant" and orders him sent home.
Delta's chief executive unveils a turnaround plan aimed at cutting annual costs by $5 billion in a bid to avert a
bankruptcy filing. The strategy includes increasing the airline's long-haul and international flights, abandoning
its Dallas-Fort Worth hub and slashing 7,000 jobs, or about 10% of its work force.
Frank Quattrone is sentenced to 18 months in prison for obstructing a government probe into how CSFB
allocated IPO shares.
10 South Korea expands disclosures of past secret nuclear work, saying its scientists ran plutonium
experiments in the early 1980s.
Michael Eisner says he will step down as Disney's CEO when his contract expires in two years.
13 US Airways files for bankruptcy-court protection for the second time in 25 months, after running out of time
in its efforts to wrest savings from its unions as it faced crucial financial hurdles.
Qwest Communications International agrees to a preliminary $250 million settlement with the SEC over
improper accounting, while ex-Chief Executive Joseph Nacchio soon may face civil charges.
WPP agrees to purchase Grey Global for $1.52 billion, further consolidating the global advertising industry.

--- Page 94 ---
Page 94 of 265 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Riggs Bank was accused in a suit of contributing to the Sept. 11 attacks through negligence by allegedly
letting funds be sent to terrorists.
14 A Sony-led group strikes a tentative deal to acquire MGM for almost $3 billion plus the assumption of $1.9
billion in debt. Sony also agrees to license its movies to Comcast and develop cable networks together.
15 Israel approves payments of as much as $115,000 for settlers willing to depart Gaza and unauthorized
West Bank outposts.
Russian President Vladimir Putin approves a plan to combine state oil company Rosneft with
Kremlin-controlled natural-gas giant Gazprom. The move would cement the government's control over
Russia's vital energy industry and create the world's largest energy company open to Western investors, with
about five times the reserves of Exxon Mobil.
16 Dow Jones plans to launch a weekend edition of The Wall Street Journal next September.
17 Hurricane Ivan visits destruction across four Gulf states and leaves at least 20 dead. Florida's panhandle
fares worst.
A Boeing ex-manager agrees in principle to plead guilty to conspiring to illegally obtain a rival company's
proprietary documents in the late 1990s.
20 The Security Council votes to threaten Sudan with oil sanctions unless Darfur attacks end.
Tropical Storm Jeanne kills 90 in Haiti, bringing its Caribbean toll to 100. The next day, Haiti reports flooding
from the storm kills 573. A week later, Hurricane Jeanne tears into Florida, causing heavy damage and
leaving several dead.
21 CBS and Dan Rather apologize and concede they no longer can defend the authenticity of files used to
raise questions on President Bush's service.
Fannie Mae's regulator outlines the results of its accounting probe, finding a pattern of decisions aimed at
manipulating profit.
Hynix Semiconductor faces a possible fine after South Korean regulators found $1.73 billion of accounting
irregularities at the chip maker.
22 The Justice Department agrees to defer prosecution of Computer Associates in its probe of accounting
fraud. Under terms of the accord, charges would be dropped if the software maker enacts certain reforms.
The Fed raises rates by another quarter point, to 1.75%, saying the economy "appears to have regained
some traction" and that inflation fears have eased.
The SEC warns American International Group that it could face civil action over deals the agency says let
PNC keep bad loans off the bank's books.
Spain's BBVA is buying Laredo National of Texas for $850 million as part of its drive to tap the U.S.'s growing
Hispanic banking market.
23 Iraq insurgents show the beheading of American Jack Hensley. NATO agrees to train police within Iraq.
The U.S. signs off on the Yaser Esam Hamdi deal. Under it, there will be no prosecution. The "enemy
combatant" held without charge since his 2001 capture in Afghanistan will go to Saudi Arabia and give up his
U.S. citizenship.
Sanjay Kumar is indicted for allegedly helping to orchestrate a widespread accounting fraud at Computer
Associates and then lying to investigators. The software maker's ex-CEO, along with its former sales head,
face charges including securities fraud and obstruction.
Fannie Mae's regulator accuses the mortgage-finance giant of a wide range of improper accounting practices
in a report that follows an eight-month probe.
24 Congress gives final approval to a package of $146 billion in tax cuts for the middle class and business.
Halliburton says it is restructuring its Kellogg Brown &Root subsidiary and will consider selling it or spinning it
off.
27 Pakistani police kill Amjad Hussain Farooqi, an al Qaeda assassin wanted for his role in the 2002 slaying
of Journal reporter Daniel Pearl as well as two attempts last year on President Musharraf's life.

Fannie Mae may fall well below the minimum federal requirement for regulatory capital due to its accounting
woes. Based on numbers from 2003, the mortgage firm may have been about $4.6 billion below the
minimum. Fannie and its regulator, Ofheo, hold talks on a significant increase in the firm's minimum capital
requirements.
China Minmetals is in exclusive talks to acquire Canadian mining concern Noranda for a likely price of more
than $5 billion.
28 Fannie Mae agrees to a long list of demands by its regulator, including new accounting practices and
higher capital requirements. The accord will curb the mortgage company's growth prospects and profitability.
Two days later, the Justice Department opens an investigation of possible accounting fraud at Fannie Mae.
The Dow industrials fall 58.70 to 9988.54, their first close below 10000 since Aug. 17.
NBC says Conan O'Brien will succeed Jay Leno as host of "The Tonight Show" in 2009.
29 Oil tops $50 before settling at $49.90, as worries over supply disruptions in producer nations undermine a

Roy Disney and Stanley Gold suspend efforts to oust Disney CEO Eisner, citing a board pledge to name a
successor by June.
30 IBM agrees to pay $300 million to thousands of older current and former employees to partially settle
pension-plan litigation.
OCTOBER
1 Iraq bombs kill 35 children gathered for candy from U.S. troops. Abu Musab al-Zarqawi's group claims
responsibility for the atrocity.
Israel mounts a massive military raid in northern Gaza, where rockets that killed two Israeli children were
fired. Fighting leaves 28 Palestinians dead, the biggest one-day toll in 30 months.
The Russian cabinet sends the Duma the Kyoto Protocol. In November, the Legislature ratifies the
global-warming treaty, putting the accord into effect despite U.S. opposition and sounding the starting bell for
a global market in emissions credits.
Merck pulls Vioxx from the market after a clinical trial found that patients who used the painkiller for more
than 18 months were twice as likely to have a heart attack or stroke. The news sends Merck shares plunging
27% to $33, erasing almost $27 billion of its market capitalization and raising questions about the drug
maker's future.
4 PeopleSoft fires Chief Executive Craig Conway, leader of the software company's resistance to Oracle's
$7.7 billion hostile takeover bid.
6 The European Union will recommend inviting Turkey to join, but talks may take 10 years and attached
conditions risk bruising Turkish pride.
U.S. officials urge doctors to ration flu vaccines after British regulators shut down a plant operated by Chiron,
a principal supplier. The U.S. company said it wouldn't fulfill orders in any market. The shutdown deals a huge
blow to global public-health efforts just as the flu season gets under way in many countries. U.S. prosecutors
launch an investigation into Chiron's vaccine production.
7 After failing to reach an accord in talks, the U.S. and Europe sue each other at the World Trade
Organization over the government subsidies paid to their respective aircraft giants, Boeing and Airbus, in a
trade fight certain to rattle the multibillion-dollar aerospace industry.
Howard Stern agrees to join Sirius Satellite Radio in a $500 million deal after his contract with Viacom's
Infinity expires.

--- Page 96 ---
Page 96 of 265 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Fannie Mae denounces its regulator's methods of examining its accounting and rebuts charges it manipulated
its books to make profit growth seem smoother.
AT&T announces cuts of 7,000 more jobs in its retreat from the consumer market. The long-distance giant will
also write off $11.4 billion in assets.
8 Bombs devastate Egyptian Red Sea resorts filled with Israelis on the last day of the Jewish festival of
Sukkot, killing at least 35 people and wounding more than 160.
12 Congress passes a huge corporate-tax bill that repeals a controversial export subsidy and includes about
$100 billion in new relief.
The Nobel prize in economics is awarded to Edward C. Prescott, a researcher with the Minneapolis Federal
Reserve, and his collaborator, Norwegian-born Finn Kydland.
13 Russia announces plans to sell a big stake in Yukos's main oil-production unit, fueling fears the Kremlin
will sell the assets to a Kremlin ally at a knockdown price.
14 A six-month delay is announced in a Financial Accounting Standards Board plan requiring companies to
treat stock-option compensation as an expense that counts against earnings.
The SEC settles civil charges against Ahold without levying any fine against the Dutch supermarket operator
over its accounting practices.
15 Marsh &McLennan is accused of cheating corporate clients by rigging bids and collecting fees from
insurers for throwing business their way. The allegations are contained in a civil suit filed against the
insurance broker by New York Attorney General Spitzer. The suit also names insurers American International
Group, Ace, Hartford Financial and Munich-American Risk Partners as participants.
General Motors, reporting net rose 3.5% in the third quarter, slashes its 2004 profit forecast and unveils plans
to cut jobs at its European operations. Standard &Poor's cuts its rating on GM debt to one notch above "junk"
status.
18 The FDA orders "black-box" label warnings, its strongest, to detail the risks of adolescent suicide linked to
antidepressants.
20 Citigroup ousts Vice Chairman Deryck Maughan and two other senior executives in connection with a
scandal over its private-banking operations in Japan. The departures represent the most decisive move thus
far by Chief Executive Charles Prince to set a new tone at the financial-services firm, which has been
battered by regulatory problems.
21 Rafiq Hariri dissolves Lebanon's cabinet, and the prime minister says he won't lead the next government,
a surprise move that could bolster Syria's role in Lebanese affairs.
22 A soldier draws an eight-year sentence for abuses at Abu Ghraib; Sgt. Ivan Frederick's lawyer calls the
punishment, the most severe yet, "excessive" and blames the military for inadequate training and supervision.
Western Japan digs out from wreckage left by Typhoon Tokage, the most powerful to hit the area in decades.
At least 67 died and 21 are missing. The following week, Japan is jolted by a 6.8 magnitude quake, deadliest
since the 1995 Kobe disaster. It leaves at least 23 dead and widespread damage, including a bullet-train
derailment.
American International Group is the target of a grand-jury probe into insurance-policy sales that prosecutors
believe were aimed at helping companies improperly smooth earnings.
The SEC alleges Qwest Communications engaged in pervasive fraud led by top management; the
telecommunications company agrees to pay a $250 million penalty.
Italian prosecutors put Citigroup, Deutsche Bank, UBS and Morgan Stanley under investigation over the
alleged Parmalat fraud.
Merck's earnings fall 29% on a Vioxx-related write-off.
Crude oil settles at $55.17, another Nymex high.
25 Insurgents massacre dozens of Iraqi soldiers, unarmed on leave. In one of the most horrendous acts
aimed at choking off cooperation with the U.S., the troops' buses are ambushed 95 miles east of Baghdad
and nearly 50 were executed with shots to the head. The Zarqawi group claims responsibility.

--- Page 97 ---
Page 97 of 265 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
26 Jeffrey Greenberg quits as Marsh &McLennan's chairman and chief executive under pressure from
prosecutors in the bid-rigging case. Michael Cherkasky, the former head of the company's Kroll investigations
unit, will succeed him. The insurance broker plans to announce it will no longer accept commissions from
insurers to whom it steers business.
27 An Iraq-explosives debate rages as Vice President Dick Cheney says it is unclear if a dangerous cache
still was where U.N. inspectors left it prior to the invasion, feeding speculation Saddam Hussein may have
moved it.
27 Budget carrier ATA Holdings files for bankruptcy-court protection, hobbled by record fuel prices, intense
competition and costly aircraft-lease payments.
Shopping.com stock rises 60% to close at $28.80 on the first day of trading in the shares of the online
price-comparison company.
28 The Boston Red Sox, 3-0, sweep the St. Louis Cardinals to end an 86-year World Series title drought. The
Red Sox never lost after the New York Yankees had them one game from elimination.
Delta Air Lines and its pilots union reach a tentative accord for about $1 billion in wage and benefit cuts,
sparing the airline a bankruptcy-court filing.
DreamWorks prices its initial public offering at a higher-than-expected $28 a share, raising $812 million for
the animation studio. In the first day of trading, the shares rise 38% from the offering price, giving the
animation firm a valuation near that of rival Pixar Animation Studios.
29 Auto retailers say they no longer will fill their lots with slow-selling vehicles to help Detroit avoid production
cuts. General Motors and Ford Motor already are planning to cut overtime and temporarily lay off thousands
of workers. Industry executives expect even more cuts.
Shell says its Dutch and British parents will merge under one board and CEO. It also warns more cuts in
energy reserves are possible.
China raises interest rates for the first time in nine years, signaling a more market-driven approach to
cooling its economy.
NOVEMBER
1 An Osama bin Laden video with advice to American voters elicits bipartisan revulsion but little indication it is
an election-tipping "October surprise." A Wall Street Journal/NBC poll puts the race at 48%-47%, with
President Bush in front a statistically insignificant hair over John Kerry.

Merck fought for years to keep safety concerns from destroying Vioxx's commercial prospects, according to
company e-mail and documents and outside scientists. The drug maker's public statements continued to
reject a connection between the painkiller and increased heart risk, even after its research chief
acknowledged the link.
2 Toyota Motor says it plans to expand annual sales to 8.5 million vehicles by 2006, putting it on track to
challenge General Motors as the world's No. 1 car producer by volume.
Moscow slaps Yukos with $10 billion in new back-tax bills, bringing the total amount of tax claims against the
company to $17.6 billion, an amount near the asking price for its main production unit.
3 A Dutch filmmaker is shot dead in Amsterdam. Theo van Gogh had received threats after his "Submission"
criticized Muslim women's treatment.
4 Hamid Karzai is declared the winner of Afghanistan's Oct. 9 presidential vote.
The United Nations reports strong evidence of large-scale, systemic war crimes in Sudan.

Page 98 of 265 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
A jury finds four former Merrill bankers and an Enron ex-executive guilty of conspiracy and fraud in
connection with a scheme to inflate the energy firm's 1999 earnings. The verdicts in the first Enron criminal
trial involve the purported sale by Enron to Merrill of an interest in barges off the coast of Nigeria.
Gold Fields rejects a $7.2 billion takeover offer from Harmony Gold Mining, calling the rival South African
gold producer "financially stretched."
Time Warner sets aside $500 million to cover costs related to America Online accounting probes and will
restate 2000 and 2001 results.
5 Constellation Brands strikes a $1.03 billion deal for Robert Mondavi Corp., effectively ending the reign of a
family that has become synonymous with California's Napa Valley winemaking region. Constellation, the
world's largest wine marketer, now will control about 20% of wine sold in the U.S.
8 Fighting in Iraq sharpens as U.S. forces move on Fallujah insurgents. A force of 10,000 Marines and
soldiers seizes western parts of the city, including the main hospital and two Euphrates bridges.
The U.S. imported more agricultural goods than it exported in June and August, marking the first monthly
trade deficits for the farm sector since 1986.
Halliburton acknowledges bribes may have been paid to Nigerian officials in connection with a
natural-gas-plant contract.
9 Merck announces the Justice Department has launched a criminal investigation into the drug maker's
handling of Vioxx and that the SEC has begun an informal probe. The company warns the conclusions could
have an adverse effect on its financial position and liquidity.
CBS, Fox and DirecTV agree to pay a total of $11.5 billion to retain TV rights to the National Football League,
a 40% increase from current contracts.
10 Marsh &McLennan, scrambling to contend with bid-rigging charges against its insurance-brokerage unit,
says it will lay off 3,000 employees amid a 94% plunge in earnings.
11 Yasser Arafat, 75, dies at a Paris hospital, ending a life embodying a dream of Palestinian statehood that
still is distant.
The Federal Reserve raises interest rates a quarter point to 2%, its fourth increase this year, and leaves the
door open for more.
PeopleSoft's board rejected Oracle's new $9.2 billion bid, arguing that the software firm's business now is
worth substantially more.
12 Intel names Paul Otellini to succeed Craig Barrett as chief executive; Martha Stewart Living picks Susan
Lyne, a former ABC executive, as its president and CEO.
15 Wm. Wrigley Jr. will buy Kraft Foods's Life Savers and Altoids businesses for close to $1.5 billion after an
auction that involves rivals Hershey Foods, Mars, Nestle and Cadbury Schweppes.
Dow Jones agrees to pay $519 million to buy MarketWatch, parent of the financial-news Web site CBS
MarketWatch.
16 Fannie Mae estimates it will have to post a $9 billion loss if the SEC finds it has been accounting
improperly for derivatives. Regulator Ofheo says Fannie incorrectly applied accounting rules in a way that let
the mortgage company spread out losses over many years rather than taking an immediate hit.
Pension Benefit Guaranty Corp. says its long-term deficit more than doubled in 2004, under pressure from
failing airline plans.
Boeing's former finance chief, Michael Sears, pleads guilty to illegally negotiating a job for former Air Force
acquisition official Darleen Druyun.
17 U.S. forces sweep Mosul, retaking police stations from insurgents. About 1,200 troops take part in the
offensive in the northern Iraq city of one million, where guerrillas emerge in force during the heat of the
Fallujah battle. The British family of Margaret Hassan accepts she is dead after a video of the longtime Iraq
aid worker's execution surfaced.
18 Kmart Holding agrees to buy Sears Roebuck in an $11.5 billion deal that will create the No. 3 U.S. retailer.

Page 99 of 265 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
19 Food and Drug Administration official David Graham criticizes the agency's approach to drug risks, saying
it discounts recommendations from its own researchers and doesn't give sufficient weight to safety concerns
once drugs are approved. At a hearing on Merck's Vioxx, Mr. Graham cites five other drugs on the market he

Nike founder Philip Knight resigns as president and chief executive. William Perez, chief of S.C. Johnson, will

Viacom's former president, Mel Karmazin, will join satellite-radio company Sirius as chief executive.
22 Russia puts Yukos's core asset up for auction with a starting price of $8.7 billion, signaling the oil

23 Ukraine protests flare as Kuchma-backed presidential candidate Viktor Yanukovych claims victory over
Western-leaning opposition chief Viktor Yushchenko, whom a U.S.-backed poll projects as the winner. The

McDonald's Chief Executive Charlie Bell resigns amid a fight with cancer, the second change at the
company's helm since April. Vice Chairman Jim Skinner will succeed him.
Computer Associates picks John Swainson, a 26-year veteran of rival IBM, to be the software maker's next
chief executive.
24 Hank Greenberg is being investigated by U.S. prosecutors as to whether the AIG chairman manipulated
the insurer's share price in 2001 to save money on the company's takeover offer for American General. Mr.
Greenberg allegedly called the office of then-NYSE head Dick Grasso to ask him to help shore up the stock's
sinking price.
AIG says separately it reached tentative settlements with the Justice Department and SEC over transactions
that allegedly helped clients commit accounting fraud.
Viacom agrees to a record $3.5 million settlement with the FCC to expunge all but one pending indecency
complaint.
Dan Rather announces he will step down as anchor of the "CBS Evening News" on March 9, 2005, after 24
years. No successor is named.
29 Iran agrees to completely suspend uranium enrichment, dropping a demand to keep 20 centrifuges
running, Western diplomats say.
Many retailers report surprisingly strong sales for the Thanksgiving weekend amid near-record crowds.
Results were best at high-end stores; online sales also climbed. Wal-Mart Stores slashes its prediction for
November growth, citing disappointing holiday sales.
DECEMBER
1 Tom Ridge will step down as secretary of Homeland Security. He plans to stay on until at least Feb. 1 and
says the U.S. is safer than two years ago, though much remains to be accomplished, including international
cooperation and private investment to protect infrastructure.
Philippine floods kill about 340 people, and 150 are missing. Rescuers try to reach three coastal towns before
an expected typhoon.
Ken Jennings's streak ends on a question about H&R Block. The "Jeopardy" contestant shattered TV
game-show records by winning $2,520,700.
Corporate profits fell 2.4% in the third quarter from the second, the largest quarterly decline since the third
period of 2001 on a seasonally adjusted basis. Results were hurt by rising raw-material prices, creeping labor
costs and bad weather.
2 The U.S. will bolster its military force in Iraq by 12,000. The increase is set to last until March and will raise
troop numbers to 150,000, higher than at any point since the invasion. The expansion will include extended
tours and fresh soldiers.
The Centers for Disease Control and Prevention says nearly one million Americans have the AIDS virus. The
rate of new cases is steady despite increased efforts to fight its spread.

--- Page 100 ---
Page 100 of 265 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The California Public Employees' Retirement System, or Calpers, replaces Sean Harrigan as president of its
board, but says his removal won't have a major effect on the pension fund's corporate-governance
campaigns.
3 Retailers report mostly disappointing sales for November, forcing them to ponder bigger markdowns and
clearance events.
A U.S. appeals court rejects a proposed $1.2 billion asbestos-liability settlement plan for a U.S. unit of
Switzerland's ABB, saying the proposal was unfair because it doesn't treat all plaintiffs the same.
6 Gunmen kill 17 Iraqis going to work at a U.S. ammunition dump. The ambush on a bus carrying unarmed
passengers near Tikrit lifts the toll from three days of attacks to at least 70 Iraqi dead and dozens hurt in an
apparent campaign to frighten people who cooperate with the U.S.
Attackers strike the U.S. Consulate in Jidda, Saudi Arabia. Five non-American employees are killed in an
incident that further strains U.S.-Saudi ties. An al Qaeda faction claims responsibility in a Web site posting.
7 The World Trade Center attacks were two occurrences, a jury rules, entitling developer Larry Silverstein to
a double insurance payout and potentially jumpstarting ambitious plans to build five office towers around
Ground Zero in a program that could cost upward of $9 billion.

8 Eliot Spitzer says he will run for New York governor in 2006. The state attorney general has gained renown
for high-profile investigations of securities and insurance companies.
International Business Machines agrees to sell its personal-computer business to Lenovo for $1.25 billion in
cash and stock. As part of the deal, IBM will get an 18.9% stake in its Chinese rival.
9 The Senate votes 89-2 to approve legislation overhauling U.S. intelligence-gathering, clearing the way for
Bush's expected signature.
General Motors agrees with European unions to eliminate thousands of jobs and rolled out a national sales
promotion in the U.S., as it intensified efforts to bolster performance in its global auto operations.
13 The SEC isn't expected to file civil-securities charges against former Global Crossing Chairman Gary

General Electric authorizes a stock buyback of as much as $15 billion and raises its dividend.
14 A suicide bomber kills 13 Iraqis near a government compound. Abu Musab al-Zarqawi's group claims
responsibility for the attack on people waiting to enter Baghdad's Green Zone, home to the U.S. Embassy
and the Iraqi government.
President Bush nominates Environmental Protection Agency head Michael Leavitt to be secretary of the
Department of Health and Human Services. Officials play down Bernard Kerik's withdrawal of his Homeland
Security nomination and defend the vetting process. NASA chief Sean O'Keefe resigns. William Rehnquist
indicates he has no plans to leave the Supreme Court as he fights cancer.
A California jury recommends the death sentence for Scott Peterson for murdering his pregnant wife. Formal
sentencing will be on Feb. 25.
PeopleSoft agrees to be acquired by Oracle after Oracle sweetened its bid to $10.3 billion, or $26.50 a share,
66% more than its initial offer in June 2003. The deal will leave Oracle and SAP as the only two big suppliers
of software that companies use to run their business functions.
15 The Fed raises its key interest-rate target a quarter point to 2.25%, its fifth increase this year. The central
bank says the economy is expanding at a "moderate pace" despite high energy prices and inflation remains
well-contained. It reaffirms its intention to raise rates at a "measured" pace.

20 Fresh Iraq assaults appear to target election plans. Bombs kill 67 people attending a funeral in Najaf or
waiting for buses at a crowded Karbala station. In Baghdad, unmasked gunmen kill three election workers in
broad daylight on a busy street.
Pfizer decides to keep Celebrex on the market after a study links high doses of the painkiller to greater
heart-attack risks. The FDA asks the company to suspend its use of direct-to-consumer advertising and alter
its marketing.
A little-known Russian firm, Baikal Finance Group, wins a government auction for Yukos's oil-producing
assets after favorite Gazprom declines to bid.
21 A Chilean appeals court upholds the indictment and house arrest of ailing ex-dictator Augusto Pinochet for
abuses of human rights under his 1973-90 rule.
Edward D. Jones agrees to pay $75 million to settle charges that it steered investors to funds without
disclosing it received payments.
Exelon agrees to acquire Public Service Enterprise Group for $12 billion in stock plus the assumption of
$14.1 billion in debt, creating a utility giant.
22 The U.S. suffers its deadliest attack since the war in Iraq started. The Pentagon initially says a
122-millimeter rocket struck a crowded mess tent at a base near Mosul, but suspicion soon shifts to a suicide
bomber. The attack kills 22, including 14 U.S. soldiers. Scores more are wounded. The Ansar al-Sunnah
Army, a Sunni group, later claims responsibility.
The White House vows full investigation of new evidence of Guantanamo and Iraq prisoner abuse, and
apparent efforts to cover it up, laid out in documents the Army and FBI were forced to give to the American
Civil Liberties Union.
Iran continues to process uranium, stopping just short of the enrichment step that would violate its recent
accord with EU nations, diplomats say.
Fannie Mae's CEO, Franklin Raines, and Timothy Howard, the chief financial officer, step down amid growing
pressure from regulators over accounting violations. Daniel Mudd will be interim CEO, Robert Levin will serve
as interim finance chief. The mortgage company's board also dismisses KPMG as outside auditor.
23 Russia's state-owned oil company, Rosneft, buys the firm that acquired Yukos's core asset.
27 A massive undersea quake spawns a tsunami that devastates south Asia. The magnitude-9.0 temblor in
the Indian Ocean strikes off the Indonesian island of Sumatra and sends 20-foot waves into the Andaman
Sea and Bay of Bengal, resulting in an 11-country death toll that by year end is raised to more than 123,000,
with thousands assumed missing. Fatalities occur as far away as Somalia. U.N. health officials warn
waterborne diseases may exact an even bigger toll. Nations from around the world pledge aid, including $350
million from the U.S. An Italian scientist says the quake disturbed the Earth's rotation.
Ukrainian opposition leader Viktor Yushchenko claims victory in a presidential revote; officials soon announce
he took 52% of the vote, an eight-point victory over Prime Minister Viktor Yanukovych. Mr. Yanukovych
resigns as prime minister on New Year's Eve, after his appeal of the results is rejected by election officials.
He vows to appeal to Ukraine's Supreme Court.
Holiday sales rose 8.1% from 2003 to exceed retail industry expectations.
Comair and US Air travelers face canceled flights and lost luggage after weather, worker absences and
computer glitches left thousands stranded over the Christmas holiday weekend.
28 The Iraqi Islamic Party, the biggest Sunni political group, pulls out of Iraqi elections, saying violence in
areas where its constituents live precludes a fair vote, but stops short of calling for a boycott. A purported
tape by bin Laden names Zarqawi his "emir" in Iraq and urges a boycott of Jan. 30 voting.
The dollar sinks to a new low against the euro, which hit $1.3640 in intraday trading.
29 The Dow industrials rise 78.41 points to 10854.54, a fresh 3 1/2-year high.
Susan Sontag, 71, a leading intellectual whose rigorous social criticism provoked both admiration and
disregard, dies in New York, of leukemia.
30 Fannie Mae plans to sell about $5 billion in preferred stock, the largest capital placement ever by the
mortgage giant. The sale is aimed at shoring up Fannie's capital position after its regulator called it
undercapitalized.

Quarterly Mutual Funds Review --- Commodities ETFs Strike Gold --- StreetTRACKS Shows Way For Launch of More Funds, Which Offer Broad...
Quarterly Mutual Funds Review --- Commodities ETFs Strike Gold --- StreetTRACKS Shows Way For
Launch of More Funds, Which Offer Broad Exposure
By Tara Siegel Bernard Dow Jones Newswires
2,591 words
6 January 2005
The Wall Street Journal
J
R18
English
Copyright 2005 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
AN EXCHANGE-TRADED FUND tracking gold made a sparkling debut in the U.S. late last year -- and
investors can expect ETF players to continue to work busily in 2005 to bring more commodity-based ETFs to
market.
Exchange-traded funds, the increasingly popular vehicles that trade like stocks but resemble index-tracking
mutual funds, are available in a wide variety of stock flavors -- 143 to be exact. They range from those based
on large benchmarks such as the Standard &Poor's Depositary Receipt, or SPDR (pronounced "spider"), to
those tracking different sectors, geographic regions, countries and styles. There are also six funds available
tied to bonds.
However, a sinking dollar and lackluster stock-market returns have led investors to focus on commodities,
which tend to rise when the greenback falls and aren't synchronized with the stock and bond markets.
Financial advisers would most like to see an ETF created from a diversified basket of commodities -- perhaps
spanning precious metals, energy and agriculture. But there have been rumblings that silver might be next,
though there is also talk about oil, platinum and foreign currencies such as the yen.
Meanwhile, the New York Mercantile Exchange is constructing a series of metals and energy indexes that
could be used as ETF benchmarks, a Nymex spokeswoman says, though it still is exploring its options.
"Given that commodities aren't highly correlated with equities and fixed income, you find that there is interest
and demand for products that can provide exposure, especially since we are in a low-return environment for
traditional asset classes, i.e. equities and fixed income," said Deborah Fuhr, an ETF strategist for Morgan
Stanley in London. "That is the forecast right now."
But it isn't an easy product to deliver. Exchange-traded funds -- which had $211 billion in combined assets as
of Nov. 30 -- that track stock indexes are much easier to construct because stocks operate in the same way.
Commodities, on the other hand, are as different as the markets they encompass, from precious metals to
pork bellies. These funds need to be engineered slightly differently from traditional stock ETFs and come with
their own unique set of regulatory hurdles, ETF players say.
"Once you launch the first equity ETF, you've created the cookie-cutter template," said Gus Fleites, president
of State Street Global Advisors' mutual-fund group, the second-largest ETF player with nearly $71.2 billion in
ETF assets in the U.S. as of Nov. 30. "The problem with commodity ETFs are big structural issues. What they
launch with gold may not be applicable with silver or oil."
For instance, if an ETF linked to a diversified basket of commodities were developed, it probably would be
more derivatives-based rather than linked to the physical commodity, given the complexity of creating such a
product, Mr. Fleites says. "It's much simpler to take delivery of a financial instrument rather than the actual
commodity."
"There is no simple system for all commodities," added Lee Kranefuss, who heads the ETF business at
Barclays PLC's Barclays Global Investors, the largest ETF provider with about $107 billion in assets. "The
nuts and bolts of owning it, taking delivery of it and accounting for each one is different."
Though it took two years to gain regulatory approval from the Securities and Exchange Commission, the code
recently was cracked for a gold-backed ETF. The first commodity-tracking ETF in the U.S -- called
streetTRACKS Gold Shares -- was launched in November by the World Gold Council and its marketing
agent, State Street Global Markets. It gives investors the opportunity to invest in shares backed by actual
gold without requiring custody of the metal, which can be expensive. Each share, listed on the New York
Stock Exchange, represents one-tenth of an ounce of gold bullion as priced by the London Bullion Market
Association.

--- Page 104 ---
Page 104 of 265 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Barclays Global Investors is expected soon to launch its own version of a gold ETF. Its iShares Comex Gold
Trust, to be listed on the American Stock Exchange, also will be equal to one-tenth of an ounce of gold, as
measured by the price of spot month gold futures announced by Comex, a division of the New York
Mercantile Exchange, according to a May filing with the SEC.
The initial success of the streetTRACKS Gold Shares wowed the ETF community, as it collected more than
$1 billion in assets within three days of trading and was averaging a daily volume of more than six million
shares, according to the NYSE. As of Jan. 4, it had $1.25 billion in assets with an average daily volume of
about 4.3 million shares.
"Word on the street is that there are more products in registration," said Herb Blank, president of QED
International, a New York consulting firm, adding that he believes the next logical candidate might be crude
oil. But while such an ETF would raise many questions -- among them, whether to track the commodity or its
futures, where one might store the commodity and how investors would be taxed on these investments --
none are unsolvable, he says. "An index of futures for petroleum would be a much more feasible and
manageable way of doing things."
Robert McSweeney, senior vice president for competitive position at the New York Stock Exchange, added
that the exchange is "aware that issuers are in the process of creating securities based on the value of other
commodities and we certainly would be interested in listing them if they receive SEC approval."
While the two largest ETF providers say they are interested in exploring more commodity-related funds, they
declined to comment on what they may be working on specifically. "We are always looking at those areas
where you see investor interest," State Street Global Advisors' Mr. Fleites said. Barclays's Mr. Kranefuss
added: "We are generally interested in commodities as a whole, not just precious metals, but other areas as
well."
As it stands now, the easiest way for retail investors to gain exposure to commodities markets is through
mutual funds that either invest in companies that are influenced by commodities -- for instance, those that
collect, refine or market commodities such as oil, natural gas, metals or paper. There are also a couple of
funds that invest directly in commodities, largely through futures contracts, such as the Pimco Commodity
RealReturn Strategy and Oppenheimer Real Asset Fund, says Sonya Morris, a fund analyst at Morningstar
Inc.
And it appears that the demand is there, at least among some financial advisers, for a diversified product
where they can park about 5% or so of their clients' assets.
"The reason is, right now, the only other alternative asset class other than stocks, bonds, and cash are real
estate investment trusts," said Helen Modly, a principal and certified financial planner with Focus Financial
Consultants in Middleburg, Va., adding that both REITS and commodities tend to do well in inflationary
periods. "Since the market looks like it will be less robust than it has been in the last decade, you have to find
little pieces where you can add juice. I wouldn't make any big bets in commodities because they are more
volatile and subject to more variables than traditional asset classes, but I would allocate a small portion."
An ETF would give her that option because as a fee-only adviser, she doesn't use commission-based
products, which precludes her from using certain mutual funds. But if and when these products become
available still remains to be seen. "The key point is that there are more kinds of unique complexities
associated with each individual product," said Cliff Weber, senior vice president of the ETF Marketplace at the
American Stock Exchange. "It's a complicated process and will take more time, but there is a lot of demand
for [commodity-based ETFs] and a lot of attention focused on them, so I think it will get done."
