Stocks Climb on Retail-Sales Report and Merger News
Monday's Markets
Stocks Climb on Retail-Sales Report and Merger News
By Scott Patterson The Wall Street Journal Online
512 words
14 December 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
HOLIDAY SHOPPERS and deal-hungry chief executives have been in a spending mind-set, and investors
cottoned to their way of thinking yesterday, pushing stocks to highs not seen in months.
Shares were driven up across the board by an upbeat retail-sales report and a flurry of merger news,
including Oracle finally lassoing PeopleSoft after more than a year of wrangling. Shares of both software
makers surged 10%.
The Dow JonesIndustrial Average rose 95.10 points, or 0.9%, to 10638.32, its highest close in nine months.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index rose by the same percentage, or 10.68 points, to 1198.68, a
three-year high. The Nasdaq Composite Index rose 0.96% to 2148.50.
"There's a confidence level that's being generated by corporations willing to expand their business through
acquisitions," said Ned Riley, chief investment strategist at State Street Global Advisors in Boston. Despite a
choppy start to the holiday season, consumers have been festive. Excluding autos, retail sales were up more
than expected last month, and October sales were revised sharply higher.
While shoppers were snapping up gifts and gadgets, captains of industry were on a buying spree themselves.
Just a few of the blue chips tied up in deals include Dow components Johnson &Johnson, IBM and
Honeywell. The recent surge of mergers and acquisitions, fueled by the hoards of cash sitting in corporate
coffers, has pumped optimism into the markets.
Charles R. Geisst, a professor at Manhattan College and author of "Deals of the Century: Wall Street,
Mergers and the Making of Modern America," said today's deals differ from during the dot-com bubble.
While Oracle dominated Monday's session, today's will fall under the shadow of Federal Reserve Chairman
Alan Greenspan. The Fed is expected to raise its target for short-term interest rates by a quarter percentage
point to 2.25%. Investors will be more interested in divining if the Fed's comments hint at a "moderate pace"
of further rate increases.
Oil futures, which have fallen 25% since peaking in October, rose 30 cents to $41.01 a barrel.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks rose. On the Big Board, where 1.4 billion shares traded, 2,250 stocks rose, and 1,024 fell.
Bonds gained. The 10-year Treasury note was flat, the yield holding at 4.153%.
The dollar weakened to 104.85 yen, down from 105.16; the euro rose to $1.3306 from $1.3226.

A Cautionary Word on U.S. Bonds --- Money Managers Say Overseas, Commodity Funds Provide Better Returns
Personal Finance
A Cautionary Word on U.S. Bonds --- Money Managers Say Overseas, Commodity Funds Provide
Better Returns
By Christopher Oster
1,006 words
14 December 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
D2
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Investors would be wise to rethink their fixed-income strategies.
Big money managers are cautioning investors to revamp their approach to bonds because of a falling dollar
and rising U.S. inflation. Fund managers say investors should take the recent sharp rise in long-term bond
yields as sign to start shifting their portfolios, even from traditional holdings like U.S. Treasurys and into
foreign bonds that will benefit if the dollar weakens further. The rise in yields just after Thanksgiving has
reversed, but many money managers say the rate drop isn't enough to change the longer-term picture.
It's a theme emerging among many fund managers and financial advisers: Rethink the kind of U.S. bonds you
buy and take some of your fixed-income dollars overseas.
Although there are a few dissenters, notably Vanguard Group, a consensus seems to be forming that the
picture for investors has changed. Pimco Funds, the nation's second-biggest purveyor of bond mutual funds,
behind Vanguard, is one company cautioning investors. As recently as last summer, the company had
published promotional materials urging investors to "Stay the Course" and hold onto their U.S. bonds.
Now, looking toward 2005, says Bill Gross, Pimco's chief investment officer, bond investors need to "guard
against inflation and a sub par U.S. economy."
Investors should diversify "globally both in markets and currencies," Mr. Gross says. As the dollar
depreciates, threatening Americans' standard of living, he adds, "global diversification is one way to protect"
income and lifestyle. One approach is to use Treasury inflation-protected securities. "Commodities, including
gold, are attractive hedges as well," he says.
Professional investors have been waiting for bonds to end their bullish streak. "It's been a game of chicken for
the last year or year and a half," says Jeff Tyler, senior portfolio manager of the American Century Strategic
Allocation Funds, which have varying asset-allocation mixes. "Everybody's been going back and forth trying
to be the first guy out of the market when it looks poised to break."
Still, small investors shouldn't panic, fund managers say. There are some smart moves to be made to avoid
suffering from a further decline in prices. Bond prices go down as yields go up.
Mr. Tyler had prepared his portfolio for these changes over the last year, with a focus on short-term bonds
and a greater focus on stocks. Now, he says, he's shifting a bit more money out of domestic bonds and into
fixed-income instruments in Japan and Europe.
Over the past three months, the average world bond fund tracked by Morningstar has returned 6.10%, more
than any other bond category. Some international bond funds are up nearly 10% since Oct. 1, when the
dollar's decline steepened. The FFTW International Fund has climbed 9.44% in that period, Delaware Pooled
International Fixed Income is up 9.91% and GMO International Bond III is up 10.28%.
Commodity funds, meanwhile, have been on a tear, with the average natural-resource fund up 32% through
the end of November. Money managers think there are several reasons to expect commodities to continue to
do well, and in particular point to China's increasing demand for materials to fuel its rapid economic growth.
The best recent performer among commodity funds has been State Street Global Resources Fund, up 40.6%
so far this year despite a slump this month, according to Morningstar. That fund, whose Class A shares carry
a 5.75% front-end load, gained another 60% last year thanks to its heavy weighting in energy stocks. The
Oppenheimer Real Asset Fund and the Pimco CommodityRealReturn Strategy Fund, two funds that invest
directly in commodities -- as opposed to owning shares of companies in commodity-heavy industries -- are up
16.46% and 11.89% so far this year.

--- Page 176 ---
Page 176 of 265 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
While many pros think bond yields are all but certain to rise, dissenters such as those at Vanguard preach a
stay-with-bonds sermon. Vanguard strategists suggest that individual investors aren't in a good position to
predict which direction currencies or interest rates will go, and shouldn't retool right now.
Fran Kinniry, a principal in Vanguard's investment counseling and research business, says that investors
need to realize that if there were real consensus on the path of the dollar and on interest rates, currency
exchange rates and bond prices would already reflect that knowledge. Mr. Kinniry says the volatility of recent
weeks and months is mostly due to the fact that rate moves are magnified when interest rates are so low.
And Mr. Kinniry says that investors who heeded the advice of some experts and kept their money only in
short-term bond funds have missed out on the added yield offered by longer-term bonds.
Long-term bond funds, on average, have gained 5.84% this year, while funds that invest in short-term bonds
have gained 1.54%, according to Morningstar. That means "a lot of money being left on the sidelines," Mr.
Kinniry says.
He suggests that investors maintain a mix of bond maturities -- some long, some short, some medium -- and
that investors keep the balance constant. If long-term bonds do well, for example, investors should sell some
of those holdings. That approach should keep the portfolio from becoming overweight in any maturity.
---

Commodities Hold Key for Stocks --- Bulls Believe Prices of Oil, Metals Have Peaked, Taming Inflation; Bears Prepare for Wolf at the Door
Abreast of the Market
Commodities Hold Key for Stocks --- Bulls Believe Prices of Oil, Metals Have Peaked, Taming
Inflation; Bears Prepare for Wolf at the Door
By E.S. Browning
1,101 words
13 December 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
IT'S UNUSUAL in these high-tech times for commodities like oil and copper to roil the stock market, but it
happened this year. Those who want to see a strong stock market next year are hoping that has just
changed.
As the prices of oil, gold, aluminum and other commodities soared for most of this year, the stock market
languished. It was only when commodities began to run out of steam at the end of the year that stocks finally
turned upward. Oil, the commodity most closely linked to stocks, peaked in late October, and many other
commodities followed it, peaking and then falling last month.
The market forecasters who expect a good year for stocks in 2005 tend to be the ones who think commodity
prices are under control. The ones who are biting their nails are those who fear prices will surge again.
"I think that the fundamentals are going to keep driving oil prices down, which is a good thing for the
economy, for consumers' discretionary income and for corporate profitability -- and therefore a good thing for
the stock market," says Joseph Keating, chief investment officer at AmSouth Asset Management in
Birmingham, Ala.
Mr. Keating is forecasting oil at around $40 a barrel next year; it settled Friday at $40.71 a barrel on the New
York Mercantile Exchange, down 26% from its October high of $55.17. Copper is 6% off its high and
aluminum is down 5% from its peak.
But not everyone thinks prices will stay down. They think continued demand could push them back up.
"I am not sure that the crude-oil story is over yet," says Brian Pears, head stock trader at Victory Capital
Management, the money-management arm of KeyCorp in Cleveland. "Oil is more likely to move toward the
mid-$50 range than the mid-$40 range next year. Over the long haul it has to act as a depressant on the
economy and on earnings."
Lately, the optimists have been in the driver's seat. The Dow Jones Industrial Average has rebounded 8%
since Oct. 25, although last week it pulled back 48.99 points, or less than 1%, to 10543.22, including Friday's
drop of 9.60 points. It is up almost 1% this year, while the broader Standard &Poor's 500-stock index, also
down slightly last week, is up 6.8% for 2004.
Why all the focus on commodities? Because the motor behind the stock recovery during the past two years
has been a classic economic rebound, fired by low interest rates and resurgent Asian economies that have
been eating up iron, steel, copper, aluminum and oil.
The fear isn't that commodities will become as dominant a force as they were in the 1970s. For oil costs to
get back to the same share of the economy they represented back then, some analysts have calculated, oil
would have to push above $100 a barrel.
"U.S. businesses have strong balance sheets and are well-positioned to handle any price shocks," says
James Swanson, chief investment strategist at Boston money-management group MFS Investment
Management. "It is more likely that oil will slow, but not stop, economic expansion."
The question is, by how much? A year ago, U.S. corporate-profit growth was running at an annual rate of
more than 20%. Next year, it is expected to fall to about half that, according to analysts' estimates tracked by
Thomson First Call. That still is above the historical average of around 7%, but it no longer is eye-popping,
and some investors fear that the analysts are too optimistic. Stock gains have been based partly on views
such as Mr. Swanson's and Mr. Keating's, that profit growth will remain strong. If profits get hit more than they
expect, stocks will suffer.

--- Page 180 ---
Page 180 of 265 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
And earnings are only half the story. Historically, stocks have been driven by two things: earnings and
interest rates. This year, the Federal Reserve has doubled its target interest rate for overnight bank lending,
and it is expected to push the target up a further quarter percentage point tomorrow to 2.25%. That still is only
about equal to inflation, meaning it is exceptionally low.
If world growth remains strong and commodity prices rise again, and if the dollar continues to fall, that would
almost certainly push up inflation and force the Fed's hand. It would have to step up the pace of its rate
increases, and that would deal stocks a blow.
"Inflation and interest rates will bring down" the stock market eventually, says James Paulsen, chief
investment strategist at Wells Capital Management. He thinks that will happen later rather than sooner
because he believes commodity prices already have seen most of their gains.
If so, inflation should rise only gradually, as the weak dollar increases demand for U.S. goods and causes
American companies to boost payrolls and investment. That would help the stocks of manufacturers and
technology companies put in nice gains next year, which is what Mr. Paulsen is forecasting. He expects that
the inflation problem is for later, not for now. The real problem would come if he is wrong and inflation rises
more rapidly.
The hope for next year is a return to the "Goldilocks" economy that flourished in the late 1990s, when
inflation was tame, commodity prices were under control and economic growth was not too hot and not too
cold. That kind of moderation is good for stocks. If commodity prices suddenly start to surge again, however,
and not to mix fairy tales, but the Big Bad Wolf could once more rear his ugly head.
---

Buck Up!
WEEKEND JOURNAL
Buck Up!
By Paula Szuchman
2,789 words
10 December 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
W1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
[The weak dollar is pushing up prices for luxury imports. But from Swiss watches to Japanese SUVs,
bargains still exist -- and sometimes beat U.S. rivals. Paula Szuchman hunts down the deals.]
GERALD WALD, a Miami attorney, prides himself on being a savvy wine shopper, able to find good
European bottles for under $20. When the dollar began sliding and imports rose in price, he turned to
domestic labels.
But recently, Mr. Wald's version of oenophile arbitrage hasn't been working as well. Some California
winemakers have raised prices by as much as 8% in the past year, partly spurred by demand from euro-rich
buyers. So for the holidays, Mr. Wald is planning to serve his latest find -- a $15 Cabernet Sauvignon from the
Colchagua Valley in Chile. "I'm going to start searching out South African wines now," he says.
With the U.S. dollar near historic lows against the euro and close to decade lows against currencies from the
Swiss franc to the English pound, Americans shopping for imported luxury goods are feeling the pinch. Prices
are up for everything from Scottish cashmere sweaters to Italian truffle oil, and more companies are figuring a
weaker dollar into their next round of pricing: Hermes says the stronger euro will likely cause it to bump the
U.S. price of its silk twill scarves by as much as 7%, to $315, in March, while a bottle of 18-year-old
Glenmorangie single malt scotch is set to go up $20 next spring, to $110.
So what does all this mean for U.S. consumers now, and during the post-holiday bargain period to come?
Weekend Journal looked at luxury imports from Irish crystal to Asian rugs, and found that sniffing out the
deals is difficult -- but not impossible. Some vendors have held the line, either by buying from less-expensive
sources, planning in advance to hedge against a falling dollar, or simply taking it on the chin and absorbing
the price difference.
Shopping for a Tag Heuer watch? Though the Swiss franc is up 9% against the dollar over the last three
months, watch prices have remained flat because their suppliers set prices earlier in the year. Designer
fashion may be giving U.S. shoppers sticker shock, but you can still get one of Diane von Furstenberg's iconic
wrap dresses for the same $298 as last year. (She gets the silk from China, where the currency is pegged to
the dollar.) And New York specialty food store Tuller is recommending customers start trying cheeses from
Spain -- aged Manchegos, semi-soft Mahons from Minorca -- as producers there keep prices down to gain an
edge over more well-known French cheeses.
On the other hand, buying American doesn't automatically mean getting a better deal. In spite of a
strengthening yen, some dealers are discounting slow-selling Japanese SUVs, such as the Acura MDX, even
more heavily than the domestic Cadillac Escalade. And while the euro is hogging the headlines, Guatemala's
quetzal has been gaining on the dollar, too -- helping push wholesale prices for that country's organic coffee
up as much as 12% in 9 months.
A weak dollar affects the price of luxury in other ways: Jewelry shoppers are discovering a sagging
greenback has been accompanied by higher prices for gold and silver, which investors see as alternative
investments. And of course, a product's final price depends on more than just exchange rates. Costs for labor
and natural resources are factors, and prices for certain brands have more to do with how much consumers
will pay than with how much they cost to make. What's more, a weaker dollar can provide an excuse for
companies to raise prices -- sometimes even more than the currency swing itself would dictate.
The dollar began weakening as far back as early 2002, a result of rising U.S. debt, a growing trade deficit and
low U.S. interest rates. But it has fallen much more dramatically since August, as global investors apparently
grew weary of accumulating ever greater sums of dollars in their portfolios. Though the dollar staged a
mini-rally against the yen and euro in recent days, the dollar has hit a five-year low against the yen and an
all-time low against the euro in the last three months. (The euro closed at $1.33 yesterday, up nearly 6%

--- Page 185 ---
Page 185 of 265 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
year-to-date.) Even the Canadian dollar -- a currency Americans have long counted on to provide bargains up
north -- just hit a 12-year high against the dollar.
In all, the weak dollar and high oil prices pushed import prices up by nearly 10% in November from the
previous year, among the highest annual growth rates since the late 1980s, according to the most recent
figures from the Bureau of Labor Statistics. Analysts say that if the U.S. keeps borrowing from abroad and
widening its trade deficit, the dollar will likely not rise significantly for some time. "Our expectation is that the
dollar weakness is here to stay for the next couple of years," says Mark Austin, global head of foreign
exchange research at HSBC.
Some retailers are finding creative ways to soften the price squeeze. In Beverly Hills, Calif., men's clothier
Carroll &Co. says it has been negotiating better rates with its Scottish cashmere manufacturer by ordering the
knitting done in April, when the factory isn't as busy. (Most cashmere knitting takes place in the summer, for
fall delivery.) Owner John Carroll says he's willing to accept some currency-related increases, but he has
limits. "It's one thing to sell a cable-knit sweater for $475 that went for $400 last year," he says. "But if I have
to push it up to $550 next year, I just won't do it."
For our luxury-goods price check, we concentrated on industries where imports -- particularly those from
Europe -- account for a large part of the market. We talked to distributors about the price of goods imported in
smaller quantities (Taiwanese tea, French linens) and to larger retailers for tips on how to get that Italian
leather handbag without paying European import prices. We found out the best time to buy -- silk now,
televisions next year -- and even discovered that sometimes, goods from abroad are actually cheaper than
their U.S. counterparts. (Hint: Say cheese.)
Housewares
BUY NOW: Hand-knotted Indian rug
BUYER BEWARE: Crystal candlesticks
Holiday shoppers, rethink the gift of crystal. After a few years of squeezing margins to give U.S. consumers a
break, Irish crystalmaker Waterford recently said it would raise some prices in the next few months. That's
risky, because sales for parent company Waterford Wedgwood fell 10% in October from the previous year in
large part because of the decline in value of U.S. sales. "Our costs are on the rise," says a spokesman. "But
you can only take the price up so far before you begin to impact sales."
High-end lamps are taking a hit -- in a way that sheds light on how the various parts of a manufactured
product show up in the final price. Jeff Goodman, owner of lamp-maker JGoodDesign, says he's paying more
for plugs, cords and sockets from Italy, and last year, German suppliers of colored glass added a "currency
fluctuation surcharge." In all, he says, his own costs have gone up 17%. The result: An Oculus Pendant
ceiling lamp that went for $815 last year now runs $895.
U.S. consumers can still catch a break -- at least for a bit longer -- on Oriental rugs. Prices for imported
Indian, Pakistani and Iranian hand-woven rugs have been dropping for more than a decade, thanks to
overproduction and competitive pressure from higher-quality, machine-made rugs. Contracts are also
negotiated in dollars, a shield for American buyers. But now, rug importers say a recent shortage of weavers
-- especially in India, where information-technology jobs are creating new opportunities -- could lead to price
hikes. "I wouldn't be surprised if I get hit with a 10% increase next year," says New York importer and
wholesaler Mark Harounian.
Food &Wine
BUY NOW: Maine lobster
BUYER BEWARE: Nova Scotia lobster
From Canadian maple syrup to Madagascar cinnamon and Spanish almonds, gourmet ingredients are more
expensive this season. That's forcing retailers and restaurants to raise prices, or consider going without.
Online gourmet food vendor Urbani Truffles has a 55-ml jar of white truffle oil from Italy for $9 -- up from
$6.42 a couple of weeks ago.
Want a nice bottle of red with that? Wine retailers say they'll be forced to raise prices on particularly
in-demand bottles as early as the first quarter of next year, when many contracts with distributors are set to
expire. "It's hard to prepare yourself for something like this," says Michael Aaron, chairman of
Sherry-Lehmann, which has a retail store in New York and distributes throughout the U.S. But as prices of
European bottles rise, some U.S. makers feel emboldened to keep up. As a result, U.S. consumers such as
Miami's Mr. Wald are seeking wines from less-established areas such as Chile -- relative bargains, even
though that country's peso has also been gaining on the greenback.

--- Page 186 ---
Page 186 of 265 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The good news for U.S. shoppers: Some food vendors are eating rising costs themselves. Roaster and
retailer Gimme Coffee is holding its one-pound bag of organic Guatemalan beans at $10.95, though its
wholesale suppliers have raised prices for the first time in years. Prices for some Japanese green teas are up
thanks to a stronger yen and higher labor costs, but U.S. importer In Pursuit of Tea says it will keep retail
prices flat at least through the new year. And in New York, restaurant Jean-Georges isn't raising prices for
dishes containing Holland peppers or Nova Scotia hard-shelled lobsters, even though they cost up to 20%
more than last year. "Our customers are very price-sensitive," says purchasing director Michael Snyder.
Apparel &Accessories
BUY NOW: Chinese silk
BUYER BEWARE: Italian leather
(MORE)
As retailers grapple with whether to raise prices on European goods, some companies are beginning to
import high-end items from some surprising places. With Italian leather prices on the rise, luxury-goods
catalog Vivre has turned to a source of more affordable leather -- Spain -- for a line of private-label handbags,
wallets and picture frames. Starting price: $190, compared to $250 for similar Italian products. Scoop, a
retailer with stores in Miami and New York, is selling Indian-made sandals for the second year, and has kept
the price flat at $78. "We have to span the globe to find places that are still cost-effective," says co-owner
Stefani Greenfield, who is heading for the first time next month to seek out new designers in Argentina, where
the peso has barely budged against the dollar in the past three months.
Meanwhile, French apparel giant Hermes says the strong euro isn't the only reason U.S. consumers will soon
see higher prices on its silk twill scarves. The scarves are made in France, but the silk comes from Brazil --
where the real is also gaining on the dollar. "It's all currency," says Robert Chavez, Hermes of Paris CEO.
"It's a little eye-popping."
Cars
BUY NOW: Land Rover Discovery
BUYER BEWARE: Cadillac Escalade
European and Japanese car manufacturers have largely shielded U.S. consumers from
exchange-rate-related increases, in part to match up against domestic makers in the highly competitive U.S.
auto market. Some European companies have plants in the U.S. -- Mercedes-Benz has one in Alabama --
and protect themselves against a weak dollar by, among other things, locking in prices for future purchasing
contracts. This year, excess supply has also resulted in incentives from dealers and makers who need to
move inventory which is piling up as next year's cars arrive.
Analysts say makers who have been lowering margins on luxury cars for the past five years to keep prices
competitive might not be able to continue for much longer, and could attempt increases as early as next year.
"There's no rational reason to buy a luxury car," says Jeremy Anwyl, president of Edmunds.com, an online
industry information and research company. "It's very much fashion-driven."
To find a deal on a luxury car, Mr. Anwyl suggests targeting models that are older or are slower sellers -- like
the Mercedes-Benz S-Class, which sold for about $1,000 less in October than it did in October 2002. With
fewer new models and fears of rising gas prices, SUVs may also be relative bargains this year: Discounts on
the Acura MDX and Land Rover Discovery, which is being discontinued, are especially high.
Jewelry
BUY NOW: Pearl earrings from Indonesia
BUYER BEWARE: Diamond earrings from France
If you're in the market for gold jewelry, expect to pay a lot more now: Dealers say they're being forced to
pass on the higher cost of gold, which last month hit a 16-year high of $454 an ounce. Jewelers say price
increases are inevitable after the holidays. U.S. jeweler David Yurman, for example, raised its retail prices 5%
in January 2004 -- and plans to raise them again in the new year in part to offset more expensive materials.
Some European retailers say they have been able to absorb a bit of the impact themselves. Rome-based
luxury jeweler Bulgari says it has combated the weak dollar in part by buying raw materials in dollars rather
than euros. Still, though the company hasn't raised its prices in the U.S., it says it will not be able to absorb
the effects of a weak dollar indefinitely.

--- Page 187 ---
Page 187 of 265 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Other companies are starting to push less-expensive materials. Tiffany recently launched its first Iridesse
pearl boutique, in McLean, Va., with cultured pearls from China, Indonesia and the U.S. Strands start at $100,
compared with $225 for a silver ring with a diamond accent at the company's flagship New York store. Van
Cleef &Arpels' new Hawaii Collection uses stones like amethyst, citrine and rubelite. "It's part of a move
toward more affordable, daytime jewelry," says CEO Nathalie Guedj.
Another option for U.S. shoppers: Swiss-made watches. Jake Spigelman, owner of Jay Roberts Jewelers in
Marlton, N.J., says his imported watches, including those from Cartier and Tag Heuer, are selling briskly.
Suppliers raised prices earlier this year, and many retailers expect those to hold until at least early next year.
"I bought a little extra inventory this year just in case," says Mr. Spigelman. "Who knows what'll happen next
year?"
Electronics
BUY NOW: Loudspeakers from Kansas
BUYER BEWARE: Loudspeakers from England
No need to rush to stock up on Chinese electronics. With the Chinese currency pegged at 8.2 yuan to the
dollar, electronics made in the country are a relative bargain. Even if the Chinese government allows the yuan
to move higher, such a move isn't likely to impact prices significantly: The country's labor costs are still
relatively low, and Chinese companies that purchase components from places like Taiwan and South Korea
would benefit from a stronger currency. "People's expectations are too great about what a revaluation [of the
yuan] would mean for U.S. consumers," says Nicholas Lardy, a senior fellow at the Institute for International
Economics in Washington, D.C.
And the tried-and-true rule of buying electronics still holds: Older technologies are cheaper than new ones. It
might be wise to put off buying an LCD flat-panel television, in particular, as prices are expected to drop up to
27% by the end of 2005 as newer and more efficient factories boost supplies. (A 42-inch set is expected to
cost $3,100 next year, down from $4,500 today, according to DisplaySearch.)
European electronics are a different story. High-end speakers are expected to be particularly vulnerable to
price hikes: Many, like those made by Vienna Acoustics in Austria, or Sonus Faber in Italy, have handmade
components and don't benefit from the higher efficiencies of new factories. Retailers suggest buying these
products before the spring, when some new lines come out. Danish maker Bang &Olufsen says it's keeping
increases in line with previous years -- it raised its prices 3.5% in June -- by lowering manufacturing costs.
This summer, the company opened its first factory in the Czech Republic, where costs are about one-fifth of
those in Denmark, says chief operating officer Peter Thosrup.

Dollar's Dominance Erodes --- Greenback Weakens, but Has Room to Fall; Primacy Not in Danger
Going Global
Dollar's Dominance Erodes --- Greenback Weakens, but Has Room to Fall; Primacy Not in Danger
By Michael R. Sesit, G. Thomas Sims and Andrew Morse
907 words
10 December 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C14
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
THE DOLLAR'S DOMINANCE as the world's premier currency isn't likely to end anytime soon, but it is seeing
some of its status chipped away.
For most of the past century, the dollar has enjoyed an unparalleled position, beloved by central banks and
big companies as the most solid and convenient way to hold money. That has provided enormous benefits to
the U.S. Recently, though, the U.S. currency has at least seen its untouchable reputation come under greater
scrutiny.
On Wednesday, the Russian central bank confirmed that it was considering reducing the dollar share -- and
increasing the euro portion -- of its $117 billion foreign-currency portfolio. On Monday, the Bank for
International Settlements, which monitors global markets and cross-border banking issues, reported that the
Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries' dollar-denominated bank deposits fell to 61.5% of total
deposits in the second quarter of this year from 75% in the third quarter of 2001, while over the same period,
euro-denominated deposits rose to 20% from 12%. Part, but not all, of this reflects the depreciation of the
dollar.
Analysts also point out that China's holdings of U.S. government securities, its main way of holding
dollar-denominated assets, hasn't kept pace this year with the growth in its total foreign-exchange reserves.
That implies it is building up reserves in other currencies. Indeed, since its launch in 1999, the euro's share of
all central-bank reserves has grown to 19.7% from 13.5%.
With the dollar's recent slide -- especially against the euro, to all-time lows -- some analysts say the euro
could continue to make headway as a viable alternative reserve currency. In part, that's because the dollar
may fall more because of the huge U.S. budget deficit and current-account deficit -- the broadest measure of
trade in goods and services -- and apparent disinterest from the Bush administration.
As long as the dollar is weak and U.S. interest rates are low, some may seek higher-returning assets, such
as euro-denominated notes. At the same time, the large dollar holdings in central-bank portfolios, especially
in Asia, make it unlikely that central bankers would take actions that might threaten those holdings.
Still, many economists reprise Mark Twain's observation that reports of his death had been exaggerated
when it comes to the dollar's reserve status. "There is some room for some erosion; it isn't that all is well for
the dollar," said Frederic Mishkin, a finance and economics professor at Columbia University's business
school in New York and former official at the Federal Reserve.
Just this week, the dollar got a vote of confidence from Japan, which has $840 billion in reserves, of which
more than $720 billion is invested in U.S. Treasury bonds -- despite the dollar's 7.5% fall against the yen over
the past month.
Examples of dollar dominance abound. China pegs its yuan to the dollar. Emerging-market nations borrow in
dollars. Most commodities, especially oil, are priced in dollars. And although Saddam Hussein insisted on
being paid in euros for Iraqi oil, when the former dictator was captured last December, he was carrying
$750,000 in $100 bills.
At the end of 2003, the latest figures available, an overwhelming 63.8% of central-bank reserves were held
in dollars, compared with 19.7% in euros, 4.8% in yen and 4.4% in sterling, according to the International
Monetary Fund. And when it comes to trading in the $1.9 trillion-a-day global foreign-exchange market, 89%
of all transactions involve the dollar, compared with 37% for the euro and 20% for the yen, according to the
BIS.
"Since World War II, really since the end of World War I, the U.S. has been the world's leading trading nation,
the leading source of foreign finance, and has possessed the world's deepest and most liquid financial

--- Page 189 ---
Page 189 of 265 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
markets -- all of which makes it attractive for firms, governments and banks," including central banks, "to use
dollars when they transact with us and when they transact with one another," said Barry Eichengreen, an
international economist at the University of California at Berkeley.
Many of the world's multinational companies are based in the U.S.; the nation's banks are the bedrock of the
global financial system; and its financial markets attract investments -- in dollars, of course -- from around the
world.
---
Patrick Barta contributed to this article
---
Send comments and questions to michael.sesit@wsj.com

Some Question U.S. Bond Rating
Credit Markets
Some Question U.S. Bond Rating
By Aaron Lucchetti
1,358 words
7 December 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
IN A DEVELOPMENT that underscores the growing concern over America's twin deficits, some investors and
analysts are starting to question the unquestionable: the U.S. government's triple-A bond rating.
They are anxious about years of budget and trade deficits. Their fears have been compounded by a
weakening dollar and looming questions about how the U.S. will pay for Medicare and Social Security as
scores of baby boomers start retiring.
To suggest that the U.S. is a triple-A credit "would be to suggest that it can pay its bills over a long period of
time in a stable currency," says William Gross, chief investment officer at Pacific Investment Management
Co., or Pimco, which runs the nation's largest bond mutual fund. "That is no longer true."
Of course, the Treasury bond still is the benchmark by which all other debt is measured. Bond yields remain
relatively low, a sign of investor confidence. And the three major credit-rating companies -- Moody's Investors
Service, Standard &Poor's and Fitch Ratings -- all still rate the U.S.'s debt as a triple-A, with no downgrades
in sight.
"We expect that somehow the politicians will come up with reform" to address long-term financial challenges,
says Steven Hess, U.S. analyst at Moody's. If they don't, the triple-A rating would be "in jeopardy at some
date." At S&P, John Chambers, a managing director, says: "The triple-A credit standing of the U.S. is secure
against all reasonable scenarios."
The three ratings companies point out it would be very difficult for a nation such as the U.S. to default when it
issues virtually all of its debt in dollars, even the bonds bought by foreigners. But the triple-A rating isn't
guaranteed. In the late 1990s and early 2000s, ratings firms downgraded Japan's sovereign debt, even
though most of it was denominated in yen. And ratings analysts acknowledge that the U.S. financial picture
could deteriorate someday to the point that they would consider a downgrade, possibly if defense costs soar
or changes weren't implemented to curb the costs of government programs such as Social Security.
Such deterioration might force foreign bondholders to demand that the U.S. issue more of its debt in foreign
currency, which would be one of the longer term risks to the rating, says Moody's Mr. Hess. "The U.S. is a
long way from not being triple-A," adds Lionel Price, Fitch's chief economist. "But it's not set in stone."
While the big credit-rating companies remain confident in the U.S. debt rating, one small upstart has taken a
different tack. On Friday, Egan-Jones Ratings Co. sent a research note to clients arguing that U.S. bonds
should carry a double-A rating, two notches below the highest possible rating of triple-A and below countries
such as Canada, the United Kingdom and France. While still a strong rating, double-A is a world apart in
perception, especially for a bond that has been -- and remains -- the gold standard of safe investing.
A country's debt rating influences how much interest it has to pay on the money it borrows. The high level of
confidence investors have in the U.S. helps keep the country's borrowing costs low compared with other
countries, and keeps U.S. consumers' debt-servicing rates relatively low when they borrow to buy homes,
cars and other goods and services.
But how much debt can the U.S. take on? This year, S&P estimates that the U.S.'s net general government
debt will increase to about 60% of gross domestic product, far more than the 20% that is typical for a triple-A
rated country. The U.S.'s current-account deficit, a broad measure of debt, meanwhile, will grow to a record
5.1% of GDP, up sharply from 1.6% in 1997, according to Moody's.
Many say the numbers alone aren't cause for concern. The American economy is huge and fast growing, and
taxes are relatively low: all making it easier for the U.S. to pay back its debts. The U.S. also has another
advantage: It borrows in its own currency, meaning that a decline in the dollar doesn't hurt its ability to pay
back debt.

--- Page 193 ---
Page 193 of 265 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The dollar has been weakening for some time and recently dropped to multiyear lows against other
currencies, much to the chagrin of foreign investors who own about 40% of Treasury securities. While the
10-year Treasury note has returned 4.3% this year through Friday, Japanese holders have lost 0.8% after
converting the return into yen, and European investors have lost 2.5% in euro terms.
The weaker dollar is a double-edged sword. While it may spur exports, it could bring inflation and cause
foreign investors to slow purchases of Treasurys in search of better-performing investments. Both would likely
force U.S. interest rates higher, hurting the value of existing U.S. bonds and making it more expensive for
the U.S. to pay future debts. Higher rates could also eventually slow the economy, making it harder for the
U.S. to generate tax revenues.
The U.S. has been through worse since it was first rated triple-A by Moody's in 1917. During the Depression,
it paid back some bondholders in depressed dollars instead of gold. During World War II, it took on debt
greater than its annual GDP. Its debts also rose, without a ratings downgrade, during the oil spikes of the
1970s and the heavy defense spending of the 1980s.
The debt of the U.S. government when compared with GDP is also comfortably below where Japan's was
when that country's bonds lost their triple-A rating after years of financial problems.
"While the probability of default is low, the U.S. is allowing its currency to deteriorate and we're reflecting that
in our rating," says Sean Egan, managing director of Egan-Jones. The Haverford, Pa., firm has rated
corporate bonds since 1995 and is seeking recognition by the Securities and Exchange Commission as an
official ratings firm.
Moody's officials, meanwhile, lately have faced questions from foreign investors and officials about the U.S.
rating. At a conference in London this fall, for example, two investors raised the issue.
At Payden &Rygel, an investment-management firm specializing in bonds, deficits are a hot topic. "It's a
question of confidence," says James Sarni, a portfolio manager at the firm who is thinking about the U.S.
rating for the first time in years.
Pimco's Mr. Gross declined to say what the U.S. rating should be, but cast doubt about whether it should
remain at the top. "Take away our military might, and a lower rating would be a near-unanimous opinion," he
said.
Treasurys
Treasurys gained modestly on continued buying related to Friday's weaker-than-expected employment report.
At 4 p.m. Monday, the benchmark 10-year note was up 8/32 point, or $2.50 for each $1,000 invested, to yield
4.236%.

This Time, Markets Generally Shrug Off Oil-Price Decline
Thursday's Markets
This Time, Markets Generally Shrug Off Oil-Price Decline
By E.S. Browning
483 words
3 December 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
OIL'S PRICE SPILL continued, but the stock market yawned.
Crude-oil futures fell 4.9% to $43.25 a barrel, a 12% two-day decline that was the sharpest in 20 months.
But the Dow Jones Industrial Average, which celebrated Wednesday's 7.4% oil-price decline by jumping 162
points to a nine-month high, took a breather. The blue chips pulled back a slim 5.10 points, or 0.05%, to
10585.12. They still are up 1.3% this year.
Investors got good news after regular trading ended, as Intel increased its quarterly sales forecast. The stock
rose 7.9% in after-hours trading.
Trading volume hit another one-month high on the New York Stock Exchange and a 10-month high on
Nasdaq, as some investors took profits from the previous day and others pumped new money into stocks.
Microsoft's $32.62 billion special dividend was paid, and some money managers whose funds received it put
it back into stocks.
"It was a consolidation day," with some investors worried how strong today's report on November job creation
will be, said Stephen Sachs, director of trading at mutual-fund group Rydex Investments in Rockville, Md.
"But there is a lot of money that was on the sidelines that is being put to work now."
On the New York Mercantile Exchange, oil had its lowest finish in nearly three months, reinforcing hopes for
the economy and corporate profits.
The dollar, which fell to multiyear lows against various currencies Wednesday, rebounded slightly.
But November chain-store sales reports were lackluster.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index was almost unchanged, falling 0.09%, or 1.04 points, to 1190.33, still
up 7% this year.
The Nasdaq Composite Index rose 0.25%, or 5.34 points, to 2143.57, another 10-month high and a gain of
7% this year. Volatile technology stocks, such as Internet-related and biotechnology companies, rose.
Bond prices continued to slump on fears that a stronger economy will force the Federal Reserve to push
interest rates higher; higher rates for new bonds make existing bonds less attractive.
Gold, which had hit a 16 1/2-year high of $454.00 an ounce Wednesday pulled back to $450.40.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks were mixed. On the Big Board, where 1.72 billion shares traded, 1,237 stocks rose and 2,052 fell.
Bonds declined. The 10-year Treasury note fell 8/32, or $2.50 for each $1,000 invested. The yield rose to
4.402%. The 30-year bond was down 14/32 to yield 5.055%.
The dollar rose. Late in New York, it traded at 103.21 yen, up from 102.67, while the euro fell against the
dollar to $1.3270 from $1.3323.

Direct-Commodity Funds Hold Their Appeal
Fund Track
Direct-Commodity Funds Hold Their Appeal
By Peter A. McKay and Ian McDonald
1,132 words
3 December 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
WITH THE DOLLAR sinking, investing in mutual funds focused on commodities might seem like a good bet.
When the dollar falls, commodities, priced in dollars, tend to rise, since a weak buck means it takes more
dollars to buy the same bushel of soy beans or barrel of oil.
But few traditional mutual funds put their money in actual commodities. Instead they buy shares in the
companies that produce them, bringing with them a host of risks involving management and other issues
unrelated to the price of gold, oil, paper or other raw materials.
For investors willing to do some digging of their own -- and to endure some risks unlike those of more
traditional mutual funds -- it is possible to bet on commodities, especially two of the best-performing
resources the last few years, precious metals and energy.
"Clearly, natural-resources holdings have been a way -- and continue to be a way -- to play a falling dollar,"
says Michael Cuggino, manager of the Permanent Portfolio Fund, which holds more than $50 million, or 30%
of its assets, in gold and silver bullion and coins. "And the indications we've seen seem to point to a
continued fall in the dollar. At some point, it will stabilize, but I can't tell you what that point is."
While there are more than 40 natural-resources mutual funds and more than 20 gold funds on the market,
according to data from fund researcher Lipper Inc., the vast majority invest in shares of companies that
collect, refine or market commodities like oil, natural gas, metals or paper. Only a handful invest directly in
commodities, primarily through futures contracts, which are also viewed as a shelter from rising inflation for
U.S. investors.
A closer look at Lipper's data demonstrates both the benefits of commodities' traditional inverse relationship
to the dollar, and the quirks of using company shares as a proxy for that relationship.
According to Lipper, natural-resources funds are up 33% this year, many of them because of surging oil
prices. But gold funds, which were up more than 50% in each of the last two years, have fizzled, up only
0.6% so far this year, barely on pace with increases of about 1% in major stock indexes like the Dow Jones
Industrial Average, Nasdaq Composite and Standard &Poor's 500. Gold companies have been hurt by wild
swings in that commodity's price, which is up dramatically of late, but only 1.5% for the year because of sharp
declines earlier in the year.
"I always say, gold stocks are stocks first," says Mr. Cuggino, whose fund is up more than 14% this year,
according to fund tracker Morningstar Inc. "Some of the things that go on at the companies, especially
hedging programs, can really isolate the stock price from the commodity price." By hedging programs, Mr.
Cuggino is referring to the trades that companies make in an effort to neutralize the risk of fluctuating
commodity prices.
Of those funds that do invest directly in natural resources, many also invest a portion of their money in
inflation-protected bonds and other fixed-income securities to cut down the risk of high-volatility futures
contracts, which are agreements to buy or sell an underlying resource on a fixed expiration date at a certain
price. They're often traded using high amounts of leverage -- essentially, borrowed money -- and can be
financially perilous.
Only a few of these funds have multiyear track records. The oldest is the $1.1 billion Oppenheimer Real
Asset Fund, which launched in 1997 and primarily uses futures to invest directly in commodities with most of
its investments focusing on oil. In 2002 Pacific Investment Management Co., better known as Pimco,
launched the Pimco Commodity RealReturn Fund, which offers investors broader exposure to investments in
raw materials.

--- Page 199 ---
Page 199 of 265 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Given commodities' run, it isn't surprising that the funds have performed well. Over the past 12 months the
Oppenheimer fund is up 34% and the Pimco fund is up 24%, compared with 13% for the S&P 500 and 11%
for the average U.S. stock fund, according to Morningstar. Investors appear to have noticed: The Pimco
fund's assets have already hit $6.1 billion, nearly eight times the size of the nation's average
natural-resources fund.
Hot returns have stoked investor interest and strong sales have done the same for marketers. This year two
other commodity-focused funds have launched, the Merrill Lynch Real Investment Fund and the streeTracks
Gold Shares, an exchange-traded fund. or ETF, that invests directly in bullion. Another gold-focused ETF is
on the way from Barclays Global Investors, according to filings with the Securities and Exchange
Commission. (ETFs hold baskets of securities but, unlike traditional mutual funds, trade all day on an
exchange like a stock.)
Earlier this year Pimco filed preliminary paperwork for a closed-end counterpart to its open-end commodities
fund. In September the closed-end Scudder Global Commodities Stock Fund launched. It has the leeway to
invest in both commodity-producing companies and directly in commodities, but at the end of October it had
nearly 80% of its assets in stocks.
"There's a lot of interest in commodities funds because of the hot streak they've been on and the Pimco
funds' ability to collect sizable assets," says Dan McNeela, a fund analyst covering natural-resources funds at
Morningstar.
Not everyone is enamored of the new offerings, though. For instance, money manager Peter Schiff, of Euro
Pacific Capital Inc., says the new exchange-traded gold fund doesn't provide the same benefits as owning
the metal. "If you really want exposure to gold, it's better to do it in some direct way, by actually owning
bullion," says Mr. Schiff, a longtime bear on the broader stock market who has helped his clients buy real
metal through Australia's Perth Mint.
Other strategies for benefiting from a falling dollar include buying foreign stocks or bonds, through a mutual
fund, because U.S. investors' foreign holdings rise in dollar terms if the fund doesn't hedge its currency risk.
A more exotic route is to invest in foreign currencies, through separately managed accounts or a mutual fund
like the $208.5 million Franklin Templeton Hard Currency Fund, which invests primarily in money markets and
currency contracts denominated in foreign currencies. Launched in 1989, the fund averages just a 2.1%
annual gain in the decade ended Nov. 30, but averages a more than 14% annual gain over the past three
years, according to Morningstar.

Blue Chips Close Best Month of '04 With a Down Note
Tuesday's Markets
Blue Chips Close Best Month of '04 With a Down Note
By E.S. Browning
719 words
1 December 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
INVESTORS GAVE BACK another chunk of last week's holiday gains, unsettled by the sight of the dollar

though big companies reported gains.

in November. Gold pulled back from Monday's 16-year high of $453.70 an ounce, but still was up 5% last
month.

2096.81, but still rose 6% in November, its strongest month in more than a year. That gain left it up 4.7% for

year.

Wealth Manager: Wealthy Investors Add Coins to Portfolios; A $400,000 Penny
Wealth Manager: Wealthy Investors Add Coins to Portfolios; A $400,000 Penny
By Jeff D. Opdyke
1,505 words
1 December 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
D1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
RARE COINS ARE STARTING to attract investors more at home with stock brokers than coin dealers.
The interest in coins comes as sophisticated investors are increasingly looking for assets outside of the U.S.
stock market, which many market observers expect to post only modest gains during the coming year. In
buying rare coins, individuals not only acquire a collectible asset, but they are also getting exposure to
precious metals. The prices of gold and silver, from which many popular U.S. coins are made, are both rising
smartly.
The Internet and coin-grading services are playing a part in drawing a new breed of coin investors. The
Internet allows collectors to buy and sell rare coins at locations other than their local coin shops. The grading
services authenticate coins and grade them based upon how closely they resemble a freshly minted coin,
making it easier to certify that the coins being traded are investment grade. And electronic registry services
that have sprung up on the Internet during the past few years let investors register their collections online and
compete against others in building the highest graded, most valuable sets.
All this has helped make investment-grade coins nearly as liquid as the stock market. The coin market even
has its own index -- the CU3000 Rare Coin Index. During the past year, that index of the 3000 most-actively
traded coins, has gained about 7.5%, while the Standard &Poor's 500-stock index is up about 11.5%. In the
past three years, the coin index has gained more than 18%, far outpacing the S&P, though coins are off by
about two-thirds from their high in 1989.
The coin market presents risks. Novice collectors often get burned, despite the Internet and grading services.
Moreover, many smaller shops are popular but can undercut sellers and overcharge buyers, some dealers
and collectors say. And the precious-metals market is notoriously volatile.
Also, coin prices have moved substantially, meaning investors aren't buying at bargain levels any more. Still,
coin experts say, coins are about two years into a bull-market that, if it mimics history, will last four or five
years.
"There is a lot of smart money going into coins right now because the wealthy individuals see very attractive
valuations," says Mark Salzberg, chairman of Sarasota, Fla.,-based Numismatic Guaranty Corp. of America,
or NGC, a firm that grades and certifies the authenticity of coins. These are individuals "who have the money
to come in and buy the ultra-rarities."
In addition to the lackluster outlook for stocks, the weak U.S. currency is also driving people to hard assets
that aren't tied to the dollar. "The dollar is decaying right before our eyes and people want to own those
assets not directly tied to the dollar, hard assets," says Geoffrey Hodes, a vice president at Monex, a Newport
Beach, Calif., precious-metals trading firm.
And while gold and silver bullion are less expensive per ounce than coins, "bullion is not beautiful or sexy to
look at and there's no story behind," he adds. "Rare coins have all that. It's like collecting art."
An ultrarare 1933 Saint-Gaudens-type gold coin -- one of only a few that still exist since most were melted
down after President Franklin D. Roosevelt outlawed the hoarding of gold -- sold for nearly $7.6 million,
including fees, at auction two years ago. And in August, at the American Numismatic Association's annual
World's Fair of Money, a rare, 1792 copper cent -- found in an old tobacco tin -- was valued at $400,000 and
is expected to fetch more at auction.
Wealthy investors are gravitating toward the most popular and liquid investment coins: the $20 Double Eagles
and Saint-Gaudens, each comprising nearly an ounce of gold; Morgan and Peace dollars pressed from
slightly more than three-quarters of an ounce of silver; and Liberty Walking half-dollars that hold about a third
of an ounce of silver.

--- Page 210 ---
Page 210 of 265 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Those coins, depending on preservation, rarity and other factors, range from a few hundred dollars to
hundreds of thousands of dollars. In general, investors are building high-quality, investment-grade portfolios
of various gold and silver coins for between $2,000 and $10,000 per coin -- up from just several hundred to a
few thousand dollars a couple years ago. Unlike gold and silver, which investors often own only in paper
form, investors hold coins directly, if only to be able to appreciate them as artwork-caliber engravings.
At auction firm Spectrum Numismatics, a unit of Greg Manning Auctions Inc. in West Caldwell, N.J., wealthy
retail investors are making up a larger portion of the business these days. Through various subsidiaries,
Spectrum holds upward of 130 auctions a year -- from high-end affairs hawking coins that fetch $250,000 or
more, to low-end Internet auctions where coins sell for a few hundred dollars.
"People buying these days often have the intent to sell some day," says Greg Roberts, president and chief
executive officer. To them, he says, "coins have become an alternative hard asset."
The discovery last year of the S.S. Republic also is piquing investor interest. The Civil War-era steamer,
carrying $400,000 in gold and silver coins, sunk in a hurricane off the coast of Georgia on its way to New
Orleans, and numerous high-grade coins being pulled from that wreckage are selling for tens of thousands of
dollars.
Coin dealers are mainly guiding deep-pocketed investors interested in collecting. Established dealers not only
locate worthwhile coins around the country, but also they can help build investment-caliber collections and
design profitable exit strategies for selling off the collection. To find a dealer, the American Numismatic
Association's Web site, www.money.org, has links to what it considers to be reputable ones.
Investors generally demand coins be graded independently to ensure authenticity and to determine a coin's
mint state, or how closely it resembles a freshly minted coin. Coins are graded on a scale of one to 70, with
70 being perfect. Investment-grade coins typically start at MS65 (MS stands for "mint state"), though with
some particularly rare or historically significant coins, lower grades are acceptable. Graded coins are locked
in sealed, tamper-resistant plastic holders, what the industry refers to as "slabs."
Not all slabbed coins are equal, though. Grading services vary in their quality, and "that's where new
investors typically get burned," says Barry Stuppler, a rare-coin dealer and president of the California Coin
and Bullion Merchants Association. Investors looking for bargains often jump at seemingly high-grade coins
graded by low-tier firms. But they end up overpaying for what turns out to be an inferior coin, Mr. Stuppler
says.
The most subtle scratch or flaw can drop a coin into a lower grade and result in a big difference in value. With
an 1891 Morgan silver dollar minted in Carson City, Nev., for instance, a very narrow grade shift of MS67 to
MS66 changes the value of that coin by about $25,000, according to Professional Coin Grading Service, or
PCGS, in Newport Beach, Calif.
PCGS and NGC are widely praised, and coins graded and slabbed by these two grading services routinely
trade just on the basis of their reputations. Independent Coin Grading Co., or ICG, and ANACS are gaining
respect but still lack wide acceptance. The other services, according to coin-dealer surveys, collectors and
independent industry experts, are widely dismissed as too liberal with their standards, and the coins they
grade are generally deeply discounted to account for grade inflation.
Of course, you don't have to be rich to invest in coins. In fact, the lower end of the market is booming, too. For
instance, Jefferson nickels minted in Denver in 1938 -- the first year the U.S. Mint released that series -- have
been hot for the past year, and today the most immaculate examples of those five-cent pieces fetch upward
of $2,000 each, according to PCGS, which also produces a much-watched price guide for collectible coins.
---

investors interested in rare coins.

a sense of how rare a particular coin is in its mint state.

What Supply-Side Really Is...and Isn't
Letters to the Editor
What Supply-Side Really Is...and Isn't
361 words
1 December 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
A11
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Christopher Wood says ("Currency on a Collision Course," editorial page, Nov. 29) that "This is an
administration driven by ideological supply-siders, not fiscal conservatives," and that "the Bush emphasis on
growth at all costs means a continuing benign acceptance of a weaker dollar . . ." Mr. Wood's concern about
the falling dollar may be justified. His comments about supply-siders are ill-founded.
Supply-side economics is not about generating growth at all costs, and it is certainly not about manipulation
of the exchange rate.
The supply-side movement aimed to achieve growth without inflation, in reaction to the stagflation of the
1970s. It recommended cutting marginal tax rates to boost incentives to supply labor and capital to
encourage private output. It called for trimming government spending to free up resources for private use. It
explicitly rejected the notion of using deficits to prime the "demand" pump. It called for curbing money growth
to fight inflation.
A sound currency was a major part of that policy mix. Some supply-siders, like Robert Mundell and Arthur
Laffer, favored fixed exchange rates anchored in gold or other commodities. Others, like Norman Ture and
Beryl Sprinkel, rejected a gold link and fixed exchange rates, but favored keeping money as tight as
necessary to maintain stable prices.
Inflation is a growth-killer. Inflation leads to understatement of costs and overstatement and over-taxation of
profits. Debasing a currency, not only relative to other currencies, but to commodities and goods and services
in general, means inflation. It can be achieved only through excess money creation, that is, bad monetary
policy. The resulting inflation will raise nominal production costs and negate the presumed real exchange
rate advantage.
An economy enjoying faster growth of capacity and output due to supply enhancement can require and
absorb new money at a faster rate than a stagnant economy. But money growth in excess of what the
economy demands will still lead to inflation, a falling dollar and a weaker economy. That, supply-siders do
not support.
Stephen J. Entin
President
Institute for Research on the Economics of Taxation
Washington

MAYBE THE TRADERS ate too much leftover turkey.
Whatever the reason, yesterday's early stock gain ran out of steam. With traders nervous at the sight of
bonds falling heavily and with Wal-Mart Stores offering a disappointing November sales forecast, big stocks
pulled back after last week's holiday rally.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average, which finished last week only about 50 points short of an eight-month
high, pulled back 46.33 points, or 0.44%, to 10475.90. It did manage to rebound from the day's lows, which at
one point had it down 105 points.
Technology stocks rose on hopes of strong end-of-year demand for tech gear, pushing the Nasdaq
Composite Index up 0.23%, or 4.90 points, to 2106.87, a 10-month high.
Many retailers other than Wal-Mart reported strong Thanksgiving sales volume, and that, together with
general optimism about the economy's strength, sent stocks to early gains. But the rally soon fizzled.
Wal-Mart, a component of the Dow industrials and of the Standard &Poor's 500-stock index, fell almost 4%
and gave up more than $9 billion in market value.
More worrisome to some traders was the sight of bond prices falling sharply. They blamed the bond
weakness on worries that a weak dollar could force the Federal Reserve to stave off inflation by pushing up
U.S. interest rates faster than investors had hoped.
Rising interest rates make new bonds, with their higher rates, more lucrative to own than existing ones.
Investors also are talking about the risk of an increase in European rates, which could push the euro up even
more against the dollar.
The bellwether 10-year Treasury note fell 25/32, or $7.81 for every $1,000 invested, pushing its yield up to
4.330% - still low by historical standards. The dollar managed a small gain after falling to multiyear lows last
week against several major currencies.

three-week high. But oil remains well off the 21-year high of $55.17 hit in late New York trading last month,
and some stock traders are turning their attention to the currency and bond markets.
"Oil is kind of moving to the background a little bit and it seems as if the dollar is coming a little to the
forefront," said Todd Leone, head of listed trading at New York brokerage firm SG Cowen.
The Dow industrials, which surged 25% last year, are struggling to finish this year with any gain at all. After
jumping 8% from late October through mid-November, the industrials have gotten stuck a bit, just below the
eight-month high of 10572.55 hit Nov. 18. And even after that surge, they remain virtually unchanged for the
year -- up a spare 0.2%. Traders are debating again whether stocks have gotten ahead of themselves, even
though the industrials remain more than 10% below their record 11722.98, hit almost five years ago.
The broader S&P 500, by contrast, hit a three-year high earlier this month and remains up almost 6% this
year. The S&P 500 yesterday fell 0.34%, or 4.08 points, to 1178.57. But despite this year's gains, the S&P
500 remains more than 20% off the record 1527.46, hit in March 2000. It contains more battered technology
stocks than do the Dow industrials.
The Nasdaq Composite Index, filled with tech stocks, still is more than 50% off its March 2000 record.

Profit Takers Are Expected to Swat Highflying Gold
Commodities Report
Profit Takers Are Expected to Swat Highflying Gold
By Gavin Maguire Dow Jones Newswires
609 words
30 November 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C3
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- Spot gold prices, currently more than $450 an ounce, are at their highest levels since 1988,
and many market participants are looking at $460 and $500 an ounce as the next price targets.
But with gold prices around 14% above the levels seen in late November 2003 and widely indicated to be
overbought according to technical measures, a hefty spate of profit-taking is expected at some point.
Yesterday, December gold rose $4.40 an ounce to $453.70 on the Comex division of the New York
Mercantile Exchange.
Since the beginning of 2002, gold prices have climbed more than 50% from around $285 an ounce. The
primary driver behind gold's strength since then has been the U.S. dollar's cross-currency spiral downward,
especially its retreat to historic lows against the euro. Gold's contra-cyclical tendencies toward the U.S.
currency make it an appealing alternative to the beleaguered dollar.
Geopolitical uncertainty as well as concerns about the potential for a return of inflation also have steadily
drawn buyers to gold.
Still, concerns are growing that gold prices have recently ventured too far, too fast. "By any measure, this
market is heavily overextended, so I think we're in for some serious consolidation, if not a correction, at some
point soon," said Peter Grandich, editor of the Grandich Letter, an investor newsletter. "I'm a roaring gold bull
and have been for some time, but right now I have to say the next $20 move in gold could go either way."
Tom Boustead, precious-metals analyst at futures-brokerage house Refco LLC, agreed: "The underlying
themes that drove gold higher are still in place, but it's clear we're overstretched up here and could be due a
correction."
The timing of any correction is open to debate, but given that hedge funds have been the primary drivers of
gold's gains in recent months, their calendar-centric behavior may be the determining factor.
"Hedge funds have been the heaviest buyers of gold recently, and they work within calendar years, so we
could see some year-end profit taking coming through in the next few days," Mr. Grandich said.
Despite the fears of such a sudden retreat, Mr. Grandich said a price pullback and shake-out of some
"weak-handed longs" could be healthy for the market and allow for the establishment of a base from which
further probes higher could be made in due course.
"Gold's climb over the past couple of years could be described as `two steps forward, one step back,' and I
think we're due one of those steps back soon," Mr. Grandich said. "We hit $400 and then came back, hit $430
and came back, and now I think we'll see the same after having hit $450 or $460. But after that, I think the
target is $500."
In other commodity markets:
NATURAL GAS: Prices fell sharply on the Nymex, giving back a hefty chunk of the previous session's gains
amid the belief that government data last Wednesday showing a surprisingly large draw from storage must
have been an anomaly. the prices of the natural-gas futures contract for January delivery fell 80.2 cents, or
9.2%, to $7.837 a million British thermal units.
COFFEE: Arabica coffee prices set contract highs for all months but December on the New York Board of
Trade. December futures rose 5.75 cents to 94.65 cents a pound, while the active March contract rose 5.9
cents to 97.85 cents.

Currency on a Collision Course
Currency on a Collision Course
By Christopher Wood
1,201 words
29 November 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
A14
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The comments emanating from Washington since the November presidential election, and the related action
in the foreign exchange markets, make it clear that "go for growth" will be the preferred Bush strategy. This is
an administration driven by ideological supply-siders, not fiscal conservatives. Sure, this suggests a still wider
current-account deficit. And America's current-account deficit already accounts for about 77% of the world's
total current-account deficits. But this is a game of chicken which Asia will be expected to continue to finance.
In the meantime, the Bush emphasis on growth at all costs means a continuing benign acceptance of a
weaker dollar so long as that trend is not precipitous. Dollar weakening is clearly underway again and this is
renewing focus on Asia's failure so far to let its own currencies appreciate. The pain of this adjustment is
again becoming apparent in Europe, while the Japanese are threatening intervention again. All of this means
renewed attention on the renminbi's perceived extreme undervaluation. This is best reflected in China's
growing share of world exports. China's share of world exports has risen from 1.8% in 1990 to 6% in the first
five months of this year.
The result of the above is renewed focus among the world's community of speculators on the renminbi
"revaluation." Courtesy of America's "go for growth" strategy, the Asian asset-reflation story is also back in full
swing since if Asian currencies are being artificially held down by central bank intervention, as has been the
case, then the only way natural appreciation pressures can be expressed is via higher asset prices. It is, as a
result, like early 2003 all over again, with the dollar declining and Asian asset-reflation stocks rising.
The main difference is that, this time, there is much more focus on the inevitable need for Asian currencies to
start rising. This growing speculative pressure is clear from the renminbi forward market as well as the
strength of other Asian currencies. The renminbi 12-month non-deliverable forward rate has risen by 4% over
the past four months to Rmb7.87/$, or a 5.1% premium over the spot rate. Meanwhile, the Japanese yen has
risen by 8% against the dollar over the past two months, and the Korean won has risen by 11% in the year to
date to a seven-year high. The Singapore dollar has also risen by 5% over the past four months to its highest
level since December 1998.
In financial markets, it is usually better to travel than to arrive. In part, this also applies to the asset-reflation
theme in Asia. For the story is largely driven by the seeming inevitability of Asian currency appreciation,
which is, in turn, seen to be triggered by the presumed inevitability of a renminbi revaluation. Thus, so long as
the Chinese do not move, the story can continue to run. Traditionally, with the notable exception of Sars, the
PRC leadership prefers to do nothing when there is external pressure on it to do something. On this point, the
market's renewed focus on the renminbi, as reflected in the rising premium on the spot market, would suggest
nothing will happen on the revaluation front.
But on this occasion, this writer is no longer quite so sure as before that the old rules apply. China is now at
the center, not the periphery, of discussions about developments in the world economy. This massively
higher profile on the global stage makes it much harder for the Chinese to ignore external pressures. Their
problem is, however, if they only revalue by a marginal amount -- by which is meant 10% or less -- this is only
likely further to fuel speculative pressures as the animal spirits move in for the kill.
All this raises another question: Is it really in America's interests to pressure China for a renminbi revaluation?
The U.S. trade deficit is clearly one motivating factor. The U.S. merchandise trade deficit rose by 19%
year-on-year in the first nine months of this year to $470 billion, with China accounting for 24% of the total.
But this is, to a certain extent, offset by China's trade deficit with other countries, notably those in Asia.
Excluding America, China posted a trade deficit of $50 billion with the rest of the world and $62 billion with
Asia alone in the first nine months of this year. But U.S. pressure also seems to be driven by other
considerations, notably the ideological view that floating currencies are a good thing and that mercantilism is
fundamentally unfair as well as misconceived in that it suppresses the purchasing power of Asian consumers.

--- Page 224 ---
Page 224 of 265 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
If this is all true, it also ignores the point that America needs to be careful what it wishes for. The Bush
administration can only "go for growth" on the assumption that Asia will keep financing its current-account
deficit because it has no choice if the region's exporters want to maintain access to America's domestic
markets. This is all very well. But America can only run such a strategy because the dollar holds the
privileged position of being the world's reserve currency. The dollar paper standard, which has been in place
since Richard Nixon broke the last link with gold in 1971, has meant that America has the luxury of running
its own independent monetary policy. That happy situation can continue only for so long as the dollar
decline remains gradual and does not turn into a rout.
Another consequence of Asian currency revaluation is that it would increase the inflationary pressure in
America, or at least reduce the deflationary bias stemming from the boom in importing cheap Asian goods.
American imports from Asia ex-Japan rose by 20% year-on-year in the first nine months of this year. This
would raise the risk of an inflation scare. Ironically, this would be ultimately bullish for government bonds in
America since any inflation scare in America means significantly higher interest rates, which would likely
precipitate a deflationary deleveraging cycle among America's highly indebted consumers -- for the
consumer-finance driven American economy would prove highly sensitive to higher short-term interest rates.
There are, then, risks, from the narrow U.S. perspective, of pushing China and other Asian governments to
revalue since the present "game of chicken" suits the U.S. even if this means increasingly scary macro
imbalances which, if they are allowed to continue to grow, will ultimately trigger the demise of the dollar paper
stand to the benefit of owners of gold bullion. But from the point of view of the whole world, Asian currency
revaluation is required to bring about a more balanced world economy by boosting the dollar purchasing
power of Asia's cashed-up consumers. Bring on the Plaza 2 Accord.
---
Mr. Wood, equity strategist for CLSA Asia-Pacific Markets in Hong Kong, is the author of "The Bubble
Economy" (Atlantic Monthly Press, 1992).
(See related letter: "Letters to the Editor: What Supply-Side Really Is . . . and Isn't" -- WSJ Dec. 1, 2004)
