Dollar Anxiety
REVIEW &OUTLOOK (Editorial)
Dollar Anxiety
845 words
11 November 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
A16
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Sorry to interrupt the current White House euphoria, but someone ought to mention that the great American
middle class didn't re-elect President Bush so he could debase the currency.
We offer this heretical thought amid the dollar's recent fall, as well as the conventional Beltway and Wall
Street wisdom that this decline must continue in order to reverse the awful, terrible, frightening -- pick your
adjective -- U.S. current-account deficit. The press is reporting that even the Bush Treasury now supports this
view, privately cheering the greenback's fall even as it lip-syncs support for a "strong dollar."
If anyone inside the Bush Administration is in fact peddling this line, we hope the President fires him. More
than one White House term has been damaged by currency crises (Nixon, Carter, Reagan II). And it's simply
impossible to run a muscular foreign policy if the world starts to lose confidence in your currency. If
devaluation were the path to prosperity, Argentina would rule the world.
In any case, the idea that the current-account deficit is the cause of dollar weakness is as mistaken now as
ever. Currency values are determined by monetary policies set by a cartel of central banks. The dollar's
weakness is largely a function of the fact that the Federal Reserve has for some time been running a looser
monetary policy than either the European or Japanese central banks.
Even with yesterday's quarter-point increase in the fed funds rate to 2%, the Fed seems to be behind the
curve of inflationary expectations. Gold is above $430 an ounce, with the price of gold in dollars increasing at
a faster rate during the last year than the gold price in either euros or yen. As an historic hedge against
inflation, gold has a track record of anticipating future price swings. Other commodity prices, especially oil,
are near their recent highs.
Economist Ed Hyman noted the other day that dollar declines on the order of 20% have usually been
manageable. But steeper declines than that have been associated with currency crises that can trigger
stock-market crashes. So far the greenback's current decline is close to 20% from its most recent peak. The
way to avoid a dollar crisis is either for the Fed to print fewer dollars, or the Japanese and European central
banks to print more yen or euros, or all of the above.
At the present moment, the Fed looks as if it has more of the work to do. The U.S. economy is growing
smartly, capital spending is up and job growth has resumed. Notwithstanding yesterday's rosy Fed statement
about the equally balanced risks between growth and price stability, the weak dollar and other price signals
tell us that the greater dangers are on the inflation side.
Meanwhile, it wouldn't hurt if everyone relaxed about the trade deficit, since attempts to "correct" it will do
more harm than the deficit itself. By accounting definition, the trade deficit is the reverse of the U.S. capital
surplus. This means that the U.S. is continuing to attract investment and goods, both of which are signals of a
healthy expansion that continues at a faster rate than most of the rest of the world.
Germany may be able to exult in its trade surplus, but its domestic growth remains anemic and its jobless rate
high. Instead of hoping for a weaker dollar, U.S. officials would be better off coaxing Europe and Japan to
shake off their socialist and regulatory shackles and grow faster. It's true the U.S. can't run a trade deficit of
$600 billion a year forever, but it can run it for a long time as long as its economic policies make it a
destination for global capital. And any decline in that deficit is best accomplished not by U.S. contraction or
devaluation but with better growth elsewhere.
Which gets us to the issue of Mr. Bush's second-term economic team. Our sense is that it's no longer
possible for Karl Rove to run economic policy out of his hip pocket. With interest rates expected to rise in the
next couple of years, one thing we know for sure is that there will be financial casualties at home and abroad.
Some companies and countries always make bad bets, and their troubles will need a Treasury with enough
financial skill and experience to manage the fallout.

--- Page 260 ---
Page 260 of 265 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Whether John Snow stays or goes as Secretary, the Treasury Department needs more financial help in its
other senior ranks. The Treasury does need a leader to sell tax cuts and Social Security reform. But the
legislative agenda won't go anywhere if a financial or dollar crisis erupts first.
---

EUROPE
Terror-Network Link Is Probed in Dutch Killing
BERLIN -- Police have expanded the investigation of a Dutch filmmaker's murder to include a group of
Islamists based in the Netherlands and Germany who may have penetrated the Dutch intelligence services,
intelligence and law-enforcement officials said yesterday.
The broadened probe is examining possible links between the chief suspect and a network that may be
involved in terror plots in Europe and beyond. Theo van Gogh's killer left behind a note condemning the
victim's recent film criticizing Islam's treatment of women.
A violent backlash since the murder intensified yesterday as three Dutch antiterror police officers were injured
by a grenade during a raid in The Hague. Two suspects surrendered after a 15-hour standoff. The raid came
after several days of violence against religious property in Holland, with arson attacks on mosques and
churches. Six suspects, believed to be members of a radical Islamic terrorist group, are in police custody in
connection with Mr. van Gogh's murder, including the alleged killer, 26-year-old Mohammed Bouyeri, who
holds dual Dutch and Moroccan citizenship.
The new course of the investigation has focused on a suspected five-man cell that allegedly planned bomb
attacks in the Netherlands in late 2003, according to a spokesman from the Dutch national prosecutor's office.
According to German police officials, they last year lost track of an alleged member of this cell, whom Dutch
officials identified as Redaoun al-Issar, a 43-year-old Syrian; he is a close confidant of Mr. Bouyeri, according
to officials from both countries.
According to a German police official, Mr. Issar applied for political asylum in Frankfurt in 1995, but was
rejected. He went to Holland, but overstayed his visa and was deported back to Germany in October 2003,
then went underground. Some German officials, however, have contested this version of events.
According to Dutch officials, Mr. Issar is believed to have controlled a double agent within the Dutch secret
service. Vincent van Steen, spokesman for the Dutch intelligence agency, says one of its officers was
arrested in September after a secret report was discovered in a suspected Islamist safe house. A spokesman
for the Dutch prosecutor says the building was being searched for bomb-making materials when the report
was found.
Spanish police also are investigating links between Mr. van Gogh's murder and a Moroccan suspect jailed in
Spain in connection with last year's bombing in Casablanca, Morocco, officials told the Associated Press in
Madrid.
-- David Crawford
Russia Rejects Ban on Drinking Beer in Public
MOSCOW -- The upper house of the Russian parliament rejected a bill that would have banned drinking beer
in public, the Interfax news agency reported.
The bill, already approved by the lower house, the State Duma, had widely been expected to pass. Both the
Duma and the upper house, the Federation Council, are dominated by the pro-Kremlin United Russia Party.
However, the Federation Council voted down the measure 40-73.

--- Page 264 ---
Page 264 of 265 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Public consumption of beer is ubiquitous in Russia, where pedestrians frequently stroll with a bottle in hand.
Drinking vodka and hard alcohol in public is illegal, and officials in the Duma had said the new legislation was
appropriate given recent terrorist attacks and heightened security measures.
Brewers, however, called the measure a witch hunt and blamed the country's powerful vodka industry, which
has seen its market share decline as beer quality and availability improves.
A bill banning beer commercials between 7 a.m. and 10 p.m. went into effect in September.
-- Associated Press

ASIA/PACIFIC
Newmont Mines in Indonesia Face More Scrutiny
JAKARTA, Indonesia -- A new report about alleged pollution at Newmont Mining Corp.'s gold mine on the
Indonesian island of Sulawesi has raised fresh questions about the facility as well as concerns that a much
larger Indonesian mine owned by Newmont could become the next target for environmental groups.
The hotly contested study, leaked yesterday by environmental groups who participated in an analysis led by
Indonesia's Environment Ministry, claimed to have found evidence of pollution in a bay where PT Newmont
Minahasa Raya dumps its treated waste rock.
The report was based on the same data released last month, but the earlier report didn't include the
environmental groups' analysis. The new report argues that fish in the bay are unsafe to eat in large
quantities and urges the government to consider relocating some people who live nearby. It also says the
government should prosecute Newmont executives for allowing the alleged pollution to occur.
Denver-based Newmont, the world's largest gold-mining company, strongly disputes those findings. It insists
the mine complies with all of Indonesia's environmental regulations and argues that the latest report was
written largely by environmental groups that have opposed it in the past.
The report's findings pose a challenge for Indonesia's new government, which is trying to entice foreign
investors to return after years of being scared by its turbulent politics and economics. But sustained attacks
on Newmont -- one of the country's biggest foreign investors, having poured about $2.4 billion into several
projects there -- are unlikely to further that goal.
Five Newmont executives were recently held for one month without charge by police investigating the
pollution allegations. They were released from custody in October, but remain unable to leave northern
Sulawesi.
-- Timothy Mapes and Rin Hindryati
---
THE AMERICAS
Canadian Exports Drop Following Rate Increase
TORONTO -- A fall in Canadian exports in September has some economists sounding alarms about the
stronger Canadian dollar's impact and wondering if the Bank of Canada's monetary-tightening agenda could
be derailed by a deteriorating trade picture.
The trade surplus was 5.06 billion Canadian dollars (US$4.24 billion) in September, down from C$6.44 billion
in August. The August total was revised lower from a preliminary C$7.42 billion. Economists surveyed by
Thomson IFR Bonddata projected September's surplus to come in at C$6.8 billion.
Imports rose 0.4% to C$31.03 billion, driven by gains in forestry, industrial and consumer goods. Exports fell
3.4% to C$36.09 billion with declines in all categories. The exports drop was the sharpest since August 2003.
The marked fall may be the first clear signal the stronger currency is starting to damage exports. The Bank of
Canada cited stronger-than-expected exports when it launched its interest-rate-tightening campaign in
September. It said strong export growth was helping close the gap between actual and potential output in the
economy, requiring the removal of monetary stimulus to forestall inflationary pressures.

--- Page 265 ---
Page 265 of 265 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
On Sunday, Bank of Canada Governor David Dodge said the central bank will continue to carefully assess
the implications of the rising local dollar, though he also said exports have yet to feel much of a pinch from
the stronger currency.
-- Dow Jones Newswires
Briefly:
QUITO, Ecuador -- Ecuadorean opposition parties abandoned their attempt to impeach President Lucio
Gutierrez after failing to get enough votes in Congress to begin investigating claims of corruption. Foreign
investors have worried that political strife during impeachment proceedings could have interfered with
Ecuador's progress toward an agreement with the International Monetary Fund and endangered bills to open
up industries to foreign investment.
